%% S1 — Philosophy & Fundamentals
%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\secdivider{Section S1}{Philosophy \& Fundamentals of OOP}{cA}{cA}
\markboth{S1 --- Philosophy \& Fundamentals of OOP}{}
\label{sec:S1}
\titleformat{\section}{\large\bfseries\color{cA}}{}{0em}{}[\titlerule]
\section{S1 --- Philosophy \& Fundamentals of OOP}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Explain the key features of object-oriented programming (OOP). How does a class differ from a structure in OOP? \mk{6+4}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2023--24}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Key Features of Object-Oriented Programming:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Encapsulation:} Bundling data (attributes) and the
    functions that operate on that data into a single unit called a
    \emph{class}, and restricting direct access to some of the
    object's components via access specifiers (\texttt{private},
    \texttt{protected}, \texttt{public}).

  \item \textbf{Abstraction:} Hiding implementation details and
    exposing only the necessary interface to the user.  The user
    interacts with an object through its public methods without
    knowing the internal workings.

  \item \textbf{Inheritance:} A mechanism by which a new class
    (\emph{derived class}) acquires the properties and behaviours of
    an existing class (\emph{base class}), promoting code reuse.

  \item \textbf{Polymorphism:} The ability of a single interface to
    represent different underlying forms --- achieved through function
    overloading (compile-time) and virtual functions (run-time).

  \item \textbf{Message Passing:} Objects communicate by calling each
    other's methods, analogous to sending messages.
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textbf{Class vs.\ Structure in OOP (C++):}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Class} & \textbf{Structure (\texttt{struct})} \\
\midrule
Members are \texttt{private} by default. &
Members are \texttt{public} by default. \\[3pt]
Supports all OOP features: inheritance,
encapsulation, polymorphism, access control. &
In C++ also supports OOP features, but by
convention used for plain data aggregates. \\[3pt]
Typically used to model objects with both
data and behaviour. &
Typically used for lightweight, passive data
containers (no or minimal methods). \\[3pt]
Inheritance default: \texttt{private}. &
Inheritance default: \texttt{public}. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Explain the importance of using constructors and destructors in C++. Why and how does Java drop writing destructors in programs? \mk{5+5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2023--24}

%% ── Answer: Constructors/destructors; Java GC ───────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Importance of Constructors in C++:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Guarantee that every object starts in a \emph{valid,
    well-defined state} --- no uninitialized memory or dangling
    pointers.
  \item Can be overloaded to support multiple ways of creating an
    object (default, parameterised, copy constructors).
  \item Enable the \emph{RAII} (Resource Acquisition Is
    Initialisation) idiom: resources such as file handles or heap
    memory are acquired in the constructor.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Importance of Destructors in C++:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Automatically release resources when an object's lifetime
    ends, preventing \emph{memory leaks} and \emph{resource leaks}.
  \item Essential for classes that manage raw heap memory
    (\texttt{new}/\texttt{delete}), file I/O, network sockets, etc.
  \item Complete the RAII contract: what the constructor acquires,
    the destructor releases.
\end{itemize}

\medskip
\textbf{Why Java drops destructors:}

Java uses \textbf{automatic garbage collection (GC)}.  The JVM
tracks all object references and automatically reclaims heap memory
when an object is no longer reachable --- the programmer never calls
\texttt{delete}.  Since memory management is automated, an explicit
destructor is unnecessary.

Instead, Java provides:
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \textbf{\texttt{finalize()} (deprecated):} Called by the GC
    before reclaiming an object, but its timing is non-deterministic
    and unreliable.
  \item \textbf{\texttt{try-with-resources} / \texttt{AutoCloseable}:}
    The recommended modern approach for deterministic release of
    non-memory resources (files, connections).
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Describe why constructor overloading is possible while destructor overloading is not allowed. \mk{11}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23}

%% ── Answer: Constructor overloading vs destructor overloading ────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Constructor Overloading is possible} because:
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item An object can be created in multiple ways --- from no
    arguments, from specific values, from a copy of another object,
    etc.
  \item Each overloaded constructor has a \textbf{different parameter
    list}, so the compiler can unambiguously select the correct one
    based on how the object is created.
  \item This follows the same rules as regular function overloading.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Point {
public:
    Point() : x(0), y(0) {}              // default
    Point(int a, int b) : x(a), y(b) {}  // parameterised
    Point(const Point& p) : x(p.x), y(p.y) {} // copy
private:
    int x, y;
};
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Destructor Overloading is NOT possible} because:

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item A destructor \textbf{takes no parameters} --- it is always
    called with \emph{no arguments} by the compiler when the object's
    lifetime ends.  Without parameters, there is no basis for
    distinguishing multiple versions.

  \item A destructor \textbf{returns nothing} --- there is no return
    type to differentiate on either.

  \item There is \textbf{only one way} an object's lifetime can
    end --- the compiler must know exactly which destructor to call
    without any ambiguity.  Multiple destructors would make this
    impossible.

  \item A class can have \textbf{at most one destructor}, named
    \texttt{\textasciitilde ClassName()}, with no parameters.
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Discuss the difference between a class and an object. Describe how C++ supports inheritance. \mk{11}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22}

%% ── Answer: Class vs object; inheritance in C++ ──────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Class vs.\ Object:}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Class} & \textbf{Object} \\
\midrule
A \emph{blueprint} or \emph{template} that defines the structure and
behaviour of a type. &
A concrete \emph{instance} of a class that occupies memory. \\[3pt]
Defined once; no memory allocated for the class itself. &
Each object has its own copy of the data members. \\[3pt]
Exists at compile time as a type definition. &
Created at run time via constructors. \\[3pt]
Example: \texttt{class Car \{ \ldots \};} &
Example: \texttt{Car myCar;} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\medskip
\textbf{How C++ supports Inheritance:}

C++ supports inheritance using the colon (\texttt{:}) syntax in the
class declaration, with an access specifier controlling how inherited
members are exposed:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Animal {          // Base class
protected:
    string name;
public:
    Animal(string n) : name(n) {}
    virtual void speak() { cout << name << " makes a sound\n"; }
};

class Dog : public Animal {   // Derived class
public:
    Dog(string n) : Animal(n) {}
    void speak() override { cout << name << " barks\n"; }
};
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Types of inheritance in C++:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=1pt]
  \item \texttt{public} inheritance: \texttt{public}/\texttt{protected}
    members remain \texttt{public}/\texttt{protected} in derived.
  \item \texttt{protected} inheritance: both become \texttt{protected}.
  \item \texttt{private} inheritance: both become \texttt{private}.
  \item C++ also uniquely supports \textbf{multiple inheritance}:
    \texttt{class C : public A, public B \{ \};}
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Explain abstraction with an appropriate example. Write down the properties of abstract class and abstract method. \mk{5+3+2}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2020--21}

%% ── Answer: Abstraction; abstract class & method ────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Abstraction} is the OOP principle of hiding internal
implementation details and exposing only the essential features of an
object to the outside world.

\textbf{Example:} A \texttt{Car} class exposes methods
\texttt{start()}, \texttt{accelerate()}, and \texttt{brake()}.  The
driver does not need to know about fuel injection, engine timing, or
brake hydraulics --- those details are hidden.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Car {
    void fuelInjection() { /* hidden */ }   // private detail
public:
    void start()      { fuelInjection(); /* ... */ }
    void accelerate() { /* ... */ }
    void brake()      { /* ... */ }
};
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Properties of an Abstract Class:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Contains at least one \emph{pure virtual function}
    (\texttt{= 0} in C++; declared \texttt{abstract} in Java).
  \item \textbf{Cannot be instantiated} --- no objects can be created
    directly from an abstract class.
  \item Can have constructors, data members, and concrete (non-pure)
    methods.
  \item A derived class must override \emph{all} pure virtual
    functions; otherwise the derived class is also abstract.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Properties of an Abstract Method (Pure Virtual Function):}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Has a declaration but \textbf{no definition} in the base
    class (e.g.\ \texttt{virtual void draw() = 0;}).
  \item Forces every concrete subclass to provide its own
    implementation.
  \item Provides a common interface across a class hierarchy.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is the purpose of constructors and destructors? Write three special properties of a constructor that make it distinct from other member functions. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17, 2020--21}

%% ── Answer: Constructors and destructors ────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Purpose of Constructors:}
A constructor is a special member function that is automatically
called when an object is created.  Its purpose is to
\emph{initialise} the object's data members to valid starting
values, allocate resources (e.g.\ heap memory), and set up any
invariants the object must maintain.

\textbf{Purpose of Destructors:}
A destructor is automatically called when an object goes out of scope
or is explicitly deleted.  Its purpose is to \emph{release} resources
held by the object (e.g.\ \texttt{delete} heap memory, close file
handles, release locks) to prevent resource leaks.

\medskip
\textbf{Three special properties of a constructor that distinguish it
from other member functions:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Same name as the class:} A constructor must have
    exactly the same name as the class it belongs to.  No other
    member function may share the class name.

  \item \textbf{No return type (not even \texttt{void}):} A
    constructor does not declare or return any value.  Specifying a
    return type is a compile error.

  \item \textbf{Called automatically:} A constructor is invoked
    implicitly by the compiler whenever an object of the class is
    created --- the programmer never calls it explicitly by name.
    (Contrast with ordinary member functions, which must be called
    explicitly.)
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Differentiate between early binding and late binding with appropriate examples. \mk{8}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2014--15, 2013--14; CSE 107 | 2020--21}
\variant{CSE 201 | 2015--16, 2012--13, 2009--10, 2008--09; CSE 107 | 2017--18}

%% ── Answer: Early binding vs late binding ────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Early Binding (Static / Compile-Time Binding):}

The compiler resolves the function call and links it to the correct
function definition \emph{at compile time}, based on the declared
type of the object or pointer.  This produces faster code since no
run-time lookup is needed.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Animal {
public:
    void speak() { cout << "Animal speaks\n"; } // non-virtual
};
class Dog : public Animal {
public:
    void speak() { cout << "Dog barks\n"; }
};

Animal* p = new Dog();
p->speak();   // Early binding: calls Animal::speak()
              // because speak() is NOT virtual
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Late Binding (Dynamic / Run-Time Binding):}

The function call is resolved \emph{at run time} based on the actual
type of the object pointed to.  Achieved in C++ via \texttt{virtual}
functions and the virtual function table (vtable).

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Animal {
public:
    virtual void speak() { cout << "Animal speaks\n"; } // virtual
};
class Dog : public Animal {
public:
    void speak() override { cout << "Dog barks\n"; }
};

Animal* p = new Dog();
p->speak();   // Late binding: calls Dog::speak() at run time
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
& \textbf{Early Binding} & \textbf{Late Binding} \\ \midrule
Resolution & Compile time & Run time \\
Mechanism  & Direct call  & vtable lookup \\
Speed      & Faster       & Slightly slower \\
Flexibility & Less         & More (polymorphism) \\
Keyword    & (default)    & \texttt{virtual} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Which feature of C++ programming language do you like most and why? The feature that you mention should not also be a feature of C programming language. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}

%% ── Answer: Favourite C++ feature not in C ──────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Feature: Operator Overloading}

Operator overloading allows existing C++ operators
(\texttt{+}, \texttt{-}, \texttt{==}, \texttt{<<}, etc.) to be
redefined for user-defined types, making code significantly more
intuitive and readable.  This feature does not exist in C.

\textbf{Why it is the most useful:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item It allows user-defined types to \emph{behave like built-in
    types}.  For example, adding two \texttt{Complex} numbers can be
    written as \texttt{c1 + c2} rather than \texttt{add(c1, c2)}.
  \item It makes mathematical classes (\texttt{Matrix},
    \texttt{Vector}, \texttt{Fraction}) self-documenting and natural
    to use.
  \item It integrates seamlessly with the standard library
    (e.g.\ overloading \texttt{<<} for \texttt{ostream} to print
    custom objects).
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Example:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Complex {
    double re, im;
public:
    Complex(double r, double i) : re(r), im(i) {}
    Complex operator+(const Complex& o) const {
        return Complex(re + o.re, im + o.im);
    }
    friend ostream& operator<<(ostream& os, const Complex& c) {
        return os << c.re << "+" << c.im << "i";
    }
};
// Usage:
Complex c1(1,2), c2(3,4);
cout << c1 + c2;   // prints: 4+6i
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a short note on ``function overloading''. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}

%% ── Answer: Function overloading ────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Function Overloading} is the ability to define multiple
functions with the \emph{same name} in the same scope, provided they
differ in their \textbf{parameter list} (number, type, or order of
parameters).  The compiler selects the correct version at
\emph{compile time} based on the arguments supplied --- this is a
form of \emph{compile-time (static) polymorphism}.

\textbf{Rules:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Overloaded functions must differ in their parameter lists.
  \item The return type \emph{alone} is \textbf{not} sufficient to
    distinguish overloads.
  \item \texttt{const}-ness of the implicit \texttt{this} pointer
    (for member functions) can also distinguish overloads.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Example:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int    add(int a, int b)       { return a + b; }
double add(double a, double b) { return a + b; }
int    add(int a, int b, int c){ return a + b + c; }

int    r1 = add(1, 2);        // calls first version
double r2 = add(1.5, 2.5);    // calls second version
int    r3 = add(1, 2, 3);     // calls third version
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Advantages:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Improves code readability --- one intuitive name for
    logically related operations.
  \item Eliminates the need for functions like \texttt{addInt},
    \texttt{addDouble}, etc.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What does a default constructor (generated by the compiler) perform? What are the scenarios when a copy constructor is called? \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}

%% ── Answer: Default constructor; copy constructor scenarios ──────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{What the compiler-generated default constructor does:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item For \textbf{built-in types} (int, double, pointer, etc.):
    leaves them \textbf{uninitialised} (their values are
    indeterminate).
  \item For \textbf{class-type members}: calls the default
    constructor of each member.
  \item For \textbf{base class sub-objects}: calls the base class
    default constructor.
\end{itemize}
The compiler generates this constructor only if the programmer
defines \emph{no} constructor at all.

\medskip
\textbf{Scenarios when the copy constructor is called:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Object initialisation from another object of the
    same class:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
MyClass a;
MyClass b = a;   // copy constructor called
MyClass c(a);    // copy constructor called
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Passing an object by value to a function:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void f(MyClass x) { }   // copy constructor called for x
f(a);
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Returning an object by value from a function:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
MyClass g() {
    MyClass tmp;
    return tmp;   // copy constructor may be called (if RVO absent)
}
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Throwing or catching an exception by value} (the
    exception object is copy-constructed).
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What are the syntactic differences between C and C++ programming? \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2014--15}

%% ── Answer: Syntactic differences C vs C++ ──────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{C} & \textbf{C++} \\
\midrule
Input/output via \texttt{printf}/\texttt{scanf}. &
Uses \texttt{cout}/\texttt{cin} with \texttt{<<}/\texttt{>>}. \\[3pt]
No references; only pointers. &
Supports reference variables (\texttt{int\& r = x;}). \\[3pt]
\texttt{struct} cannot contain functions. &
\texttt{struct} and \texttt{class} can contain member functions. \\[3pt]
No function overloading. &
Function and operator overloading supported. \\[3pt]
No default arguments. &
Default arguments in function declarations supported. \\[3pt]
\texttt{void*} can be implicitly converted to any pointer type. &
\texttt{void*} requires explicit cast. \\[3pt]
No \texttt{new}/\texttt{delete}; uses \texttt{malloc}/\texttt{free}. &
\texttt{new}/\texttt{delete} for type-safe dynamic allocation. \\[3pt]
No \texttt{bool} type (uses \texttt{int} for boolean). &
Native \texttt{bool} type with \texttt{true}/\texttt{false}. \\[3pt]
No \texttt{inline} keyword with class support. &
\texttt{inline} functions; functions defined inside class are automatically inline. \\[3pt]
No \texttt{namespace}. &
\texttt{namespace} supported (\texttt{using namespace std;}). \\[3pt]
No templates. &
Function and class templates supported. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Define automatic in-line function. What are the advantages of using in-line function over parameterized macros? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% ── Answer: Inline functions vs macros ───────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Automatic Inline Function:}
An \emph{inline function} is a function prefixed with the
\texttt{inline} keyword (or defined inside a class body) that
\emph{suggests} to the compiler to replace each function call with
the actual function body at the call site, eliminating function-call
overhead (stack frame setup, jump, return).

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
inline int square(int x) { return x * x; }
// The compiler may replace: int r = square(5);
// with:                     int r = 5 * 5;
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Advantages over parameterised macros (\texttt{\#define}):}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Type safety:} Inline functions undergo full type
    checking by the compiler.  Macros are textual substitutions with
    no type checking, leading to subtle bugs.
    \begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#define SQ(x) ((x)*(x))   // SQ(1.5+0.5) -> (1.5+0.5)*(1.5+0.5)=4 OK
                           // but SQ(++a)  -> (++a)*(++a) -- a incremented TWICE
inline int sq(int x) { return x*x; } // ++a evaluated once, passed as value
    \end{lstlisting}
  \item \textbf{No multiple-evaluation side effects:} Arguments are
    evaluated exactly once; macros can evaluate arguments multiple
    times.
  \item \textbf{Scope and namespacing:} Inline functions respect
    C++ scoping rules; macros are global text substitutions with no
    scope.
  \item \textbf{Debuggable:} Inline functions appear in debugger
    symbol tables; macros do not.
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is an in-line function? What are its advantages and disadvantages? Why might the following function not be in-lined by your compiler?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void f1() { for (int i = 0; i < 10; i++) cout << i; }
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{8}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% ── Answer: Inline function details ─────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Inline Function:} A function declared with the \texttt{inline}
keyword that \emph{requests} the compiler to insert its body directly
at every call site instead of generating a regular function call.

\textbf{Advantages:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Eliminates function-call overhead (no stack frame, no jump).
  \item Can enable further optimisations (constant folding, etc.).
  \item Full type checking, unlike macros.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Disadvantages:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \textbf{Code bloat:} If a large function is inlined at many
    call sites, the binary size grows.
  \item Increased compilation time.
  \item The \texttt{inline} keyword is only a \emph{hint}; the
    compiler may ignore it.
\end{itemize}

\medskip
\textbf{Why \texttt{f1()} might not be inlined:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void f1() { for (int i = 0; i < 10; i++) cout << i; }
\end{lstlisting}

Most compilers refuse to inline functions that contain
\textbf{loops}, because:
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Inlining a loop body at every call site causes significant
    \emph{code bloat}.
  \item The performance benefit of inlining is minimal when the
    function body itself is non-trivial (the loop iterations dominate
    the cost, not the call overhead).
  \item Many compilers have heuristics that exclude functions with
    loops, recursion, or large bodies from automatic inlining.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Differentiate between real-time polymorphism and run-time polymorphism with appropriate examples. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}

\item What is compile-time polymorphism and run-time polymorphism? Differentiate with examples. \mk{13}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}
\variant{CSE 201 | 2010--11; CSE 107 | 2019--20}

%% ── Answer: Compile-time vs run-time polymorphism ───────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Compile-Time Polymorphism (Static Polymorphism):}

Resolved by the compiler before the program runs.  Achieved through:
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=1pt]
  \item \textbf{Function overloading}
  \item \textbf{Operator overloading}
  \item \textbf{Template instantiation}
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
// Function overloading -- resolved at compile time
int    area(int s)           { return s * s; }
double area(double r)        { return 3.14159 * r * r; }
int    area(int l, int w)    { return l * w; }

area(5);        // compiler picks first version
area(3.0);      // compiler picks second version
area(4, 6);     // compiler picks third version
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Run-Time Polymorphism (Dynamic Polymorphism):}

Resolved while the program is running, based on the actual type of
the object.  Achieved through \texttt{virtual} functions and base
class pointers/references.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Shape {
public:
    virtual double area() const = 0;  // pure virtual
};
class Circle : public Shape {
    double r;
public:
    Circle(double r) : r(r) {}
    double area() const override { return 3.14159 * r * r; }
};
class Rectangle : public Shape {
    double l, w;
public:
    Rectangle(double l, double w) : l(l), w(w) {}
    double area() const override { return l * w; }
};

Shape* s1 = new Circle(3.0);
Shape* s2 = new Rectangle(4.0, 6.0);
cout << s1->area();   // calls Circle::area() at run time
cout << s2->area();   // calls Rectangle::area() at run time
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is Object-Oriented programming? How are data and functions organized in an Object-Oriented program? \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% ── Answer: What is OOP; data and functions ─────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Object-Oriented Programming (OOP)} is a programming paradigm
that organises software design around \emph{objects} rather than
functions and logic.  An object is an instance of a \emph{class} that
contains both data (state) and the functions (behaviour) that operate
on that data.

\medskip
\textbf{Organisation of data and functions in OOP:}

\begin{itemize}[itemsep=3pt]
  \item \textbf{Data} (member variables / attributes) and
    \textbf{functions} (member functions / methods) are grouped
    together inside a \textbf{class}.  This grouping is called
    \emph{encapsulation}.

  \item Access to data is controlled by \emph{access specifiers}:
    \begin{itemize}
      \item \texttt{private} --- accessible only within the class.
      \item \texttt{protected} --- accessible within the class and
        its derived classes.
      \item \texttt{public} --- accessible from anywhere.
    \end{itemize}

  \item Data is typically kept \texttt{private} (hidden), while
    functions are \texttt{public} (interface).  External code
    interacts with an object only through its public methods.

  \item Each \emph{object} (instance) has its own copy of the data
    members, but all objects of the same class share the same method
    definitions.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}


\end{enumerate}
\newpage

%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
%% S2 — Classes, Objects & Access Specifiers
%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\secdivider{Section S2}{Classes, Objects \& Access Specifiers}{cB}{cB}
\markboth{S2 --- Classes, Objects \& Access Specifiers}{}
\label{sec:S2}
\titleformat{\section}{\large\bfseries\color{cB}}{}{0em}{}[\titlerule]
\section{S2 --- Classes, Objects \& Access Specifiers}

\begin{enumerate}
\item A \texttt{Student} class has a private variable to store an ID, a constructor without parameters to initialize the ID, and a private static variable. Write the class definition for \texttt{Student} and write code in \texttt{main()} to create an array of 120 \texttt{Student} objects using the \texttt{new} operator, where the IDs are initialized as 1, 2, \ldots, 120 respectively in the constructor. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | Jan 2021}

%% ── Answer: Student class with static ID ────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Student {
    int id;
    static int nextId;   // private static: shared ID counter
public:
    Student() { id = nextId++; }   // each object gets next ID
    int getId() const { return id; }
};

// Initialise static member outside the class
int Student::nextId = 1;

int main() {
    // Create array of 120 Student objects on the heap
    Student* students = new Student[120];

    // Verify first and last IDs
    cout << "First ID: " << students[0].getId()   << endl; // 1
    cout << "Last ID:  " << students[119].getId()  << endl; // 120

    delete[] students;  // release heap memory
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{How it works:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \texttt{nextId} is \texttt{static} and initialised to
    \texttt{1} outside the class.
  \item Each time a \texttt{Student} object is constructed (including
    array element construction), the default constructor assigns
    \texttt{id = nextId} and post-increments \texttt{nextId}.
  \item After \texttt{new Student[120]}, the 120 objects have IDs
    1 through 120 in order.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Imagine a tollbooth at a bridge. Cars passing by the booth are expected to pay a 50-cent toll. Mostly they do, but sometimes a car goes by without paying. The tollbooth keeps track of the number of cars that have gone by, and of the total amount of money collected.

Model this tollbooth with a class called \texttt{tollbooth}. The three data items are a list of string to hold the registration numbers of the cars passing through the bridge, a type unsigned int to hold the total number of cars, and a type double to hold the total amount of money collected. A constructor initializes both of these to 0. A member function called \texttt{payingCar()} adds the car registration number in the list, increments the car total and adds 0.50 to the cash total. Another function, called \texttt{nopayCar()}, adds the car registration number, increments the car total but adds nothing to the cash total. Finally, a member function called \texttt{display()} displays the list of cars' registration numbers and the two totals. Make appropriate member functions const. Use proper naming convention and setter-getter functions. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2020--21}

%% ── Answer: Tollbooth class ──────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <list>
#include <string>
using namespace std;

class tollbooth {
private:
    list<string> registrations;  // list of strings for registration numbers
    unsigned int totalCars;      // total number of cars
    double totalCash;            // total amount of money collected

public:
    // Constructor initializes totals to 0
    tollbooth() {
        totalCars = 0;
        totalCash = 0.0;
    }

    // Setter and Getter functions
    void setRegistrations(const list<string>& regs) { registrations = regs; }
    list<string> getRegistrations() const { return registrations; }

    void setTotalCars(unsigned int cars) { totalCars = cars; }
    unsigned int getTotalCars() const { return totalCars; }

    void setTotalCash(double cash) { totalCash = cash; }
    double getTotalCash() const { return totalCash; }

    // Car pays the 50-cent toll
    void payingCar(const string& reg) {
        registrations.push_back(reg);
        totalCars++;
        totalCash += 0.50;
    }

    // Car passes without paying
    void nopayCar(const string& reg) {
        registrations.push_back(reg);
        totalCars++;
    }

    // Display summary -- does not modify state, so const
    void display() const {
        cout << "Total cars: " << totalCars << endl;
        cout << "Total cash: $" << totalCash << endl;
        cout << "Cars' Registration Numbers:" << endl;
        for (const string& r : registrations) {
            cout << "  " << r << endl;
        }
    }
};

int main() {
    tollbooth booth;
    
    booth.payingCar("ABC-111");
    booth.payingCar("XYZ-222");
    booth.nopayCar("BAD-999");
    
    booth.display();
    
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Const correctness:} Getter methods and \texttt{display()} are declared \texttt{const} because they only read data without modifying the object's internal state.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is the difference between class and structure? Why are both of them necessary in C++ programming? \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2015--16}

%% ── Answer: Class vs structure in C++ ───────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Class} & \textbf{Structure} \\
\midrule
Members are \textbf{private} by default. &
Members are \textbf{public} by default. \\[3pt]
Inheritance default: \textbf{private}. &
Inheritance default: \textbf{public}. \\[3pt]
Conventionally used for objects with both
data and behaviour (full OOP design). &
Conventionally used for plain data records
with little or no behaviour. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Why both are necessary:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \textbf{Backward compatibility with C:} C code using
    \texttt{struct} compiles unchanged in C++.  Removing
    \texttt{struct} would break C compatibility.
  \item \textbf{Semantic clarity:} Using \texttt{struct} signals to
    readers that a type is a simple data aggregate (e.g.\ a
    \texttt{Point}, a \texttt{Color}), while \texttt{class} signals
    a fully encapsulated object.  This is a \emph{convention} that
    improves code readability.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is a static member? Why are they used? Write down the rules of using static variable and static method in a class. \mk{7}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2015--16}
\variant{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% ── Answer: Static member variables and methods ─────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Static Member:} A class member declared with the
\texttt{static} keyword.  It belongs to the \emph{class itself}
rather than to any particular object --- all objects of the class
share a single copy of it.

\textbf{Why used:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item To maintain data that is \emph{shared} across all instances
    (e.g.\ object count, configuration).
  \item To provide utility functions that do not require an object.
\end{itemize}

\medskip
\textbf{Rules for static variables:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Declared inside the class with \texttt{static}; must be
    \textbf{defined} (and optionally initialised) \emph{outside} the
    class in exactly one translation unit.
  \item Only one copy exists regardless of how many objects are created.
  \item Accessible as \texttt{ClassName::variable} without an object.
  \item Lifetime is the entire program duration.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Rules for static methods:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Can access only \emph{static} members (no access to
    \texttt{this} pointer or non-static members).
  \item Can be called without creating an object:
    \texttt{ClassName::method()}.
  \item Cannot be \texttt{virtual} or \texttt{const}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Counter {
    static int count;       // declaration
public:
    Counter()  { count++; }
    ~Counter() { count--; }
    static int getCount() { return count; } // static method
};
int Counter::count = 0;     // definition outside class
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a class named \texttt{MyClass} with a variable named \texttt{objectCount} that keeps track of the number of objects created. When the total number of objects is greater than 100, reinitialize \texttt{objectCount} to 0. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2014--15}

%% ── Answer: MyClass with objectCount ────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class MyClass {
    static int objectCount;
public:
    MyClass() {
        objectCount++;
        if (objectCount > 100)
            objectCount = 0;
    }
    static int getObjectCount() { return objectCount; }
};

// Definition outside the class (required for static members)
int MyClass::objectCount = 0;

int main() {
    MyClass a, b, c;
    cout << MyClass::getObjectCount() << endl; // 3
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Key points:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \texttt{objectCount} is \texttt{static} --- shared by all
    objects; incremented in the constructor each time a new object
    is created.
  \item The \texttt{if (objectCount > 100)} guard resets the counter
    to 0 whenever it exceeds 100.
  \item The static member is defined (and initialised to 0) outside
    the class body.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What are the properties of static variables and methods? Explain dynamic method dispatch with example. \mk{6+9}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% -- Q ------------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Static Variables/Methods and Dynamic Method Dispatch}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Properties of static variables:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Shared by \textbf{all objects} of the class --- only one copy exists.
  \item Initialized once when the class is loaded.
  \item Accessible via \texttt{ClassName::variable} without any object.
  \item Lifetime is the entire program.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Properties of static methods:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Belong to the class, not to any instance.
  \item Can only access static members directly.
  \item No \texttt{this} pointer is available inside them.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Counter {
    static int count;
public:
    Counter() { count++; }
    static int getCount() { return count; }
};
int Counter::count = 0;

// Counter::getCount() -- no object needed
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Dynamic Method Dispatch:}

The mechanism by which a virtual function call is resolved at
\textbf{run time} based on the actual type of the object, not the
declared type of the pointer/reference. The compiler uses a
\emph{virtual table} (vtable) per class.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Shape {
public:
    virtual void draw() { cout << "Shape" << endl; }
};
class Circle : public Shape {
public:
    void draw() override { cout << "Circle" << endl; }
};
class Square : public Shape {
public:
    void draw() override { cout << "Square" << endl; }
};

int main() {
    Shape* s;
    s = new Circle(); s->draw();  // Circle -- run-time dispatch
    s = new Square(); s->draw();  // Square -- run-time dispatch
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Explain why the \texttt{protected} access specifier is needed in C++ programming. \mk{3}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% ── Answer: protected access specifier ──────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{When we use \texttt{protected}:}

We use the \texttt{protected} access specifier for a class member
when we want it to be:
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \textbf{Inaccessible} from outside the class (like
    \texttt{private}), AND
  \item \textbf{Accessible} to \emph{derived classes} (unlike
    \texttt{private}).
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Why it is needed:}

Without \texttt{protected}, a derived class would have no access to
the base class's internal data unless it was made \texttt{public}
(which would expose it to everyone --- breaking encapsulation).
\texttt{protected} achieves a middle ground: internal to the
hierarchy, hidden from the outside world.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Animal {
protected:
    string name;   // accessible to derived classes, not to outside
    int    age;
public:
    Animal(string n, int a) : name(n), age(a) {}
};

class Dog : public Animal {
public:
    Dog(string n, int a) : Animal(n, a) {}
    void bark() const {
        // Can access 'name' because it is protected:
        cout << name << " says: Woof!\n";
    }
};

int main() {
    Dog d("Rex", 3);
    d.bark();         // OK
    // d.name;        // ERROR: 'name' is protected
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item How can I call a member function of a class without creating any object of that class? Explain. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% ── Answer: Call member function without object ──────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

The only way to call a member function of a class \emph{without
creating an object} is to declare it as a \textbf{static member
function}.

A static member function does not have a \texttt{this} pointer and
is associated with the class itself, not with any instance.  It can
be called using the class name and the scope resolution operator:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class MathUtils {
public:
    static int square(int x) { return x * x; }
    static double pi()       { return 3.14159265; }
};

int main() {
    // Called without any object:
    int s = MathUtils::square(5);    // s = 25
    double p = MathUtils::pi();      // p = 3.14159...
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Restrictions:} A static member function can only access
\emph{static} data members and call other \emph{static} member
functions.  It cannot access non-static members because there is no
object (and hence no \texttt{this} pointer) associated with the call.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write the detail definition of a class \texttt{Pentagon} that will allow maximum five objects of the class to be created. After the fifth object, any attempt to create a new object will return the reference to the fifth object. \mk{12}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}

%% ── Answer: Pentagon class (max 5 objects) ──────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Pentagon {
    static int  count;       // number of objects created so far
    static Pentagon* last;   // pointer to the 5th (last) object

    Pentagon() { count++; }  // private: can't call new directly

public:
    // Factory method replaces the constructor
    static Pentagon* create() {
        if (count < 5) {
            last = new Pentagon();
            return last;
        }
        // 5 already exist: return the 5th object
        return last;
    }
    static int getCount() { return count; }
};

int      Pentagon::count = 0;
Pentagon* Pentagon::last  = nullptr;

int main() {
    Pentagon* p[7];
    for (int i = 0; i < 7; i++)
        p[i] = Pentagon::create();

    cout << Pentagon::getCount() << endl;          // 5
    cout << (p[4] == p[5] ? "same" : "diff") << endl; // same
    cout << (p[4] == p[6] ? "same" : "diff") << endl; // same
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Design:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item The constructor is \texttt{private} to prevent direct
    instantiation with \texttt{new Pentagon()}.
  \item The \texttt{static} factory method \texttt{create()} is the
    only way to obtain a \texttt{Pentagon}.
  \item After the 5th object, \texttt{create()} returns a pointer to
    the same 5th object every time.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Can constructors/destructors be declared as \texttt{public}, \texttt{private}, or \texttt{protected}? What will be the problems if a constructor/destructor is declared as \texttt{private}? \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}

%% ── Answer: Access specifiers for constructor/destructor ────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Yes}, constructors and destructors can be declared as
\texttt{public}, \texttt{private}, or \texttt{protected}.

\medskip
\textbf{Problems with a \texttt{private} constructor:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Objects \textbf{cannot be created from outside the class}
    using \texttt{new} or stack allocation.
  \item This is intentional in the \textbf{Singleton pattern} and
    \textbf{Factory pattern} --- creation is controlled through a
    static factory method (as in the \texttt{Pentagon} example).
  \item Derived classes \textbf{cannot call} a private constructor
    of the base class directly, preventing uninherited construction.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Problems with a \texttt{private} destructor:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Objects \textbf{cannot be destroyed on the stack} --- the
    compiler calls the destructor when a stack object goes out of
    scope, which fails if it is private.
  \item \texttt{delete ptr} from outside the class will fail.
  \item The class must provide a public \texttt{destroy()} method
    that calls \texttt{delete this} internally.
  \item Useful in reference-counted objects where the object controls
    its own lifetime.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{\texttt{protected} constructor/destructor:}
Allows creation/destruction only within the class itself and its
derived classes --- useful for abstract base classes.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Can a static method of a class use the \texttt{this} pointer? Why or why not? \mk{4}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}

%% ── Answer: Static method and this pointer ───────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{No}, a static member function \emph{cannot} use the
\texttt{this} pointer.

\medskip
\textbf{Why not:}

The \texttt{this} pointer is an implicit parameter automatically
passed to every \emph{non-static} member function.  It points to the
specific object on which the function is being called.

A \textbf{static} member function, however, is associated with the
\emph{class} itself, not with any particular object.  It can be
called without creating an object:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
MyClass::staticMethod();   // no object involved
\end{lstlisting}

Because there is no object in the picture, there is nothing for
\texttt{this} to point to.  The compiler therefore does not pass
a \texttt{this} pointer to static functions, and any attempt to use
\texttt{this} inside a static method is a \textbf{compile error}:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class MyClass {
    int x;
    static int s;
public:
    static void f() {
        s = 10;       // OK  -- accesses static member
        // x = 5;    // ERROR: 'this' is unavailable in static function
        // this->x=5; // ERROR: 'this' is unavailable
    }
};
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a class named \texttt{SelfCounter} that can count the number of instances in a program. Include a member function \texttt{getObjectCount()} which returns the number of objects created. Also write a \texttt{main} function to illustrate its functionality. \mk{7}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2008--09}

%% ── Answer: SelfCounter class ───────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class SelfCounter {
    static int count;   // shared count of live objects
public:
    SelfCounter()              { count++; }   // created
    SelfCounter(const SelfCounter&) { count++; }  // copy
    ~SelfCounter()             { count--; }   // destroyed

    static int getObjectCount() { return count; }
};
int SelfCounter::count = 0;

int main() {
    cout << SelfCounter::getObjectCount() << endl; // 0

    SelfCounter a, b;
    cout << SelfCounter::getObjectCount() << endl; // 2

    {
        SelfCounter c, d, e;
        cout << SelfCounter::getObjectCount() << endl; // 5
    }  // c, d, e destroyed here

    cout << SelfCounter::getObjectCount() << endl; // 2
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Note:} The copy constructor also increments \texttt{count}
because copy-constructing an object creates a new live instance.
The destructor decrements the count when any object (original or
copy) is destroyed.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Explain the usage of \texttt{const} and \texttt{mutable} keywords with examples. \mk{7}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2008--09}

%% ── Answer: const and mutable keywords ──────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{\texttt{const} keyword:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Const variable:} Value cannot be changed after
    initialisation.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
const int MAX = 100;   // MAX cannot be modified
\end{lstlisting}
  \item \textbf{Const member function:} Does not modify any data
    member of the object.  Can be called on \texttt{const} objects.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Circle {
    double radius;
public:
    Circle(double r) : radius(r) {}
    double area() const { return 3.14159 * radius * radius; }
    // area() cannot modify 'radius' or any other member
};
\end{lstlisting}
  \item \textbf{Const pointer / reference parameter:} Prevents the
    function from modifying the passed argument.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void print(const string& s) { cout << s; }
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textbf{\texttt{mutable} keyword:}

A data member declared \texttt{mutable} can be modified even inside
a \texttt{const} member function.  It is used for members that
represent \emph{implementation state} (caches, locks, call counts)
rather than \emph{logical state}.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Database {
    mutable int queryCount;   // mutable: can change in const functions
    string data;
public:
    Database() : queryCount(0) {}
    string fetch(int id) const {
        queryCount++;          // allowed because queryCount is mutable
        return data;
    }
    int getQueryCount() const { return queryCount; }
};
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What are the applications of \texttt{bool} and \texttt{wchar\_t} data types? Explain with examples. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% ── Answer: bool and wchar_t data types ─────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer}},fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{\texttt{bool} data type:}

Introduced in C++ (not in C) to represent Boolean truth values.
Holds exactly one of two values: \texttt{true} (1) or
\texttt{false} (0).

\textbf{Applications:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Flags and condition variables.
  \item Return type of predicate functions.
  \item Control flow conditions.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
bool isPrime(int n) {
    if (n < 2) return false;
    for (int i = 2; i * i <= n; i++)
        if (n % i == 0) return false;
    return true;
}
bool found = false;
if (isPrime(17)) found = true;
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{\texttt{wchar\_t} data type:}

A wide-character type designed to hold characters from large
character sets (Unicode, multi-byte encodings) that cannot fit in a
single \texttt{char} (1 byte).  Typically 2 bytes (Windows/UTF-16)
or 4 bytes (Linux/UTF-32).

\textbf{Applications:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Internationalisation (i18n): storing and processing text in
    non-ASCII scripts (Chinese, Arabic, Bengali, etc.).
  \item Windows API programming (\texttt{LPCWSTR}, \texttt{LPWSTR}).
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <cwchar>
using namespace std;

int main() {
    wchar_t ch   = L'A';           // wide character literal
    wchar_t str[] = L"Hello, \u09AC\u09BE\u0982\u09B2\u09BE"; // "Hello, \u09AC\u09BE\u0982\u09B2\u09BE" (Bengali)
    wcout << str << endl;
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}





\end{enumerate}
\newpage

%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
%% S3 — Inline Functions, References & Default Arguments
%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\secdivider{Section S3}{Inline Functions, References \& Default Arguments}{cC}{cC}
\markboth{S3 --- Inline Functions, References \& Default Arguments}{}
\label{sec:S3}
\titleformat{\section}{\large\bfseries\color{cC}}{}{0em}{}[\titlerule]
\section{S3 --- Inline Functions, References \& Default Arguments}

\begin{enumerate}
\item What is inline function? How does it differ from automatic inline function? ``Inline specifier is a request, not a command'' --- explain. \mk{3+2+5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2020--21}
\variant{CSE 107 | 2017--18}

%% ── S3-Q1 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Inline function vs automatic inline; request not command}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Inline Function:}
A function declared with the \texttt{inline} keyword explicitly
requests the compiler to expand the function body at every call site
instead of generating a normal function call.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
inline int square(int x) { return x * x; }
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Automatic Inline Function:}
Any function defined \emph{inside the class body} is implicitly
treated as inline by the compiler --- no \texttt{inline} keyword is
needed.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Circle {
    double r;
public:
    double area() { return 3.14159 * r * r; } // automatic inline
};
\end{lstlisting}

The key difference: \textbf{explicit inline} is written outside the
class with the keyword; \textbf{automatic inline} happens implicitly
for in-class definitions.

\medskip
\textbf{``Inline specifier is a request, not a command'':}

The \texttt{inline} keyword is only a \emph{hint} to the compiler.
The compiler is free to \textbf{ignore} it and generate a normal
function call if any of the following conditions apply:
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item The function body contains a loop, recursion, or
    \texttt{switch} statement.
  \item The function is too large (exceeds the compiler's size
    threshold).
  \item The address of the function is taken.
  \item The compiler's optimisation level is too low.
\end{itemize}
Conversely, the compiler may inline a function even \emph{without}
the keyword if it judges inlining beneficial (e.g.\ with
\texttt{-O2} flags).

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write three different ways to declare an object of the C++ \texttt{string} class with examples. \mk{8}
\yr{CSE 107 | Jan 2021}


%% ── S3-Q14 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Three ways to declare a string object}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <string>
using namespace std;

int main() {
    // Way 1: Default constructor (empty string)
    string s1;
    s1 = "Hello";
    cout << s1 << "\n";   // Hello

    // Way 2: Initialise with a string literal
    string s2 = "World";
    cout << s2 << "\n";   // World

    // Way 3: Copy from another string object
    string s3(s2);
    cout << s3 << "\n";   // World

    // Bonus: n copies of a character
    string s4(5, '*');
    cout << s4 << "\n";   // *****

    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Two overloaded functions are declared as follows:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void space(char ch) { }
void space(char ch, int count) { }
\end{lstlisting}
Write down the \texttt{main()} function that declares two variables for storing the addresses of the above two functions and makes function calls using those variables. What problem arises if the second function is declared as \texttt{void space(char ch, int count = 0)}? \mk{7}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2020--21; CSE 201 | 2014--15}


%% ── S3-Q16 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Function pointers for overloaded space(); default arg ambiguity}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

void space(char ch)            { cout << ch << "\n"; }
void space(char ch, int count) {
    for (int i = 0; i < count; i++) cout << ch;
    cout << "\n";
}

int main() {
    void (*fp1)(char)      = space;  // first version
    void (*fp2)(char, int) = space;  // second version

    fp1('*');      // *
    fp2('-', 5);   // -----
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Problem if second declared as
\texttt{void space(char ch, int count = 0)}:}

Both \texttt{space(char)} and \texttt{space(char, int=0)} match a
one-argument call.  When the compiler tries to resolve
\texttt{fp1 = space}, it finds two equally valid matches and reports
an \textbf{ambiguity error}.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item \texttt{"If a function returns a reference of any type, then it can be used both as an l-value and r-value"} --- explain with an example program. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20, 2022--23}

%% ── S3-Q7 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Function as l-value and r-value}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

A function can be used as both an l-value and an r-value when it
\textbf{returns a reference}.  The call evaluates to a reference to
the actual memory location --- readable (r-value) and writable
(l-value).

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

int data[5] = {1, 2, 3, 4, 5};
int& item(int i) { return data[i]; }

int main() {
    item(2) = 99;             // l-value: write to data[2]
    cout << data[2] << "\n";  // 99

    int val = item(0);        // r-value: read data[0]
    cout << val << "\n";      // 1

    item(1) = item(4) + 10;   // both: data[1] = data[4]+10 = 15
    cout << data[1] << "\n";  // 15
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is wrong with the following statement?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void func(int x = 99, int y);
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}


%% ── S3-Q13 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- What is wrong with void func(int x=99, int y)?}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void func(int x = 99, int y);   // ERROR
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Problem:} Default arguments must be specified from
\textbf{right to left}.  Once a parameter has a default, every
parameter to its right must also have one.  Here \texttt{x} has a
default but \texttt{y} (to its right) does not --- compile error.

\textbf{Correct declarations:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void func(int x, int y = 0);       // OK: default on rightmost
void func(int x = 0, int y = 0);   // OK: all have defaults
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is an in-line function? What are the advantages and disadvantages? When and why will you make a function inline? When may a compiler ignore this specifier? \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2016--17; CSE 107 | 2017--18}
\variant{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% ── S3-Q2 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Inline function full details}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Inline Function:} A function prefixed with \texttt{inline}
that the compiler replaces at the call site with the actual code
body, avoiding function-call overhead.

\textbf{Advantages:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Eliminates call overhead (no stack frame, no jump/return).
  \item Enables further compiler optimisations at the call site.
  \item Safer than macros: full type checking, no double-evaluation.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Disadvantages:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \textbf{Code bloat:} the function body is duplicated at every
    call site, increasing binary size.
  \item Increased compilation time.
  \item Changes to the body require recompilation of all includers.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{When to make a function inline:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item The body is \textbf{small} (1--3 lines) and called very
    frequently.
  \item Simple accessors / getters:
    \texttt{int getX() const \{ return x; \}}
  \item Performance-critical inner loops.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{When the compiler may ignore the specifier:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Function contains a loop or \texttt{switch}.
  \item Function is recursive.
  \item Function is too large.
  \item The address of the function is taken.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item When can a function be used both as an l-value and r-value? Explain with an example. \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}



\item What is the output of the following code segment?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int& f();
int x;
int main() {
    f() = 10;
    int a = 10, b = 20;
    int& ref = a;
    ref = b;
    cout << a << " " << b << " " << ref << " " << x;
    return 0;
}
int& f() { return x; }
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17}


%% ── S3-Q8 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Output of reference code snippet}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int& f();
int x;
int main() {
    f() = 10;
    int a = 10, b = 20;
    int& ref = a;
    ref = b;
    cout << a << " " << b << " " << ref << " " << x;
    return 0;
}
int& f() { return x; }
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Step-by-step trace:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \texttt{f() = 10;} --- \texttt{f()} returns reference to
    global \texttt{x}. Assigns 10 $\Rightarrow$ \texttt{x = 10}.
  \item \texttt{int a=10, b=20;} --- local variables.
  \item \texttt{int\& ref = a;} --- \texttt{ref} is alias for
    \texttt{a}.
  \item \texttt{ref = b;} --- assigns \texttt{b}'s value (20) to
    \texttt{a} through \texttt{ref}. So \texttt{a = 20}. (Not a
    rebind.)
  \item Output: \texttt{a}=20, \texttt{b}=20, \texttt{ref}=20,
    \texttt{x}=10.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Output:}
\begin{lstlisting}
20 20 20 10
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Explain why namespace is added to C++. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}

%% -- Q ------------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Why Namespace is Added to C++}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Problem without namespace:}

In large programs combining multiple libraries, different libraries may
define identifiers (functions, classes, variables) with the same name.
This causes \textbf{name collision} (ambiguity), and the compiler
cannot determine which one to use.

\textbf{Solution --- namespace:}

A \texttt{namespace} groups related identifiers under a unique name,
preventing collisions.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

namespace LibA {
    void print() { cout << "LibA::print()" << endl; }
}

namespace LibB {
    void print() { cout << "LibB::print()" << endl; }
}

int main() {
    LibA::print();   // no ambiguity
    LibB::print();
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Output:}
\begin{verbatim}
LibA::print()
LibB::print()
\end{verbatim}

The entire C++ standard library lives in namespace \texttt{std}
(\texttt{cout}, \texttt{cin}, \texttt{vector}, etc.) precisely to
avoid collisions with user-defined names.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is meant by ``independent reference'' in C++? How does an independent reference allow a function call to be written to the left side of an assignment operator? \mk{4+4}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2014--15}
\variant{CSE 201 | 2010--11}

%% ── S3-Q5 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Independent reference and l-value assignment}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Independent Reference:} A reference declared as a local
variable --- not as a parameter or return value.  Must be initialised
at declaration; acts as a permanent alias:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int x = 10;
int& r = x;   // r is an independent reference to x
r = 25;       // modifies x; now x == 25
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Function call on the left side of \texttt{=}:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

int vals[3] = {10, 20, 30};
int& getVal(int i) { return vals[i]; }

int main() {
    getVal(1) = 99;           // sets vals[1] = 99
    cout << vals[1] << "\n";  // 99

    int v = getVal(2);        // r-value use: v = 30
    cout << v << "\n";        // 30
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

The returned reference is an alias to \texttt{vals[i]}, so assigning
to \texttt{getVal(1)} directly modifies the array element.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is a reference? Name three different scenarios when a reference can be used. Why does a reference use the dot (\texttt{.}) operator instead of the arrow (\texttt{->}) operator? \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}


%% ── S3-Q4 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Reference --- 3 scenarios, dot vs arrow}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Reference:} An alias for an existing variable declared using
\texttt{\&}.  It must be initialised at declaration and cannot be
rebound to another variable.

\medskip
\textbf{Three scenarios:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Pass-by-reference:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void swap(int& a, int& b) { int t=a; a=b; b=t; }
\end{lstlisting}
  \item \textbf{Return by reference:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int& getElement(int arr[], int i) { return arr[i]; }
getElement(arr, 0) = 100;
\end{lstlisting}
  \item \textbf{Const reference parameter} (avoid copy of large object):
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void print(const string& s) { cout << s; }
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textbf{Why dot (\texttt{.}) not arrow (\texttt{->}):}

\texttt{->} is used with \emph{pointers} because a pointer holds an
address and must be dereferenced.  A \emph{reference} is an alias
that the compiler automatically dereferences; it behaves exactly
like the original variable, so \texttt{.} is used directly:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
struct Point { int x, y; };
Point p = {1, 2};
Point* ptr = &p;
Point& ref = p;

ptr->x = 10;   // pointer: must dereference with ->
ref.x  = 10;   // reference: same as p.x, use .
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Explain why using a default argument is related to constructor overloading. What is wrong with the following function prototype?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
double area(double width = 0, double length);
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% ── S3-Q11 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Default argument and constructor overloading; wrong prototype}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Why default arguments relate to constructor overloading:}

A single constructor with default arguments can accept multiple call
signatures, effectively replacing several overloaded constructors:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Box {
    double h, w, l;
public:
    Box(double h=1.0, double w=1.0, double l=1.0)
        : h(h), w(w), l(l) {}
};
Box b1;                // all defaults
Box b2(5.0);           // w=1, l=1 defaults
Box b3(5.0, 3.0, 2.0); // no defaults
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{What is wrong with:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
double area(double width = 0, double length);  // ERROR
\end{lstlisting}
Once a parameter has a default, all parameters to its \emph{right}
must also have defaults.  Here \texttt{length} follows
\texttt{width} but has no default.

\textbf{Correct versions:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
double area(double length, double width = 0);    // OK
double area(double width = 0, double length = 1); // OK
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a function \texttt{Compute\_Volume} to compute the volume of a 3D box with height \texttt{h}, width \texttt{w}, and length \texttt{l}. Then write a new function \texttt{New\_Compute\_Volume} that computes the volume of a rectangular box as well as a cube using default arguments, such that all of the following calls work:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
New_Compute_Volume(30, 20, 10);
New_Compute_Volume(10, 10, 10);
New_Compute_Volume(10);
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% ── S3-Q15 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- New\_Compute\_Volume with default arguments}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

double Compute_Volume(double h, double w, double l) {
    return h * w * l;
}

// When only h given -> cube; when all given -> general box
double New_Compute_Volume(double h, double w = -1, double l = -1) {
    if (w < 0) { w = h; l = h; }  // cube
    if (l < 0) { l = w; }
    return h * w * l;
}

int main() {
    cout << New_Compute_Volume(30, 20, 10) << "\n"; // 6000
    cout << New_Compute_Volume(10, 10, 10) << "\n"; // 1000
    cout << New_Compute_Volume(10)         << "\n"; // 1000
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item What is a reference? Classify references used in C++ programming. Explain with examples how a returning reference can allow a function to be used on the left side of an assignment. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% ── S3-Q3 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Reference classification and l-value}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Reference:} An alias for an existing variable --- another
name that refers to the same memory location.  Declared with
\texttt{\&}; must be initialised at declaration.

\medskip
\textbf{Classification of references in C++:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Independent (local) reference:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int x = 10;
int& r = x;   // r is an alias for x
r = 20;       // x is now 20
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Reference parameter (call-by-reference):}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void increment(int& n) { n++; }
int a = 5;
increment(a);  // a becomes 6
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Reference return value:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int arr[5] = {10, 20, 30, 40, 50};
int& element(int i) { return arr[i]; }
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Returning a reference allows a function on the left side of
\texttt{=}:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

int arr[] = {10, 20, 30, 40, 50};
int& element(int i) { return arr[i]; }

int main() {
    element(2) = 99;         // equivalent to arr[2] = 99
    cout << arr[2] << "\n";  // prints: 99
    cout << element(0);      // r-value use: prints 10
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item With appropriate example, explain how a default argument produces constructor overloading ambiguity. \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% ── S3-Q12 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Default argument causes constructor overloading ambiguity}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Point {
public:
    Point() {
        cout << "Default constructor\n";
    }
    Point(int x = 0, int y = 0) {
        cout << "Parameterised: " << x << "," << y << "\n";
    }
};

int main() {
    Point p;   // ERROR: ambiguous!
               // Both Point() and Point(int=0, int=0)
               // match a no-argument call.
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Explanation:} Both constructors can be called with no
arguments.  The compiler sees two equally valid matches and reports
an ambiguity error.  \textbf{Fix:} remove one of the constructors
or remove the defaults from \texttt{Point(int, int)}.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is namespace? Write down the problems of using global namespaces. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}
%% ── S3-Q19 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Namespace and global namespace problems}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Namespace:} A named scope that groups related identifiers
to avoid naming conflicts between libraries or modules.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
namespace Math    { double pi = 3.14; }
namespace Physics { double pi = 3.14159265; }
cout << Math::pi;    // 3.14
cout << Physics::pi; // 3.14159265
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Problems with global namespace / \texttt{using namespace std}:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Name collision:} Two libraries may define the same
    name; both in global scope causes ambiguity.
  \item \textbf{Pollutes global scope:} All names become visible
    everywhere, making large programs harder to reason about.
  \item \textbf{Maintenance burden:} A new library name may
    silently clash with existing global names.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Best practice:} Use \texttt{std::cout} or restrict to
\texttt{using std::cout;} in small scopes.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Write a function called \texttt{neg()} that reverses the sign of its integer parameter in two ways --- using a pointer parameter and using a reference parameter. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}


%% ── S3-Q10 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- neg() with pointer and reference}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

// Way 1: using a pointer parameter
void neg_ptr(int* p) { *p = -(*p); }

// Way 2: using a reference parameter
void neg_ref(int& r) { r = -r; }

int main() {
    int a = 7, b = -3;
    neg_ptr(&a);  cout << a << "\n";  // -7
    neg_ref(b);   cout << b << "\n";  // 3
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
& \textbf{Pointer} & \textbf{Reference} \\ \midrule
Caller syntax   & \texttt{neg(\&a)}  & \texttt{neg(a)} \\
Inside function & \texttt{*p = -*p} & \texttt{r = -r} \\
Can be null?    & Yes               & No \\
Readability     & Less natural      & More natural \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Explain some ways that ambiguity can be introduced when overloading functions. Why are the following two overloaded functions inherently ambiguous?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int f(int a);
int f(int &a);
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}


%% ── S3-Q18 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
