  title={\textbf{Answer --- Overloading ambiguity; int f(int) vs int f(int\&)}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Ways ambiguity can be introduced:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Implicit type conversions:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void f(int x)    { }
void f(double x) { }
f(3.14f);  // float converts to both -- ambiguous
\end{lstlisting}
  \item \textbf{Default argument matching another overload:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void g(int x)          { }
void g(int x, int y=0) { }
g(5);  // both match -- ambiguous
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Why \texttt{int f(int a)} and \texttt{int f(int \&a)} are
inherently ambiguous:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int f(int  a) { return a; }
int f(int& a) { return a; }
// f(x) where x is int: both versions match equally.
// Compiler cannot choose. ERROR.
\end{lstlisting}

When \texttt{f(x)} is called with an \texttt{int} variable, the
compiler can either copy \texttt{x} into \texttt{a} (first version)
or bind \texttt{a} as a reference to \texttt{x} (second version).
Both are equally good matches --- inherently ambiguous.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Provide any two restrictions (with examples) while creating references. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08, 2008--09}

%% ── S3-Q9 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Two restrictions on references}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Must be initialised at declaration:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int x = 10;
int& r = x;   // OK
// int& r2;   // ERROR: must be initialised
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Cannot be rebound to another variable:}
    Once bound, any assignment through the reference modifies the
    original variable; it does not rebind the reference.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int a = 5, b = 20;
int& r = a;     // r refers to a
r = b;          // sets a = 20, does NOT rebind r to b
cout << a;      // 20
cout << (&r == &a); // 1 (true: r still refers to a)
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Additional note:} A \texttt{const} reference can bind to a
temporary, but a non-const reference cannot:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
const int& cr = 5 + 3;  // OK: const ref binds to temporary
// int& r = 5 + 3;      // ERROR: non-const ref cannot bind to rvalue
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write short notes on: (i) namespace, (ii) this pointer, (iii) inline function. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2008--09}

%% -- Q ------------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- namespace, this Pointer, inline Function}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{(i) namespace:}

A \texttt{namespace} is a declarative region that provides scope to
identifiers, preventing name collisions across libraries or modules.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
namespace Math {
    int square(int x) { return x * x; }
}
// Math::square(5) -> 25
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{(ii) this pointer:}

Inside every non-static member function, \texttt{this} is an implicit
pointer to the \textbf{current object}. It is used to resolve
ambiguity between member variables and parameters, and to return the
current object.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Point {
    int x, y;
public:
    Point(int x, int y) {
        this->x = x;   // disambiguates member from parameter
        this->y = y;
    }
    Point& move(int dx, int dy) {
        x += dx; y += dy;
        return *this;  // return current object for chaining
    }
};
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{(iii) inline function:}

An \texttt{inline} function is a hint to the compiler to replace each
call with the function body, eliminating call overhead. It is best for
small, frequently called functions.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
inline int cube(int x) { return x * x * x; }

int main() {
    int r = cube(3);   // compiler may expand to: 3*3*3
    // r = 27
}
\end{lstlisting}

Declaring a function inside a class body makes it implicitly inline.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Explain the difference between binary and unary scope resolution operators with examples. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2008--09, 2007--08}


%% ── S3-Q21 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- S3-Q24-25: Binary and unary scope resolution operators}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Unary \texttt{::}} --- no left operand; accesses global scope
when a local name shadows it.

\textbf{Binary \texttt{::}} --- left operand is a class or namespace;
qualifies which scope an identifier belongs to.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

int val = 100;      // global

namespace Math { double pi = 3.14; }

class Circle {
    double r;
public:
    Circle(double r) : r(r) {}
    static double pi;
    double area();
};
double Circle::pi = 3.14159;      // binary :: -- defines static member
double Circle::area() {            // binary :: -- defines member function
    return Circle::pi * r * r;    // binary :: -- accesses static member
}

int main() {
    int val = 200;                 // local
    cout << val    << "\n";        // 200 -- local
    cout << ::val  << "\n";        // 100 -- unary :: global

    cout << Math::pi << "\n";      // 3.14 -- binary :: namespace

    Circle c(5.0);
    cout << c.area() << "\n";      // 78.5...
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item \texttt{"A reference parameter fully automates the call-by-reference parameter passing mechanism"} --- explain with example. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% ── S3-Q6 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Reference parameter automates call-by-reference}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

In C, call-by-reference requires manual pointer manipulation.
In C++, a reference parameter automates this entirely:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

// C-style: manual pointer manipulation
void swap_ptr(int* a, int* b) {
    int t = *a; *a = *b; *b = t;
}

// C++ style: reference parameter -- fully automatic
void swap_ref(int& a, int& b) {
    int t = a; a = b; b = t;
}

int main() {
    int x = 5, y = 10;

    swap_ptr(&x, &y);          // caller must write &x, &y
    cout << x << " " << y;    // 10 5

    swap_ref(x, y);            // caller writes x, y -- natural
    cout << x << " " << y;    // 5 10
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

With reference parameters, the caller passes variables directly
(no \texttt{\&} needed), yet the function receives the actual
variables.  The call-by-reference mechanism is \textbf{fully
automated} by the compiler.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item The effect of a default argument can be alternatively achieved by overloading. Discuss with an example. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}


%% ── S3-Q17 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Default argument vs overloading}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Using default argument (single function):}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void printLine(char ch = '-', int n = 10) {
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) cout << ch;
    cout << "\n";
}
printLine();         // ----------
printLine('*');      // **********
printLine('=', 5);   // =====
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Equivalent using overloading (three functions):}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void printLine() {
    for (int i = 0; i < 10; i++) cout << '-'; cout << "\n";
}
void printLine(char ch) {
    for (int i = 0; i < 10; i++) cout << ch;  cout << "\n";
}
void printLine(char ch, int n) {
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++)  cout << ch;  cout << "\n";
}
\end{lstlisting}

Both produce the same interface.  Default arguments are more
\textbf{concise}; overloading gives more \textbf{flexibility}
(each version can behave differently).

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Is there anything wrong with the following code? If yes, what should be done to correct it?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;
namespace A {
    int i;
    void dispI() { cout << i; }
}
void main() {
    namespace Inside {
        int insideI;
        void dispInsideI() { cout << insideI; }
    }
    A::i = 10;
    cout << A::i;
    A::dispI();
    Inside::insideI = 20;
    cout << Inside::insideI;
    Inside::dispInsideI();
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% ── S3-Q20 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Problems in namespace code; corrected version}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Problems:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{\texttt{void main()}:} Standard C++ requires
    \texttt{int main()}.
  \item \textbf{Namespace inside a function:} A \texttt{namespace}
    declaration is \textbf{not allowed} inside a function body.
    Namespaces can only appear at namespace scope.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Corrected code:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

namespace A {
    int i;
    void dispI() { cout << i << "\n"; }
}
namespace Inside {          // moved to global scope
    int insideI;
    void dispInsideI() { cout << insideI << "\n"; }
}

int main() {               // int, not void
    A::i = 10;
    cout << A::i << "\n";
    A::dispI();
    Inside::insideI = 20;
    cout << Inside::insideI << "\n";
    Inside::dispInsideI();
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}




\item Can we use the same function name for a member function of a class and an outside function in the same program file? If yes, how are they distinguished? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}


%% ── S3-Q22 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Member vs global function with same name}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Yes}, the same function name can be used for a member
function and a global function.  They are distinguished by calling
syntax and the scope resolution operator:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

void print() { cout << "Global print()\n"; }

class Printer {
public:
    void print() { cout << "Printer::print()\n"; }
    void test() {
        print();    // calls member (this->print())
        ::print();  // calls global (unary ::)
    }
};

int main() {
    Printer p;
    p.print();   // Printer::print()
    print();     // Global print()
    p.test();    // both
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \texttt{obj.print()} or \texttt{Printer::print()} $\to$
    member function.
  \item \texttt{print()} in non-member context $\to$ global function.
  \item \texttt{::print()} inside the class $\to$ explicitly global.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}


\end{enumerate}
\newpage

%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
%% S4 — Friend Functions & Static Members
%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\secdivider{Section S4}{Friend Functions }{cD}{cD}
\markboth{S4 --- Friend Functions }{}
\label{sec:S4}
\titleformat{\section}{\large\bfseries\color{cD}}{}{0em}{}[\titlerule]
\section{S4 --- Friend Functions }

\begin{enumerate}
\item Design a program that defines a friend function which can access and modify the private variables of two different classes. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23}

%% ── S4-Q9 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Friend function accesses and modifies two classes}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class ClassB;

class ClassA {
    int a;
public:
    ClassA(int x) : a(x) {}
    void show() const { cout << "A.a = " << a << "\n"; }
    friend void swap_and_double(ClassA& ca, ClassB& cb);
};

class ClassB {
    int b;
public:
    ClassB(int x) : b(x) {}
    void show() const { cout << "B.b = " << b << "\n"; }
    friend void swap_and_double(ClassA& ca, ClassB& cb);
};

void swap_and_double(ClassA& ca, ClassB& cb) {
    int temp = ca.a;
    ca.a = cb.b * 2;
    cb.b = temp * 2;
}

int main() {
    ClassA ca(5); ClassB cb(10);
    cout << "Before:\n"; ca.show(); cb.show();
    swap_and_double(ca, cb);
    cout << "After:\n";  ca.show(); cb.show();
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Output:}
\begin{lstlisting}
Before:
A.a = 5
B.b = 10
After:
A.a = 20
B.b = 10
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write an example program to demonstrate the use of forward declaration of a class. \mk{11}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22}


%% ── S4-Q10 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Forward declaration --- complete example}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Node;   // forward declaration

class LinkedList {
    Node* head;   // pointer to incomplete type is valid
public:
    LinkedList();
    void push(int val);
    void display() const;
};

class Node {
    int data;
    Node* next;
public:
    Node(int d) : data(d), next(nullptr) {}
    friend class LinkedList;
};

LinkedList::LinkedList() : head(nullptr) {}

void LinkedList::push(int val) {
    Node* n = new Node(val);
    n->next = head; head = n;
}

void LinkedList::display() const {
    Node* cur = head;
    while (cur) { cout << cur->data << " -> "; cur = cur->next; }
    cout << "NULL\n";
}

int main() {
    LinkedList list;
    list.push(30); list.push(20); list.push(10);
    list.display();   // 10 -> 20 -> 30 -> NULL
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item \texttt{"A friend function can be a global function not related to any particular class. A friend function can also be a member of another class."} --- Explain this statement with appropriate examples. \mk{12}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}


%% ── S4-Q8 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Friend as global and as member of another class}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{(i) Global friend function:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Celsius;
class Fahrenheit {
    double fahr;
public:
    Fahrenheit(double f) : fahr(f) {}
    friend void convert(Fahrenheit, Celsius&);
};
class Celsius {
    double cel;
public:
    Celsius() : cel(0) {}
    void show() const { cout << cel << " C\n"; }
    friend void convert(Fahrenheit, Celsius&);
};
void convert(Fahrenheit f, Celsius& c) {
    c.cel = (f.fahr - 32) * 5.0 / 9.0;
}
int main() {
    Fahrenheit f(212.0); Celsius c;
    convert(f, c); c.show();  // 100 C
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{(ii) Member of another class as friend:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Distance;
class Printer {
public:
    void printDistance(Distance& d);
};
class Distance {
    int metres;
public:
    Distance(int m) : metres(m) {}
    friend void Printer::printDistance(Distance&);
};
void Printer::printDistance(Distance& d) {
    cout << d.metres << " m\n";
}
int main() {
    Printer p; Distance d(42);
    p.printDistance(d);   // 42 m
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item What is a friend function? Why is friend function used in C++ programming? \mk{7}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2015--16}

%% ════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
%% S4 — Friend Functions & Static Members — ANSWERS
%% ════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

%% ── S4-Q1 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Friend function definition and purpose}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Friend Function:} A non-member function granted access to a
class's \texttt{private} and \texttt{protected} members via the
\texttt{friend} keyword inside the class.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Box {
    double side;
public:
    Box(double s) : side(s) {}
    friend double volume(Box b);
};
double volume(Box b) {
    return b.side * b.side * b.side;  // accesses private
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why used:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item Operator overloading where left operand is not the class
    type (e.g.\ \texttt{<<} for \texttt{ostream}).
  \item Symmetric binary operations (e.g.\ \texttt{a+b} ==
    \texttt{b+a}).
  \item A utility function needing private data but logically not
    a class member.
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item What is a friend function? When is a friend function used? Give an example of forward class declaration. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}


%% ── S4-Q2 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Friend function; when used; forward class declaration}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{When used:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item A function needs private members of two or more classes.
  \item Symmetric binary operator overloading.
  \item A non-member utility that logically operates on class data.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Forward class declaration:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class ClassB;   // forward declaration

class ClassA {
    int a;
public:
    ClassA(int x) : a(x) {}
    friend void show(ClassA, ClassB);
};

class ClassB {
    int b;
public:
    ClassB(int x) : b(x) {}
    friend void show(ClassA, ClassB);
};

void show(ClassA oa, ClassB ob) {
    cout << "A=" << oa.a << "  B=" << ob.b << "\n";
}

int main() {
    show(ClassA(10), ClassB(20));  // A=10  B=20
}
\end{lstlisting}

Without the forward declaration, the compiler would not recognise
\texttt{ClassB} when it appears inside \texttt{ClassA}.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is a friend function? Can a member function of a class be a friend function of another class? If yes, give a code example. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}


%% ── S4-Q3 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Member function of one class as friend of another}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Yes}, a member function of one class can be a friend of
another:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Alpha;

class Beta {
public:
    void display(Alpha& a);  // will be friend of Alpha
};

class Alpha {
    int secret = 42;
    friend void Beta::display(Alpha&);  // member of Beta is friend
};

void Beta::display(Alpha& a) {
    cout << "Alpha's secret: " << a.secret << "\n";
}

int main() {
    Beta b; Alpha a;
    b.display(a);   // Alpha's secret: 42
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Key rules:} Forward declaration of \texttt{Alpha} before
\texttt{Beta}; \texttt{friend} names the specific member function;
definition of \texttt{Beta::display} after full definition of
\texttt{Alpha}.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item How does a friend function differ from a member of a class? What are the advantages of using friend functions? \mk{6+6+15}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11, 2009--10}

%% ── S4-Q4 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Friend vs member function; advantages}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Friend Function} & \textbf{Member Function} \\
\midrule
Not a member of the class. & A member of the class. \\[3pt]
No implicit \texttt{this} pointer. & Has implicit \texttt{this}. \\[3pt]
Called as \texttt{func(obj)}. & Called as \texttt{obj.func()}. \\[3pt]
Can access private members of
multiple classes simultaneously. &
Only accesses own class's private. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Advantages of friend functions:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item Can access private data of two or more classes.
  \item Enables symmetric operator overloading.
  \item Keeps class interface minimal while granting necessary access.
  \item Flexible: can be a global function, a member of another
    class, or a whole friend class.
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Explain the following statements regarding friend functions with examples:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item A friend function is not a member of a class but still has access to its private members.
  \item A friend function can be a global function not related to any particular class.
  \item A friend function can be a member of another class.
\end{enumerate}
\mk{21}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2008--09}


%% ── S4-Q5 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Three statements about friend functions}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{(\alph*)}]

\item \textbf{Not a member but accesses private:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Account {
    double balance;
public:
    Account(double b) : balance(b) {}
    friend void audit(Account a);
};
void audit(Account a) {
    cout << "Balance: " << a.balance << "\n";
}
\end{lstlisting}

\item \textbf{Global function as friend:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Rectangle {
    double w, h;
public:
    Rectangle(double w, double h) : w(w), h(h) {}
    friend double area(Rectangle r);
};
double area(Rectangle r) { return r.w * r.h; }
\end{lstlisting}

\item \textbf{Member of another class as friend:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Alpha;
class Beta {
public:
    void inspect(Alpha& a);
};
class Alpha {
    int x = 7;
    friend void Beta::inspect(Alpha&);
};
void Beta::inspect(Alpha& a) {
    cout << "Alpha.x = " << a.x << "\n";
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{enumerate}
\end{tcolorbox}


\item \texttt{"A friend function is not a member of a class but still has access to its private elements"} --- explain with an example. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

\item When is a friend function compulsory? Give an example. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}


%% ── S4-Q6 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- When is a friend function compulsory?}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

A friend function is \textbf{compulsory} when overloading
\texttt{<<} and \texttt{>>} (stream operators), because the left
operand is \texttt{ostream}/\texttt{istream}, not our class.  We
cannot add a member to the standard library, so a friend is the
only option:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Point {
    int x, y;
public:
    Point(int x, int y) : x(x), y(y) {}

    friend ostream& operator<<(ostream& os, const Point& p) {
        os << "(" << p.x << ", " << p.y << ")";
        return os;
    }
    friend istream& operator>>(istream& is, Point& p) {
        is >> p.x >> p.y;
        return is;
    }
};

int main() {
    Point p(3, 4);
    cout << p << "\n";   // (3, 4)
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item \texttt{"A friend function can be used to overload the assignment operator `='."} --- True or False? Explain your answer. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}


%% ── S4-Q7 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Can friend function overload assignment operator?}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{False.} A friend function \textbf{cannot} overload
\texttt{operator=}.

The C++ standard requires \texttt{=}, \texttt{[]}, \texttt{()},
and \texttt{->} to be overloaded as \textbf{member functions} only.

For \texttt{a = b}, the left operand must be the implicit
\texttt{this} object --- only possible with a member function.  A
friend has no \texttt{this} and therefore cannot serve as the
assignment operator:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class MyClass {
    int val;
public:
    MyClass(int v) : val(v) {}
    // CORRECT: member function
    MyClass& operator=(const MyClass& rhs) {
        val = rhs.val;
        return *this;
    }
    // friend MyClass& operator=(...) -- ILLEGAL, compile error
};
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Distinguish between static and non-static class members with examples. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}


%% ── S4-Q11 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Static vs non-static class members}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Static Member} & \textbf{Non-Static Member} \\
\midrule
Belongs to the class; one copy
shared by all objects. &
Belongs to each object; every
instance has its own copy. \\[3pt]
Exists before any object is created. &
Created when an object is instantiated. \\[3pt]
Accessed as \texttt{ClassName::member}. &
Accessed as \texttt{obj.member}. \\[3pt]
Static method has no \texttt{this}. &
Non-static method has \texttt{this}. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Counter {
    static int sCount;   // shared by all
    int id;              // unique per object
public:
    Counter()  { id = ++sCount; }
    ~Counter() { sCount--; }
    static int getCount() { return sCount; }
    int getId() const { return id; }
};
int Counter::sCount = 0;

int main() {
    Counter c1, c2, c3;
    cout << Counter::getCount() << "\n";  // 3
    cout << c1.getId() << " " << c2.getId() << " " << c3.getId() << "\n"; // 1 2 3
    { Counter c4; cout << Counter::getCount() << "\n"; }  // 4
    cout << Counter::getCount() << "\n";  // 3 (c4 destroyed)
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}



\end{enumerate}
\newpage

%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
%% S5 — Constructors, Destructors & Copy Constructor
%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\secdivider{Section S5}{Constructors, Destructors \& Copy Constructor}{cE}{cE}
\markboth{S5 --- Constructors, Destructors \& Copy Constructor}{}
\label{sec:S5}
\titleformat{\section}{\large\bfseries\color{cE}}{}{0em}{}[\titlerule]
\section{S5 --- Constructors, Destructors \& Copy Constructor}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Consider the following program and mention the line(s) where the copy constructor is called. What will be the output of this program?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;
class MyClass {
public:
    int val;
    int multiplier;
    MyClass(int n, int m = 2) { val = n; multiplier = m; }
    MyClass(const MyClass& obj) {
        val = obj.val;
        multiplier = obj.multiplier;
    }
    void setValue(int v, int m = 5) { val = v; multiplier = m; }
    int calculateValue() { return val * multiplier; }
};
void functionC(MyClass* arr, int size) {
    for (int i = 0; i < size; ++i)
        cout << arr[i].calculateValue() << endl;
}
int main() {
    MyClass* arr = new MyClass[2]{MyClass(5, 2), MyClass(6)};
    arr[0] = arr[1];
    functionC(arr, 2);
    arr[0].setValue(15, 4);
    arr[1].setValue(30);
    cout << arr[0].calculateValue() << endl;
    cout << arr[1].calculateValue() << endl;
    delete[] arr;
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{13}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23}


%% ── S5-Q10 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- MyClass array --- copy constructor lines and output}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{Copy constructor called:} when initialising \texttt{arr[0]}
and \texttt{arr[1]} from the temporary \texttt{MyClass(5,2)} and
\texttt{MyClass(6)} objects.
\texttt{arr[0] = arr[1]} uses the \textbf{copy assignment operator}
(not copy constructor).

\textbf{Output:}
\begin{lstlisting}
12
12
60
150
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Trace:} After \texttt{arr[0]=arr[1]}: both have val=6,
mult=2 $\Rightarrow$ 6*2=12 each.
After \texttt{setValue}: arr[0]=15*4=60; arr[1]=30*5=150.

\end{tcolorbox}



\item Write One C++ statement to implement each of the following:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Using \texttt{new}, declare a pointer and allocate memory for a \texttt{char} variable initialized to \texttt{'E'}.
  \item Using \texttt{new}, declare a pointer and allocate memory for an array of 5 \texttt{float} variables.
  \item Using \texttt{delete}, delete the entire integer array pointed to by \texttt{a}.
  \item Using \texttt{new}, declare a pointer and allocate memory for an object of class \texttt{A} (no constructor defined).
\end{enumerate}
\mk{12}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23}


%% ── S5-Q18 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- One-line new/delete statements}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
  \item \begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
char *cp = new char('E');
\end{lstlisting}
  \item \begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
float *fp = new float[5];
\end{lstlisting}
  \item \begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
delete[] a;
\end{lstlisting}
  \item \begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
A *ap = new A;
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is the problem with the following code segment? Write a problem with \texttt{s1 = s2} and provide a solution:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class strtype {
    char *p; int len;
public:
    strtype(char *s) {
        len = strlen(s) + 1;
        p = new char[len];
        strcpy(p, s);
    }
    ~strtype() { delete[] p; }
};
strtype s1("LEVEL"), s2("TERM");
s1 = s2;
\end{lstlisting}
Without changing the constructor and destructor, write code to solve the problem. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22}

%% ── S5-Q8 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- strtype s1=s2 problem and overloaded = solution}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{Problems:} Default \texttt{=} shallow-copies \texttt{p}:
both \texttt{s1.p} and \texttt{s2.p} point to the same array.
Old \texttt{s1} buffer (\texttt{"LEVEL"}) is \textbf{leaked}, and
when both are destroyed the shared buffer is \textbf{freed twice}.

\textbf{Solution --- overload \texttt{operator=}:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <cstring>
using namespace std;

class strtype {
    char *p; int len;
public:
    strtype(char *s) {
        len = strlen(s)+1; p = new char[len]; strcpy(p,s);
    }
    ~strtype() { delete[] p; }

    strtype& operator=(const strtype& obj) {
        if (this == &obj) return *this; // self-assignment guard
        delete[] p;                     // free old memory
        len = obj.len;
        p = new char[len];              // allocate fresh memory
        strcpy(p, obj.p);
        return *this;
    }
    void show() const { cout << p << "\n"; }
};

int main() {
    strtype s1("LEVEL"), s2("TERM");
    s1 = s2;
    s1.show();  // TERM
    s2.show();  // TERM
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Differentiate between the \texttt{new} operator and \texttt{malloc()} function. Design \texttt{Class A} so that the following code does not produce any error:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int main() {
    A obj(10);
    A *array = new A[1];
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{13}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22}


%% ── S5-Q19 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- new vs malloc; design Class A}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{\texttt{new}} & \textbf{\texttt{malloc()}} \\ \midrule
C++ operator & C library function \\
Calls constructor automatically & Does not call constructors \\
Throws \texttt{bad\_alloc} on failure & Returns \texttt{NULL} on failure \\
Type-safe & Returns \texttt{void*}; requires cast \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Class A --- needs both default and parameterised constructor:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class A {
    int val;
public:
    A(): val(0){}      // default: needed for new A[1]
    A(int v): val(v){} // parameterised: needed for A obj(10)
};
int main() {
    A obj(10);       // parameterised
    A *array = new A[1]; // default
    delete[] array;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Consider the following overloaded functions and develop the equivalent function using default arguments:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void f2() { int a = 0, b = 0; ... }
void f2(int a) { int b = 0; ... }
void f2(int a, int b) { ... }
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{6}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22}


%% ── S5-Q20 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Overloaded f2() using default arguments}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
// Three overloads:
// void f2()             { int a=0, b=0; ... }
// void f2(int a)        { int b=0; ... }
// void f2(int a, int b) { ... }

// Single equivalent with defaults:
void f2(int a=0, int b=0) {
    // a=0,b=0 when called as f2()
    // b=0     when called as f2(5)
    // no default used when called as f2(5,3)
}

// All three calls still valid:
f2();        // a=0, b=0
f2(10);      // a=10, b=0
f2(10, 20);  // a=10, b=20
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Explain what a default copy constructor generated by the compiler does. Briefly describe a circumstance where this will cause a problem. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}

%% ── S5-Q7 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Compiler-generated copy constructor and its problem}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{What it does:} Member-wise (shallow) copy --- each data
member copied by value.  For pointer members, only the address is
copied, not the pointed-to data.

\textbf{Problem:} When a class contains a raw pointer to heap memory,
shallow copy makes both objects share the same block.  One object's
destructor frees it; the other is left with a dangling pointer and
causes a double-free crash.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Buffer {
    char *data;
public:
    Buffer(const char* s) { data=new char[strlen(s)+1]; strcpy(data,s); }
    ~Buffer() { delete[] data; }
    // Compiler generates: Buffer(const Buffer& o){ data=o.data; } -- WRONG
};
int main() {
    Buffer b1("Hello");
    Buffer b2 = b1;   // b1.data == b2.data (same pointer!)
    // ~b2: frees data. ~b1: frees same data again -- CRASH
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Fix:} Write a user-defined deep copy constructor.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What are the possible problems if we use ``call by value'' instead of ``call by reference'' for passing an object to a function? Explain with an example. How can we solve this problem? \mk{13}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}
\variant{CSE 201 | 2009--10}

%% ── S5-Q5 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Call-by-value problems for objects; solution}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{Problems:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Double-free / dangling pointer:} shallow copy shares
    heap memory; parameter's destructor frees it on return.
  \item \textbf{Performance:} copying large objects wastes time.
  \item \textbf{Shared state modification:} changes through the copy
    affect the original's heap data before the copy is destroyed.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Solutions:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
  \item Define a deep copy constructor.
  \item Pass by \texttt{const} reference: \texttt{void f(const Buffer\& b)} --- no copy made at all.
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What will be the output of the following program?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Point {
private:
    int x, y;
public:
    Point(int i = 0, int j = 0);
    Point(const Point &t);
};
Point::Point(int i, int j) {
    x = i; y = j;
    cout << "Normal Constructor called\n";
}
Point::Point(const Point &t) {
    x = t.x; y = t.y;
    cout << "Copy Constructor called\n";
}
int main() {
    Point *t1, *t2;
    t1 = new Point(10, 15);
    t2 = new Point(*t1);
    Point t3 = *t1;
    Point t4;
    t4 = t3;
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{9}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}


%% ── S5-Q6 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Output of Point program}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{Trace:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*:}]
  \item \texttt{t1 = new Point(10,15);} \\$\Rightarrow$ \textbf{Normal Constructor called}
  \item \texttt{t2 = new Point(*t1);} --- copy constructor.\\ $\Rightarrow$ \textbf{Copy Constructor called}
  \item \texttt{Point t3 = *t1;} --- initialisation, copy constructor. \\$\Rightarrow$ \textbf{Copy Constructor called}
  \item \texttt{Point t4;} --- default (i=0,j=0). \\$\Rightarrow$ \textbf{Normal Constructor called}
  \item \texttt{t4 = t3;} --- assignment (not initialisation), no copy constructor.\\ $\Rightarrow$ \textit{no output}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Output:}
\begin{lstlisting}
Normal Constructor called
Copy Constructor called
Copy Constructor called
Normal Constructor called
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is the difference between \texttt{delete} and \texttt{delete[]} operators? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08; CSE 107 | 2017--18}


%% ── S5-Q14 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- delete vs delete[]}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{\texttt{delete}} & \textbf{\texttt{delete[]}} \\
\midrule
For a \textbf{single} object allocated with \texttt{new}. &
For an \textbf{array} allocated with \texttt{new[]}. \\[3pt]
Calls destructor of \textbf{one} object. &
Calls destructor of \textbf{every} array element. \\[3pt]
Using on a \texttt{new[]} allocation is \textbf{undefined behaviour}. &
Using on a single \texttt{new} is \textbf{undefined behaviour}. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int*    p1 = new int;       delete   p1;   // correct
int*    p2 = new int[100];  delete[] p2;   // correct
string* p3 = new string[5]; delete[] p3;   // calls ~string() x5
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write the following: \texttt{myClass ob[] = \{-1, -2\};}. Is this valid? If yes, what does it mean? If no, what is the problem? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}


%% ── S5-Q16 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- myClass ob[] = \{-1, -2\} --- valid?}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{Valid} --- if \texttt{myClass} has a single-\texttt{int}
constructor.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class myClass {
    int val;
public:
    myClass(int v): val(v){}
    int get() const { return val; }
};

myClass ob[] = {-1, -2};
// Equivalent to: myClass ob[2] = {myClass(-1), myClass(-2)};
// ob[0].get() = -1,  ob[1].get() = -2
\end{lstlisting}

Each element in the initialiser list is implicitly converted to a
\texttt{myClass} object using the single-\texttt{int} constructor.
If the constructor is \texttt{explicit} or does not exist, this is
a compile error.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item How does the copy constructor differ from the assignment operator (\texttt{=})? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17}


%% ── S5-Q13 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Copy constructor vs assignment operator}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Copy Constructor} & \textbf{Assignment Operator (\texttt{=})} \\
\midrule
Creates a \emph{new} object. &
Copies to an \emph{existing} object. \\[3pt]
Called during \textbf{initialisation}:\newline\texttt{MyClass b = a;} &
Called during \textbf{assignment}:\newline\texttt{b = a;} (b already exists). \\[3pt]
Destination is new; no old resources to free. &
Must free destination's old resources first. \\[3pt]
Cannot return a value. &
Returns \texttt{*this} for chaining. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item What is the problem when an object of \texttt{Animal} is passed by value? What are the ways to solve it? Write a copy constructor to resolve it. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Animal {
    int *age;
public:
    Animal(int arg) { age = new int; *age = arg; }
    ~Animal() { delete age; }
};
\end{lstlisting}



%% ── S5-Q15 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Animal class --- copy constructor problem and fix}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{Problem when passed by value:} Shallow copy makes both the
original and the copy share the same \texttt{int*age} pointer.
When the copy is destroyed, its destructor calls \texttt{delete age},
leaving the original with a dangling pointer.

\textbf{Ways to solve:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
  \item Define a deep copy constructor.
  \item Pass by reference / const reference.
  \item Overload assignment operator.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Deep copy constructor:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Animal {
    int *age;
public:
    Animal(int arg) { age=new int; *age=arg; }

    Animal(const Animal& obj) {
        age=new int;        // fresh allocation
        *age=*obj.age;
    }
    ~Animal() { delete age; }
    int getAge() const { return *age; }
};
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is the output of the following code?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Animal {
    int age;
public:
    Animal() { cout << "Constructing\n"; age = 0; }
    ~Animal() { cout << "Destructing\n"; }
};
Animal process(Animal a) {
    cout << "In process\n";
    Animal b;
    b = a;
    return b;
}
int main() {
    cout << "First line\n";
    Animal a;
    cout << "Second line\n";
    Animal b = a;
    cout << "Third line\n";
    Animal c = process(b);
    cout << "Return line\n";
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17}

%% ── S5-Q17 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Output of Animal process() code}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{Output (verified by compiler):}
\begin{lstlisting}
First line
Constructing
Second line
Third line
In process
Constructing
Destructing
Return line
Destructing
Destructing
Destructing
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Trace:} \texttt{a} constructed; \texttt{b=a} (compiler copy,
no print); \texttt{process(b)} --- param copy (no print), then local
\texttt{b} constructed (\texttt{Constructing}), local \texttt{b} and
param destroyed on return (\texttt{Destructing} twice); finally
\texttt{c}, \texttt{b}, \texttt{a} destroyed at end of main.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Consider the following class definition:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class myclass {
    int *p;
public:
    myclass(int n) {
        p = (int*)malloc(sizeof(int));
        if (!p) { cout << "Allocation problem\n"; exit(1); }
        *p = n;
    }
    ~myclass() { free(p); }
};
\end{lstlisting}
Write down the problem associated with the class declaration. Write a copy constructor to solve the problem and explain how it works. \mk{5+5+5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2014--15}


\item Consider the following class definition:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class myclass {
    int *p;
public:
    myclass(int i) { p = (int*)malloc(sizeof(int)); *p = i; }
    ~myclass() { free(p); cout << "Freeing..\r"; }
    int get() { return *p; }
};
\end{lstlisting}
What is the problem when an object of the class type is passed to a regular function as a parameter? Write a copy constructor to resolve the problem. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% ── S5-Q3 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- S5-Q3 \& Q4: myclass shallow copy problem and copy constructor fix}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{The problem:} When a \texttt{myclass} object is passed by
value, the default copy constructor makes a shallow copy---both the
original and the parameter share the same \texttt{malloc}-allocated
integer.  When the function returns, the parameter's destructor calls
\texttt{free(p)}, freeing the shared memory.  The original now holds
a \textbf{dangling pointer}; its destructor causes a
\textbf{double-free crash}.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <cstdlib>
using namespace std;

class myclass {
    int *p;
public:
    myclass(int i) { p = (int*)malloc(sizeof(int)); *p = i; }

    // Deep copy constructor
    myclass(const myclass& obj) {
        p = (int*)malloc(sizeof(int));
        *p = *obj.p;
    }

    ~myclass() { free(p); cout << "Freeing..\n"; }
    int get()  { return *p; }
};

void display(myclass ob) { cout << ob.get() << "\n"; }

int main() {
    myclass a(100);
    display(a);      // deep copy; a is safe
    cout << a.get(); // 100
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is a copy constructor? What are the three cases when a copy constructor is invoked? Give examples using code. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}


%% ── S5-Q1 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Copy constructor --- definition and 3 invocation cases}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{Copy Constructor:} A special constructor that initialises a
new object as a copy of an existing object.  Prototype:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
ClassName(const ClassName& obj);
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Three cases when the copy constructor is invoked:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Object initialisation from another object:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
MyClass a(10);
MyClass b = a;    // copy constructor called
MyClass c(a);     // copy constructor called
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Passing an object by value to a function:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
void func(MyClass x) { }   // copy constructor called for x
func(a);
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Returning an object by value from a function:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
MyClass create() {
    MyClass tmp(99);
    return tmp;   // copy constructor may be called (if RVO absent)
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item What is the output of the following code segment?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class myclass {
    int who;
public:
    myclass(int n) { who = n; cout << "Constructing" << who << "\n"; }
    ~myclass() { cout << "Destructing" << who << "\n"; }
    int id() { return who; }
};
void f(myclass &o) { cout << "Received" << o.id() << "\n"; }
int main() {
    myclass x(1);
    f(x);
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}


%% ── S5-Q9 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Output of myclass pass-by-reference code}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{Output:}
\begin{lstlisting}
Constructing1
Received1
Destructing1
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Explanation:} \texttt{f} takes a reference --- no copy is
made.  Only one constructor and one destructor call occur.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is the problem with the following code segment?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class array {
    int *p; int size;
public:
    array(int sz) {
        try { p = new int[sz]; }
        catch (bad_alloc xa) { cout << "Allocation Failure\n"; exit(EXIT_FAILURE); }
        size = sz;
    }
    ~array() { delete[] p; }
    void put(int i, int j) { if (i >= 0 && i < size) p[i] = j; }
    int get(int i) { return p[i]; }
};
void print(array &ob) {
    array x = ob;
    for (int i = 0; i < 10; i++) cout << x.get(i);
}
int main() {
    array num(10);
    for (int i = 0; i < 10; i++) num.put(i, i);
    print(num);
    for (int i = 9; i >= 0; i--) cout << num.get(i);
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% ── S5-Q12 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- array class shallow copy problem in print()}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{Problem:} \texttt{array x = ob;} inside \texttt{print()}
does a shallow copy.  Both \texttt{x.p} and \texttt{ob.p} (i.e.\
\texttt{num.p}) point to the same array.  When \texttt{print}
returns and \texttt{x} is destroyed, its destructor frees the shared
array.  The loop in \texttt{main} then accesses freed memory
(undefined behaviour), and \texttt{num}'s destructor causes a
double-free crash.

\textbf{Fix --- deep copy constructor:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
array(const array& obj) {
    size = obj.size;
    p = new int[size];
    for (int i=0; i<size; i++) p[i] = obj.p[i];
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item The class \texttt{dyna} is defined as follows:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class dyna {
    int *p;
public:
    dyna(int i);
    ~dyna() { free(p); }
    int get() { return *p; }
};
\end{lstlisting}
Write the constructor \texttt{dyna(int i)}. Also write a function called \texttt{neg()} that reverses the sign of its integer parameter. What will be the problem if \texttt{cout << neg(ob);} is executed in \texttt{main()}? Write a copy constructor to solve the problem. \mk{13}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}


%% ── S5-Q11 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- dyna class --- constructor, neg(), copy constructor}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <cstdlib>
using namespace std;

class dyna {
    int *p;
public:
    dyna(int i) { p=(int*)malloc(sizeof(int)); *p=i; }

    // Deep copy constructor
    dyna(const dyna& obj) {
        p=(int*)malloc(sizeof(int)); *p=*obj.p;
    }
    ~dyna() { free(p); }
    int get() { return *p; }
};

dyna neg(dyna ob) { return dyna(-ob.get()); }

int main() {
    dyna ob(10);
    cout << neg(ob).get() << "\n";  // -10
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Problem without copy constructor:} \texttt{neg(dyna ob)}
takes by value --- shallow copy shares \texttt{p}.  Parameter's
destructor frees it; original \texttt{ob} has dangling pointer.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is a copy constructor? What types of problems are solved by using copy constructors? Explain with examples. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2008--09}



\item What type of problems are solved by using copy constructors? Explain with examples. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% ── S5-Q2 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cE!4,colframe=cE,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Problems solved by copy constructors}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cE]

\textbf{Problem --- Shallow Copy with Dynamic Memory:}

The compiler-generated default copy constructor performs a
\emph{shallow copy}: for pointer members it copies the address, not
the pointed-to data.  Both the original and the copy then point to
the \textbf{same heap block}, causing:
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Double-free crash:} both destructors free the same memory.
  \item \textbf{Dangling pointer:} after one is destroyed, the other
    holds an invalid pointer.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Solution --- deep copy constructor:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class MyData {
    int *p;
public:
    MyData(int val) { p = new int; *p = val; }

    // Deep copy: allocates its own memory
    MyData(const MyData& obj) {
        p = new int;
        *p = *obj.p;
    }
    ~MyData() { delete p; }
    int get() const { return *p; }
};

void show(MyData d) { cout << d.get() << "\n"; }

int main() {
    MyData a(42);
    show(a);         // deep copy; a is unaffected
    cout << a.get(); // still 42
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\end{enumerate}
\newpage

\label{sec:S6}
\titleformat{\section}{\large\bfseries\color{cF}}{}{0em}{}[\titlerule]
\section{S6 --- Operator Overloading \& Type Conversion}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Differentiate between early binding and late binding. Demonstrate \texttt{const\_cast} in case of \texttt{const} method with example. \mk{4+4}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2023--24}

%% ── S6-Q25 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- const\_cast with const method example}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;
class Counter {
    int count;
public:
    Counter(int n): count(n){}
    void show() const { cout<<"Count="<<count<<"\n"; }
    void increment() { count++; }
    void forceIncrement() const {
        Counter* nc=const_cast<Counter*>(this); // remove const
        nc->increment();                         // now allowed
    }
};
int main(){
    Counter c(5);
    c.show();           // Count=5
    c.forceIncrement();
    c.show();           // Count=6
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Warning:} Using \texttt{const\_cast} on a truly
\texttt{const} object (declared \texttt{const}) is undefined
behaviour.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Consider the following class declarations:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Coord {
    int x, y;
public:
    //-----------------------
    //-----------------------
};
\end{lstlisting}

Create a \texttt{Rectangle} class that uses the \texttt{Coord} class as follows:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Rectangle {
    Coord p1, p2, p3, p4;
    char *name;
public:
    //-----------------------
    //-----------------------
};
\end{lstlisting}

Answer the following questions and write down methods as instructed below:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Write down a constructor of \texttt{Coord} class accepts only positive values of $x$ and $y$ with no single $x$- or $y$-coordinate larger than 200.
    \item Write down a constructor of \texttt{Rectangle} class that accepts four points and checks whether they form a rectangle or not. A rectangle has right angles at each of its corner (and checking of any three corners are sufficient). Write a \texttt{static} function \texttt{isRightAngle()} that accepts three points and checks whether a right angle is formed at the middle point or not?
    \item Write down four member functions of \texttt{Rectangle} class for computing the length, the width, the perimeter and the area of the rectangle. The length is the larger of the two dimensions. Take care to reuse methods where possible.
    \item Include a predicate function \texttt{square} that determines whether the rectangle is a square or not.
    \item Write down a clone constructor that makes a clone of rectangle.
    \item Write down necessary conversion methods for executing the following program codes:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int value = rect; //returns the area of rect
string name = rect;
\end{lstlisting}
    Where ``rect'' is a rectangle type object.
    \item Briefly state the problem while executing the following code-
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
r1 = r2;
\end{lstlisting}
    Where both \texttt{r1} and \texttt{r2} are rectangle type object. Overwrite the assignment operator to overcome the problem.
\end{enumerate} \mk{35}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2023--24}

%% -- Q ------------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Coord and Rectangle Classes}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <cstring>
#include <cmath>
#include <stdexcept>
#include <string>
using namespace std;

class Coord {
    int x, y;
public:
    // (i) Constructor validating positive values <= 200
    Coord(int x = 1, int y = 1) {
        if (x <= 0 || y <= 0 || x > 200 || y > 200)
            throw invalid_argument("Coordinates must be positive and <= 200");
        this->x = x;
        this->y = y;
    }
    
    // Getters needed since x and y are private by default
    int getX() const { return x; }
    int getY() const { return y; }
};

class Rectangle {
    Coord p1, p2, p3, p4;
    char* name;

public:
    // (ii) static function to check right angle at p2 (middle point)
    static bool isRightAngle(const Coord& pt1, const Coord& pt2, const Coord& pt3) {
        int ax = pt1.getX() - pt2.getX();
        int ay = pt1.getY() - pt2.getY();
        int bx = pt3.getX() - pt2.getX();
        int by = pt3.getY() - pt2.getY();
        return (ax * bx + ay * by) == 0;
    }

    // (ii) Constructor verifying rectangle properties
    Rectangle(Coord a, Coord b, Coord c, Coord d, const char* n)
        : p1(a), p2(b), p3(c), p4(d) {
        // Checking any three corners is sufficient
        if (!isRightAngle(p4, p1, p2) ||
            !isRightAngle(p1, p2, p3) ||
            !isRightAngle(p2, p3, p4)) {
            throw invalid_argument("Points do not form a rectangle");
        }
        name = new char[strlen(n) + 1];
        strcpy(name, n);
    }

    // (v) Clone constructor (Copy Constructor)
    Rectangle(const Rectangle& r)
        : p1(r.p1), p2(r.p2), p3(r.p3), p4(r.p4) {
        name = new char[strlen(r.name) + 1];
        strcpy(name, r.name);
    }

    ~Rectangle() { delete[] name; }

    // Helper method to compute distance
    double dist(const Coord& a, const Coord& b) const {
        return sqrt(pow(a.getX() - b.getX(), 2) + pow(a.getY() - b.getY(), 2));
    }

    // (iii) Length, width, perimeter, area
    double length() const {
        double d1 = dist(p1, p2);
        double d2 = dist(p2, p3);
        return (d1 > d2) ? d1 : d2; // Length is the larger dimension
    }
    
    double width() const {
        double d1 = dist(p1, p2);
        double d2 = dist(p2, p3);
        return (d1 < d2) ? d1 : d2; 
    }
    
    double perimeter() const { return 2 * (length() + width()); }
    
    double area() const { return length() * width(); }

    // (iv) Square predicate
    bool square() const { return abs(length() - width()) < 1e-9; }

    // (vi) Conversion: int value = rect -> returns area
    operator int() const { return static_cast<int>(area()); }

    // (vi) Conversion: string name = rect -> returns name
    operator string() const { return string(name); }

    // (vii) Problem statement:
    // The default assignment operator (r1 = r2) performs a shallow copy.
    // This causes the `name` pointers of both objects to point to the 
    // same dynamically allocated memory. When the objects are destroyed, 
    // the destructor calls delete[] on the same memory twice, causing 
    // a double-free error and potential runtime crashes.
    
    // Overloaded assignment operator to overcome the problem
    Rectangle& operator=(const Rectangle& r) {
        if (this == &r) return *this; // Self-assignment check
        
        // Copy coordinates
        p1 = r.p1; p2 = r.p2; p3 = r.p3; p4 = r.p4;
        
        // Deep copy of dynamically allocated array
        delete[] name;
        name = new char[strlen(r.name) + 1];
        strcpy(name, r.name);
        
        return *this;
    }
};
\end{lstlisting}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item If you overload the assignment operator, should you return an object of the same type as the class it is overloaded for? Justify your answer. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23}


%% ── S6-Q3 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Should overloaded = return same type?}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\textbf{Yes} --- it should return \texttt{ClassName\&} (reference to
same class) to support \textbf{chained assignment} (\texttt{a=b=c})
and to mirror the built-in behaviour.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
MyClass& operator=(const MyClass& rhs) {
    if (this != &rhs) { /* deep copy */ }
    return *this;   // enables: a = b = c
}
\end{lstlisting}

Returning \texttt{void} breaks chaining; returning by value is
inefficient and inconsistent with built-in types.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Write a program to demonstrate a user-defined conversion function that converts a class object to a built-in data type. \mk{9}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23}


\item Consider the following \texttt{Coordinator} class. You are not allowed to modify the given code. Add code to produce output: \texttt{x coordinate: 7} and \texttt{y coordinate: 10}.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;
class Coordinator {
    int x, y;
public:
    Coordinator(int x_val = 0, int y_val = 0) { x = x_val; y = y_val; }
    int getX() { return x; }
    int getY() { return y; }
    // add your code here
};
int main() {
    Coordinator cObj(5, 8);
    cObj += 2;
    cout << "x coordinate : " << cObj.getX() << endl;
    cout << "y coordinate : " << cObj.getY() << endl;
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Coordinator += to produce x=7, y=10}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

Add the following inside the \texttt{Coordinator} class:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
Coordinator& operator+=(int val) {
    x += val;
    y += val;
    return *this;
}
\end{lstlisting}

x coordinate : 7
y coordinate : 10
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Evaluate the output of the following program: \mk{6}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class circle {
    int r;
public:
    circle(int a = 0) { 
        r = a; 
    }
    void show() { 
        cout << r << endl; 
    }
    friend circle operator++(circle &ob);
};

circle operator++(circle &ob) { 
    ob.r++; 
    return ob; 
}

int main() {
    circle c1(10);
    ++c1;
    c1.show();
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22}

%% ── S6-Q17 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Output of circle ++ program}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\textbf{Output:}
\begin{lstlisting}
11
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Explanation / Trace:}
\begin{enumerate}
    \item \texttt{circle c1(10);} instantiates an object \texttt{c1} with its member variable \texttt{r} initialized to \texttt{10} via the parameterized constructor.
    \item \texttt{++c1;} invokes the overloaded prefix increment operator function \texttt{operator++(circle \&ob)}.
    \item Because the argument is passed by reference (\texttt{circle \&ob}), the parameter \texttt{ob} acts as an alias for \texttt{c1}. The statement \texttt{ob.r++;} increments the value of \texttt{r} directly inside \texttt{c1} from \texttt{10} to \texttt{11}.
    \item Finally, \texttt{c1.show();} prints the current value of \texttt{r}, which outputs \texttt{11} to the console followed by a newline.
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Let $xi+yj+zk$ be a vector where $x$, $y$ and $z$ are real numbers as well as $i$, $j$ and $k$ represent unique vectors along x-axis, y-axis and z-axis. There is a description for each point of the vector and hence each object has a pointer to point the description. Declare a class named ``Vector'' that has members and/or friends to fulfill the following requirements. Don't use friend function if it can be implemented using member function. \hfill \textbf{(7$\times$4=28)}

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}
\begin{tabular}{|p{0.2\textwidth}|p{0.25\textwidth}|p{0.45\textwidth}|}
\hline
Requirement & Sample Code & Explanation \\
\hline
Creating instance & \texttt{Vector ob(12.0, 5.0, 2.0), ob1(3.0, 2.0, 5.0, "The peak point"), ob2;} & Use default arguments to set the vector point at the origin with null description when no parameter is given. Write appropriate constructor and copy constructor. \\
\hline
Addition of two complex number objects & \texttt{ob1 = ob2 + ob3;} & The values in each dimension will be added separately, i.e., the x values of ob2 and ob3 will be added together to give x value of ob1 and so on. Descriptions of ob2 and ob3 will be concatenated by adding a space between them to give the description of ob1. \\
\hline
Incrementing a complex number & \texttt{ob = ob1 += ob2;} & Overwrite \texttt{=} and \texttt{+=} operators. \\
\hline
Adding a real number to an object & \texttt{ob2 = ob1 + 100.0;} \newline \texttt{ob2 = 100.0 + ob1;} & The real number will be added to each value of the object, i.e., each value of x, y and z will be incremented by the amount of the real number. \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

%% -- Q ------------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Vector Class: Constructors, Overloaded Operators (+, +=, =)}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <cstring>
using namespace std;

class Vector {
    double x, y, z;
    char* desc;
public:
    // 1. Constructor with default arguments
    Vector(double x = 0.0, double y = 0.0, double z = 0.0, const char* d = NULL) 
        : x(x), y(y), z(z) {
        if (d) {
            desc = new char[strlen(d) + 1];
            strcpy(desc, d);
        } else {
            desc = NULL;
        }
    }

    // 1. Copy Constructor
    Vector(const Vector& other) : x(other.x), y(other.y), z(other.z) {
        if (other.desc) {
            desc = new char[strlen(other.desc) + 1];
            strcpy(desc, other.desc);
        } else {
            desc = NULL;
        }
    }

    // Destructor to free dynamically allocated memory
    ~Vector() {
        delete[] desc;
    }

    // 3. Overwrite = operator (Member function)
    Vector& operator=(const Vector& other) {
        if (this != &other) {
            x = other.x; y = other.y; z = other.z;
            delete[] desc;
            if (other.desc) {
                desc = new char[strlen(other.desc) + 1];
                strcpy(desc, other.desc);
            } else {
                desc = NULL;
            }
        }
        return *this;
    }

    // 2. Addition of two Vector objects (Member function)
    Vector operator+(const Vector& b) const {
        Vector res(x + b.x, y + b.y, z + b.z);
        if (desc && b.desc) {
            res.desc = new char[strlen(desc) + strlen(b.desc) + 2];
            strcpy(res.desc, desc);
            strcat(res.desc, " ");
            strcat(res.desc, b.desc);
        } else if (desc) {
            res.desc = new char[strlen(desc) + 1];
            strcpy(res.desc, desc);
        } else if (b.desc) {
            res.desc = new char[strlen(b.desc) + 1];
            strcpy(res.desc, b.desc);
        }
        return res;
    }

    // 3. Overwrite += operator (Member function)
    Vector& operator+=(const Vector& other) {
        *this = *this + other; // Reuse the overloaded + and = operators
        return *this;
    }

    // 4. Adding a real number: Vector + real (Member function)
    Vector operator+(double r) const {
        return Vector(x + r, y + r, z + r, desc);
    }

    // 4. Adding a real number: real + Vector (MUST be a friend function)
    friend Vector operator+(double r, const Vector& v) {
        return Vector(r + v.x, r + v.y, r + v.z, v.desc);
    }

    void print() const {
        cout << x << "i + " << y << "j + " << z << "k";
        if (desc) cout << " [" << desc << "]";
        cout << endl;
    }
};

int main() {
    Vector ob(12.0, 5.0, 2.0);
    Vector ob1(3.0, 2.0, 5.0, "The peak point");
    Vector ob2;
    
    ob2 = ob + ob1;
    cout << "ob + ob1: "; ob2.print(); 

    Vector ob3(1.0, 1.0, 1.0, "Base");
    Vector ob_assigned = ob3 += ob1;
    cout << "ob3 after +=: "; ob3.print();
    cout << "ob_assigned: "; ob_assigned.print();

    Vector ob4 = ob1 + 100.0;
    cout << "ob1 + 100.0: "; ob4.print();

    Vector ob5 = 100.0 + ob1;
    cout << "100.0 + ob1: "; ob5.print();

    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item If you overload \texttt{operator+}, must you return an object of the same type as your parameters? Justify your answer. \mk{7}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}


%% ── S6-Q2 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Must operator+ return same type as parameters?}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\textbf{No.} C++ imposes no restriction on the return type of
overloaded operators.  The overloaded operator is just a function;
its return type can be anything logical.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Distance { double d; public: Distance(double v):d(v){} void show(){cout<<d;} };
class Point {
    int x, y;
public:
    Point(int a,int b):x(a),y(b){}
    // operator+ returns Distance, not Point
    Distance operator+(const Point& p) const {
        double dx=x-p.x, dy=y-p.y;
        return Distance(sqrt(dx*dx+dy*dy));
    }
};
\end{lstlisting}

In practice, returning the same type enables chaining
(\texttt{a+b+c}), but it is not mandatory.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item \texttt{"There are scenarios when a friend function is very useful for operator overloading"} --- support this statement for the following class \texttt{Rational} by writing a program that must use friend function for operator overloading:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Rational {
    int n, d;
public:
    Rational() { n = 0; d = 0; }
    Rational(int a, int b) { n = a; d = b; }
};
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}


%% ── S6-Q13 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Rational class with friend operator overloading}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Rational {
    int n, d;
public:
    Rational(int a=0, int b=1): n(a), d(b){}

    friend Rational operator+(const Rational& a, const Rational& b){
        return Rational(a.n*b.d + b.n*a.d, a.d*b.d);
    }
    friend Rational operator*(const Rational& a, const Rational& b){
        return Rational(a.n*b.n, a.d*b.d);
    }
    friend ostream& operator<<(ostream& os, const Rational& r){
        os<<r.n<<"/"<<r.d; return os;
    }
};

int main() {
    Rational r(1,2), s(1,3);
    cout << r+s << "\n";  // 5/6
    cout << r*s << "\n";  // 1/6
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Given a \texttt{Date} class with \texttt{day}, \texttt{month}, \texttt{year}: overload the binary minus operator so that \texttt{ob1 - ob2} gives the difference in number of days (assume 30 days/month). Also overload \texttt{<<} to print \texttt{day-month-year}. Include a \texttt{main()} function.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Date {
    int day, month, year;
public:
    Date(int m, int d, int y) { day = d; month = m; year = y; }
};
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{17}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}


%% ── S6-Q18 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Date class --- binary - and << operators}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <cmath>
using namespace std;
class Date {
    int day, month, year;
public:
    Date(int m, int d, int y): day(d), month(m), year(y){}
    int operator-(const Date& o) const {
        int t1=year*360+month*30+day;
        int t2=o.year*360+o.month*30+o.day;
        return abs(t1-t2);
    }
    friend ostream& operator<<(ostream& os, const Date& d){
        os<<d.day<<"-"<<d.month<<"-"<<d.year; return os;
    }
};
int main(){
    Date d1(3,15,2024), d2(1,1,2024);
    cout<<d1<<"\n";              // 15-3-2024
    cout<<(d1-d2)<<" days\n";   // 74 days
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Consider the following \texttt{Photo} and \texttt{Frame} classes. Add necessary code to \texttt{Photo} and/or \texttt{Frame} class (without modifying existing code) so that \texttt{F = P + 2;} produces \texttt{Frame Width: 7} and \texttt{Frame Length: 10} when \texttt{P} is \texttt{Photo(5,8)}:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Photo { int width, length; public:
    Photo() {width=0; length=0;}
    Photo(int a, int b) {width=a; length=b;}
    int getwidth() {return width;} int getlength() {return length;}
};
class Frame { int width, length; public:
    Frame() {width=0; length=0;}
    Frame(int a, int b) {width=a; length=b;}
    int getwidth() {return width;} int getlength() {return length;}
};
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{20}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}


%% ── S6-Q29 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Photo + 2 = Frame(7,10)}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;
class Frame{
    int width,length;
public:
    Frame(int a=0,int b=0):width(a),length(b){}
    int getwidth(){return width;} int getlength(){return length;}
};
class Photo{
    int width,length;
public:
    Photo(int a=0,int b=0):width(a),length(b){}
    int getwidth(){return width;} int getlength(){return length;}
    // P+n returns Frame with each dim increased by n
    Frame operator+(int n){ return Frame(width+n,length+n); }
};
int main(){
    Photo P(5,8);
    Frame F=P+2;
    cout<<"Frame Width: " <<F.getwidth() <<"\n"; // 7
    cout<<"Frame Length: "<<F.getlength()<<"\n"; // 10
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Consider the following \texttt{Fraction} class. Overload the prefix \texttt{++} operator using a friend function so that \texttt{++n} on \texttt{Fraction(3,7)} produces \texttt{x/y: 4/8}. Only write the overloaded function:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Fraction { int x, y; public:
    Fraction(int a, int b) {x=a; y=b;}
    int getx() {return x;} int gety() {return y;}
};
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{13}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}

%% ── S6-Q30 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Fraction prefix ++ as friend}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;
class Fraction{
    int x,y;
public:
    Fraction(int a,int b):x(a),y(b){}
    int getx(){return x;} int gety(){return y;}
    friend Fraction& operator++(Fraction& f){ f.x++; f.y++; return f; }
};
int main(){
    Fraction n(3,7);
    ++n;
    cout<<"x/y: "<<n.getx()<<"/"<<n.gety()<<"\n"; // x/y: 4/8
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Why should the overloaded assignment operator (\texttt{=}) function return the \texttt{this} pointer? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18; CSE 201 | 2009--10}

%% ── S6-Q4 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Why operator= should return *this}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

Returning \texttt{*this} enables \textbf{chained assignments}
like \texttt{a = b = c}, parsed as \texttt{a = (b = c)}.
\texttt{b = c} must return a reference to \texttt{b} so it can
be assigned to \texttt{a}.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class MyClass {
    int val;
public:
    MyClass(int v=0): val(v){}
    MyClass& operator=(const MyClass& rhs) {
        val = rhs.val;
        return *this;    // enables: a = b = c
    }
};
int main() {
    MyClass a(1), b(2), c(3);
    a = b = c;    // works correctly
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Which of the operators can have default arguments when they are overloaded? \mk{3}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}


%% ── S6-Q5 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
