  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Which operators can have default arguments?}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

Only \textbf{\texttt{operator()}} (the function call operator) can
have default arguments.  All other overloaded operators cannot,
because their arity is fixed by the language.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Multiplier {
    int factor;
public:
    Multiplier(int f=1): factor(f){}
    int operator()(int x, int step=1) const {
        return x * factor * step;
    }
};
Multiplier m(3);
cout << m(5)    << "\n";  // 15  (step defaults to 1)
cout << m(5, 2) << "\n";  // 30
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Consider the class \texttt{Complex}. Write necessary functions so that the following statements execute correctly in \texttt{main}:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Complex {
public:
    int real, imag;
    Complex(int r = 0, int i = 0);
};
\end{lstlisting}
(i) \texttt{comp3 = comp1 + comp2;} \quad (ii) \texttt{comp3 = comp1 + 10;} \quad (iii) \texttt{comp3 = 10 + comp1;} \quad (iv) \texttt{comp1++;} \quad (v) \texttt{++comp1;} \quad (vi) \texttt{cout << comp1;} \quad (vii) \texttt{comp3 = comp1(5);}
\mk{25}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}


%% ── S6-Q16 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Complex class --- all 7 operations including comp1(5)}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;
class Complex {
public:
    int real, imag;
    Complex(int r=0, int i=0): real(r), imag(i){}
    Complex operator+(const Complex& o) const { return Complex(real+o.real,imag+o.imag); }
    Complex operator+(int n)            const { return Complex(real+n, imag); }
    friend Complex operator+(int n, const Complex& c){ return Complex(c.real+n,c.imag); }
    Complex& operator++()    { real++; imag++; return *this; }
    Complex  operator++(int) { Complex old=*this; real++; imag++; return old; }
    friend ostream& operator<<(ostream& os, const Complex& c){
        os<<c.real<<"+"<<c.imag<<"i"; return os;
    }
    Complex operator()(int n) const { return Complex(real*n, imag*n); }
};
int main(){
    Complex c1(3,4), c2(1,2), c3;
    c3=c1+c2;   cout<<c3<<"\n"; // 4+6i
    c3=c1+10;   cout<<c3<<"\n"; // 13+4i
    c3=10+c1;   cout<<c3<<"\n"; // 13+4i
    c1++;       cout<<c1<<"\n"; // 4+5i
    ++c1;       cout<<c1<<"\n"; // 5+6i
    c3=c1(5);   cout<<c3<<"\n"; // 25+30i
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is wrong with the following code segment?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class myclass {
    int i, j;
public:
    static void seti(int x) { this->i = x; }
    int geti() { return i; }
};
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{6}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}


%% ── S6-Q21 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Problem with this in static method}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\textbf{Problem:} \texttt{static void seti(int x)\{ this->i = x; \}}
--- a \textbf{static} member function has no \texttt{this} pointer.
This is a \textbf{compile error}.

\textbf{Fix:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
// Remove static -- make it non-static
void seti(int x) { i = x; }   // has 'this'
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Consider the following \texttt{MyInt} class. Overload: (i) \texttt{+} for adding two \texttt{MyInt} objects, (ii) \texttt{*} so a \texttt{MyInt} can be multiplied by an integer from either side, (iii) \texttt{=} for safe assignment. Note: you cannot write a copy constructor.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class MyInt {
    int *x;
public:
    MyInt(int argx) { x = new int; *x = argx; }
    int getX() const { return *x; }
    void setX(int argx) { *x = argx; }
    ~MyInt() { delete x; }
};
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{20}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17}


%% ── S6-Q20 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- MyInt --- overload +, * (both sides), =}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class MyInt {
    int *x;
public:
    MyInt(int argx){ x=new int; *x=argx; }
    int getX() const { return *x; }
    void setX(int argx){ *x=argx; }
    ~MyInt(){ delete x; }

    MyInt operator+(const MyInt& o) const { return MyInt(*x+*o.x); }
    MyInt operator*(int n)          const { return MyInt(*x*n); }
    friend MyInt operator*(int n, const MyInt& m){ return MyInt(n**m.x); }
    MyInt& operator=(const MyInt& o){ if(this!=&o) *x=*o.x; return *this; }
};
int main(){
    MyInt m1(5), m2(3);
    cout<<(m1+m2).getX()<<"\n"; // 8
    cout<<(m1*4).getX()  <<"\n"; // 20
    cout<<(3*m2).getX()  <<"\n"; // 9
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item How can you code \texttt{++ob1} and \texttt{ob1++} differently? \mk{3}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}

%% ── S6-Q6 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- How to code ++ob (prefix) and ob++ (postfix) differently}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

The dummy \texttt{int} parameter distinguishes postfix from prefix:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Counter {
    int val;
public:
    Counter(int v=0): val(v){}
    int get() const { return val; }

    // Prefix: ++ob -- increment then return new value
    Counter& operator++() { ++val; return *this; }

    // Postfix: ob++ -- save old, increment, return old
    Counter operator++(int) {
        Counter old=*this; ++val; return old;
    }
};
int main() {
    Counter c(5);
    Counter a=++c;  // c=6, a=6
    Counter b=c++;  // b=6, c=7
    cout<<a.get()<<" "<<b.get()<<" "<<c.get()<<"\n"; // 6 6 7
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Let $V = (x, y, z)$ be a vector. Define a class and include the minimum necessary members for solving: \texttt{v1 += (10 + v2);} \mk{12}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2015--16}


%% ── S6-Q14 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Vector class for v1 += (10 + v2)}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Vec {
    double x, y, z;
public:
    Vec(double a=0,double b=0,double c=0): x(a),y(b),z(c){}

    Vec& operator+=(const Vec& v){ x+=v.x; y+=v.y; z+=v.z; return *this; }

    friend Vec operator+(double n, const Vec& v){
        return Vec(v.x+n, v.y+n, v.z+n);
    }
    void print(){ cout<<"("<<x<<","<<y<<","<<z<<")\n"; }
};

int main(){
    Vec v1(1,2,3), v2(4,5,6);
    v1 += (10+v2);   // 10+v2=(14,15,16); v1+=(14,15,16)
    v1.print();      // (15,17,19)
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{tcolorbox}


\item Explain different types of casting used in C++ with examples. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2014--15; CSE 107 | 2015--16}

%% ── S6-Q24 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Four C++ casting types with examples}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{\texttt{static\_cast<T>}:} Compile-time, related
    types.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int i = static_cast<int>(3.7);   // i=3
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{\texttt{dynamic\_cast<T>}:} Safe run-time
    polymorphic downcast.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
Base* b=new Derived();
Derived* d=dynamic_cast<Derived*>(b); // null if fails
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{\texttt{const\_cast<T>}:} Adds/removes
    \texttt{const}.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
const int x=10;
int* p=const_cast<int*>(&x);
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{\texttt{reinterpret\_cast<T>}:} Low-level
    bit-reinterpretation.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
int* p=new int(42);
long addr=reinterpret_cast<long>(p);
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Why is RTTI a necessary feature of C++? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}


%% ── S6-Q26 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Why is RTTI necessary in C++?}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

RTTI (Run-Time Type Information) allows determining the actual type
of an object at run time through a base class pointer.

\textbf{Necessity:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Safe downcasting with \texttt{dynamic\_cast}:}
    verifies the cast at run time; returns \texttt{nullptr} on
    failure --- prevents corrupt memory access.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
Base* b=new Derived();
Derived* d=dynamic_cast<Derived*>(b);
if(d) d->derivedMethod();
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{\texttt{typeid} operator:} queries exact type.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
cout<<typeid(*b).name()<<"\n";  // "Derived"
\end{lstlisting}

  \item Enables correct type-specific operations in complex
    polymorphic hierarchies without unsafe C-style casts.
\end{enumerate}

Requires at least one virtual function (vtable carries type info).

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Consider the following class declaration:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Coord {
    int x, y;
    char *pointname;
public:
    // your code
};
\end{lstlisting}
(a) Create necessary constructors to support: \texttt{Coord ob1, ob2, ob3;} and \texttt{Coord ob4(0, 0, "origin");}
(b) Explain why a copy constructor works for initialization but not for assignment. Create the required copy constructor.
(c) Create necessary methods for: \texttt{ob1 = ob2 + ob3 + ob4;} and \texttt{ob1 = 100 + ob2;} (increases both x and y by 100) and \texttt{int value = 100 + ob4;} (value = $100 + \sqrt{ob4.x^2 + ob4.y^2}$, use a conversion function).
(d) How can you code \texttt{++ob1} and \texttt{ob1++} differently?
\mk{6+8+18+3}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}

%% -- Q ------------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Coord Class: Constructors, Operators, Conversion}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <cstring>
#include <cmath>
using namespace std;

class Coord {
    int   x, y;
    char* pointname;
public:
    // (a) Default constructor
    Coord() : x(0), y(0) {
        pointname = new char[8];
        strcpy(pointname, "unnamed");
    }

    // (a) Parameterized constructor
    Coord(int x, int y, const char* name) : x(x), y(y) {
        pointname = new char[strlen(name)+1];
        strcpy(pointname, name);
    }

    // (b) Copy constructor -- deep copy
    Coord(const Coord& other) : x(other.x), y(other.y) {
        pointname = new char[strlen(other.pointname)+1];
        strcpy(pointname, other.pointname);
    }

    ~Coord() { delete[] pointname; }

    // Assignment operator
    Coord& operator=(const Coord& other) {
        if (this == &other) return *this;
        delete[] pointname;
        x = other.x; y = other.y;
        pointname = new char[strlen(other.pointname)+1];
        strcpy(pointname, other.pointname);
        return *this;
    }

    // (c) operator+ : add two Coords
    Coord operator+(const Coord& other) const {
        return Coord(x + other.x, y + other.y, "result");
    }

    // (c) friend: 100 + ob2 -- adds 100 to both x and y
    friend Coord operator+(int val, const Coord& c) {
        return Coord(val + c.x, val + c.y, "result");
    }

    // (c) Conversion function: int value = 100 + ob4
    // ob4 converts to double (magnitude), then 100 + magnitude
    operator double() const {
        return sqrt((double)x*x + (double)y*y);
    }

    // (d) prefix ++ob1
    Coord& operator++() {
        ++x; ++y;
        return *this;
    }

    // (d) postfix ob1++
    Coord operator++(int) {
        Coord tmp(*this);
        x++; y++;
        return tmp;
    }

    void print() const {
        cout << pointname << "(" << x << "," << y << ")" << endl;
    }
};

int main() {
    Coord ob1, ob2, ob3;
    Coord ob4(0, 0, "origin");

    // (c) ob1 = ob2 + ob3 + ob4
    ob1 = ob2 + ob3 + ob4;
    ob1.print();

    // (c) ob1 = 100 + ob2
    ob1 = 100 + ob2;
    ob1.print();

    // (c) int value = 100 + ob4 (uses conversion)
    int value = (int)(100 + (double)ob4);
    cout << "value = " << value << endl;

    // (d) prefix and postfix
    ++ob1;
    ob1++;
    ob1.print();
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{(b) Why copy constructor works for init but not assignment:}

The copy constructor is called only during \textbf{initialization}
(e.g., \texttt{Coord c2 = c1}). For \textbf{assignment}
(\texttt{c2 = c1} after both are constructed), the
\texttt{operator=} is called instead. Without a custom
\texttt{operator=}, the default does a shallow copy of the
\texttt{char*} pointer, causing double-free bugs --- hence the
explicit \texttt{operator=} above.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Let $x+iy$ be a complex number. Declare a class named \texttt{Complex} with members/friends for:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Creating instance: \texttt{Complex ob(12.0, 5.0);}
  \item Addition: \texttt{ob1 = ob2 + ob3;}
  \item Increment: \texttt{ob++;} and \texttt{++ob;}
  \item Adding real number: \texttt{ob2 = ob1 + 100.0;} and \texttt{ob2 = 100.0 + ob1;}
\end{enumerate}
\mk{20}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2014--15}


%% ── S6-Q15 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Complex class (constructor, +, ++, +double both sides)}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;
class Complex {
    double re, im;
public:
    Complex(double r=0, double i=0): re(r), im(i){}
    Complex operator+(const Complex& o) const { return Complex(re+o.re,im+o.im); }
    Complex operator+(double n) const { return Complex(re+n, im); }
    friend Complex operator+(double n, const Complex& c){ return Complex(c.re+n,c.im); }
    Complex& operator++()    { re++; im++; return *this; }
    Complex  operator++(int) { Complex old=*this; re++; im++; return old; }
    void show() const { cout<<re<<"+"<<im<<"i\n"; }
};
int main(){
    Complex ob1(12,5), ob2, ob3(1,1);
    ob2=ob1+ob3;    ob2.show();      // 13+6i
    ob1++;          ob1.show();      // 13+6i
    ++ob1;          ob1.show();      // 14+7i
    ob2=ob1+100.0;  ob2.show();      // 114+7i
    ob2=100.0+ob1;  ob2.show();      // 114+7i
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Consider the following class:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class coord {
    int x, y;
public:
    coord(int i = 0, int j = 0) { x = i; y = j; }
    void getxy(int &i, int &j) { i = x; j = y; }
};
\end{lstlisting}
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Write a function to overload \texttt{+} operator where both x and y are incremented by the amount passed through parameter.
  \item Write a function that allows: \texttt{ob2 = 100 + ob1;}
  \item Write inserter and extractor functions.
  \item Rewrite the class definition incorporating the above four functions.
\end{enumerate}
\mk{20}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}


%% ── S6-Q7 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- coord class full overloading}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class coord {
    int x, y;
public:
    coord(int i=0, int j=0): x(i), y(j){}
    void getxy(int &i, int &j) const { i=x; j=y; }

    // (a) ob + int
    coord operator+(int i) const { return coord(x+i, y+i); }

    // (b) int + ob  (friend)
    friend coord operator+(int i, const coord& ob) {
        return coord(ob.x+i, ob.y+i);
    }

    // (c) inserter
    friend ostream& operator<<(ostream& os, const coord& c) {
        os<<"("<<c.x<<","<<c.y<<")"; return os;
    }
    // (c) extractor
    friend istream& operator>>(istream& is, coord& c) {
        is>>c.x>>c.y; return is;
    }
};

int main() {
    coord ob1(2,4);
    cout << ob1+5  << "\n";   // (7,9)
    cout << 100+ob1<< "\n";   // (102,104)
    cout << ob1    << "\n";   // (2,4)
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Consider the following \texttt{coord} class. Make necessary changes and overload the \texttt{+} operator so that it accepts both \texttt{ob + int} and \texttt{int + ob} without using unnecessary friend functions:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class coord {
    int x, y;
public:
    coord(int i = 0, int j = 0) { x = i; y = j; }
    void getxy(int &i, int &j) { i = x; j = y; }
};
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% ── S6-Q8 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- coord ob+int and int+ob without unnecessary friend}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

Since \texttt{coord(int i=0, int j=0)} is a non-explicit conversion
constructor, the compiler implicitly converts an \texttt{int} to a
\texttt{coord}.  A single member \texttt{operator+(coord)} handles
all cases:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class coord {
    int x, y;
public:
    coord(int i=0, int j=0): x(i), y(j){}
    void getxy(int &i, int &j) const { i=x; j=y; }

    coord operator+(const coord& ob2) const {
        return coord(x+ob2.x, y+ob2.y);
    }
};
int main() {
    coord ob1(2,4), ob2(1,1);
    coord r1 = ob1 + ob2;  // coord+coord
    coord r2 = ob1 + 5;    // 5 converted to coord(5,5)
    coord r3 = 3  + ob1;   // 3 converted to coord(3,3)
    // r1=(3,5), r2=(7,9), r3=(5,7)
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Consider the following declarations of \texttt{coord} class operators. Explain what happens when each statement is executed and why \texttt{o3 = 100 + o1;} will not work. Write a function to fix it.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class coord {
    int x, y;
public:
    coord(int i = 0, int j = 0) { x = i; y = j; }
    void getxy(int &i, int &j) { i = x; j = y; }
    coord operator+(coord ob2);
    coord operator+(int i);
    coord operator++();
    bool operator==(coord ob2);
    coord operator=(coord ob2);
};
coord o1(2, 4), o2, o3(3, 1);
\end{lstlisting}
(i) \texttt{(o1 + o2).getxy(p, q);} \quad (ii) \texttt{o3 = o3 + o2 + o1;}
\mk{15}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}


%% ── S6-Q9 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- coord operator statements analysis; fix 100+o1}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\textbf{(i) \texttt{(o1+o2).getxy(p,q)}:}
\texttt{o1+o2} calls \texttt{operator+(coord)} $\Rightarrow$
\texttt{coord(2,4)}.  \texttt{getxy} sets \texttt{p=2, q=4}.

\textbf{(ii) \texttt{o3=o3+o2+o1}:}
\texttt{(o3+o2)=coord(3,1)}, then \texttt{+o1=coord(5,5)}.
\texttt{o3} becomes \texttt{(5,5)}.

\textbf{Why \texttt{100+o1} fails:}
\texttt{int::operator+(coord)} does not exist.  The member
\texttt{operator+(int)} only handles \texttt{ob+int}, not
\texttt{int+ob}.

\textbf{Fix:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
friend coord operator+(int i, const coord& ob) {
    return coord(ob.x+i, ob.y+i);
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Overload the \texttt{<} and \texttt{>} operators relative to the \texttt{coord} class. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}


%% ── S6-Q10 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Overload < and > for coord}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
bool operator<(const coord& ob) const {
    return (x < ob.x) && (y < ob.y);
}
bool operator>(const coord& ob) const {
    return (x > ob.x) && (y > ob.y);
}
// coord c1(1,2), c2(3,4);
// c1<c2 -> true; c2>c1 -> true
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Create a function \texttt{reverse()} that takes a pointer to a string and a count parameter. Give count a default value that, when absent, reverses the entire string. \mk{7}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}

%% ── S6-Q28 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- reverse() with default count parameter}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <cstring>
using namespace std;

void reverse(char* s, int count=-1){
    int len=(int)strlen(s);
    if(count<0 || count>len) count=len;
    for(int i=0,j=count-1; i<j; i++,j--) swap(s[i],s[j]);
}

int main(){
    char s1[]="Hello";
    reverse(s1);         cout<<s1<<"\n"; // olleH
    char s2[]="Hello World";
    reverse(s2,5);       cout<<s2<<"\n"; // olleH World
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is an operator function? Describe the syntax of operator overloading. List the restrictions imposed on operator overloading. \mk{5+4}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08, 2009--10}


%% ── S6-Q1 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Operator function, syntax, restrictions}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\textbf{Operator Function:} A function named \texttt{operator@} that
defines the behaviour of operator \texttt{@} for user-defined types.

\textbf{Syntax:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
// Member function:
return_type operator@(params) { ... }
// Friend (non-member):
friend return_type operator@(param1, param2) { ... }
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Restrictions:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item Cannot create new operators; only overload existing ones.
  \item Cannot change arity (number of operands).
  \item Cannot change precedence or associativity.
  \item Cannot overload: \texttt{::}, \texttt{.}, \texttt{.*},
    \texttt{?:}, \texttt{sizeof}, \texttt{typeid}.
  \item \texttt{=}, \texttt{[]}, \texttt{()}, \texttt{->} must be
    \textbf{member functions}.
  \item At least one operand must be a user-defined type.
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is a conversion function? Let a class \texttt{coord} be defined as:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class coord {
    int x, y;
public:
    coord(int i, int j) { x = i; y = j; }
};
\end{lstlisting}
Write a conversion function that will automatically convert an object of \texttt{coord} to an integer. \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}


%% ── S6-Q11 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- User-defined conversion function to built-in type}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <cmath>
using namespace std;

class coord {
    int x, y;
public:
    coord(int i, int j): x(i), y(j){}
    // Conversion: coord -> int (Euclidean magnitude)
    operator int() const { return (int)sqrt((double)(x*x+y*y)); }
};

int main() {
    coord c(3, 4);
    int i = c;           // implicit: 5
    cout << i << "\n";   // 5
    cout << (int)c;      // explicit cast: 5
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Write the necessary operator functions to allow: \texttt{c3 = 10 + c1 + c2;} where \texttt{c1}, \texttt{c2}, and \texttt{c3} are \texttt{coord} objects. \mk{14}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}


%% ── S6-Q12 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- c3 = 10 + c1 + c2 operator functions}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class coord {
    int x, y;
public:
    coord(int i=0, int j=0): x(i), y(j){}
    void getxy(int &i, int &j) const { i=x; j=y; }

    coord operator+(const coord& ob) const {
        return coord(x+ob.x, y+ob.y);
    }
    friend coord operator+(int i, const coord& ob) {
        return coord(ob.x+i, ob.y+i);
    }
};
// c3 = 10+c1+c2:  (10+c1(2,4))=(12,14); +c2(3,1)=(15,15)
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a matrix class with default and parameterized constructors. Overload \texttt{+} for matrix addition (\texttt{m3 = m1 + m2}) and \texttt{*} for matrix multiplication (\texttt{m3 = m1 * m2}). \mk{20}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}


%% ── S6-Q23 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Matrix class with + and * operators}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Matrix {
    int **d; int r, c;
public:
    Matrix(int rows=0, int cols=0): r(rows), c(cols){
        d=new int*[r]; for(int i=0;i<r;i++){d[i]=new int[c]; for(int j=0;j<c;j++) d[i][j]=0;}
    }
    int& at(int i, int j){ return d[i][j]; }
    Matrix operator+(const Matrix& o) const {
        Matrix res(r,c);
        for(int i=0;i<r;i++) for(int j=0;j<c;j++) res.d[i][j]=d[i][j]+o.d[i][j];
        return res;
    }
    Matrix operator*(const Matrix& o) const {
        Matrix res(r,o.c);
        for(int i=0;i<r;i++) for(int j=0;j<o.c;j++) for(int k=0;k<c;k++) res.d[i][j]+=d[i][k]*o.d[k][j];
        return res;
    }
};
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Show with an example how one can find the address of a particular overloaded member function of a class. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}


%% ── S6-Q27 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Address of specific overloaded member function}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;
class MyClass {
public:
    void func(int x)    { cout<<"int: "   <<x  <<"\n"; }
    void func(double x) { cout<<"double: "<<x  <<"\n"; }
};
int main(){
    void (MyClass::*fp1)(int)    = &MyClass::func; // first overload
    void (MyClass::*fp2)(double) = &MyClass::func; // second overload

    MyClass obj;
    (obj.*fp1)(10);    // int: 10
    (obj.*fp2)(3.14);  // double: 3.14
}
\end{lstlisting}

The exact type of the function pointer selects the correct overload.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a class named \texttt{MyString} with the following: (i) \texttt{char* data}, (ii) \texttt{int length}, (iii) default, parameterized, and copy constructors, (iv) \texttt{operator+} for concatenation, (v) \texttt{operator-} to remove all occurrences of substring, (vi) overload \texttt{=} and copy constructor if needed. \mk{22}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2008--09}

%% -- Q ------------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- MyString Class with Operator Overloading}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <cstring>
using namespace std;

class MyString {
    char* data;
    int   length;
public:
    // Default constructor
    MyString() : length(0) {
        data = new char[1];
        data[0] = '\0';
    }

    // Parameterized constructor
    MyString(const char* s) {
        length = strlen(s);
        data   = new char[length + 1];
        strcpy(data, s);
    }

    // Copy constructor
    MyString(const MyString& other) {
        length = other.length;
        data   = new char[length + 1];
        strcpy(data, other.data);
    }

    // Destructor
    ~MyString() { delete[] data; }

    // Assignment operator
    MyString& operator=(const MyString& other) {
        if (this == &other) return *this;
        delete[] data;
        length = other.length;
        data   = new char[length + 1];
        strcpy(data, other.data);
        return *this;
    }

    // operator+ : concatenation
    MyString operator+(const MyString& other) const {
        char* tmp = new char[length + other.length + 1];
        strcpy(tmp, data);
        strcat(tmp, other.data);
        MyString result(tmp);
        delete[] tmp;
        return result;
    }

    // operator- : remove all occurrences of substring
    MyString operator-(const MyString& sub) const {
        char* result = new char[length + 1];
        char* dst    = result;
        const char* src = data;
        int subLen = sub.length;

        while (*src) {
            if (strncmp(src, sub.data, subLen) == 0) {
                src += subLen;   // skip the substring
            } else {
                *dst++ = *src++;
            }
        }
        *dst = '\0';
        MyString r(result);
        delete[] result;
        return r;
    }

    void print() const { cout << data << endl; }
};

int main() {
    MyString s1("Hello ");
    MyString s2("World");
    MyString s3 = s1 + s2;
    s3.print();                  // Hello World

    MyString s4("abcabcabc");
    MyString s5("abc");
    MyString s6 = s4 - s5;
    s6.print();                  // (empty string)

    MyString s7(s3);             // copy constructor
    s7.print();                  // Hello World
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Output:}
\begin{verbatim}
Hello World

Hello World
\end{verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Create a class \texttt{Matrix} (of double values). Include member functions to: (i) create a matrix of size $m \times n$, (ii) print the matrix, (iii) overload the parenthesis operator \texttt{()} as follows:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
Matrix m(10, 20);
m(1, 2) = 4.5;   // access an element
m();              // erase matrix, set all to zero
cout << m(1, 2); // print value at (1,2)
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}


%% ── S6-Q22 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Matrix class with operator()}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;
class Matrix {
    double **data; int rows, cols;
public:
    Matrix(int m, int n): rows(m), cols(n){
        data=new double*[m];
        for(int i=0;i<m;i++){data[i]=new double[n]; for(int j=0;j<n;j++) data[i][j]=0;}
    }
    ~Matrix(){ for(int i=0;i<rows;i++) delete[] data[i]; delete[] data; }
    double& operator()(int i, int j){ return data[i][j]; } // access/set
    void   operator()(){ for(int i=0;i<rows;i++) for(int j=0;j<cols;j++) data[i][j]=0; }
    void print(){ for(int i=0;i<rows;i++){for(int j=0;j<cols;j++) cout<<data[i][j]<<" "; cout<<"\n";} }
};
int main(){
    Matrix m(3,3);
    m(1,2)=4.5; cout<<m(1,2)<<"\n"; // 4.5
    m(); cout<<m(1,2)<<"\n";         // 0
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Write a class to represent a vector. Include functions to: (i) create, (ii) modify an element, (iii) add two vectors, (iv) compute dot product, (v) print. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% -- Q ------------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cF!4,colframe=cF,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Vector Class with Arithmetic Operations}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cF]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Vector {
    float* elem;
    int    size;
public:
    // (i) Create
    Vector(int n) : size(n) {
        elem = new float[n]();
    }

    ~Vector() { delete[] elem; }

    // (ii) Modify an element
    void set(int i, float val) {
        if (i >= 0 && i < size) elem[i] = val;
    }

    float get(int i) const { return elem[i]; }
    int   getSize() const  { return size; }

    // (iii) Add two vectors
    Vector operator+(const Vector& v) const {
        Vector result(size);
        for (int i = 0; i < size; i++)
            result.elem[i] = elem[i] + v.elem[i];
        return result;
    }

    // (iv) Dot product
    float dot(const Vector& v) const {
        float sum = 0;
        for (int i = 0; i < size; i++)
            sum += elem[i] * v.elem[i];
        return sum;
    }

    // (v) Print
    void print() const {
        cout << "[ ";
        for (int i = 0; i < size; i++)
            cout << elem[i] << " ";
        cout << "]" << endl;
    }
};

int main() {
    Vector v1(3), v2(3);
    v1.set(0, 1); v1.set(1, 2); v1.set(2, 3);
    v2.set(0, 4); v2.set(1, 5); v2.set(2, 6);

    cout << "v1 = "; v1.print();
    cout << "v2 = "; v2.print();

    Vector v3 = v1 + v2;
    cout << "v1 + v2 = "; v3.print();

    cout << "v1 . v2 = " << v1.dot(v2) << endl;
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Output:}
\begin{verbatim}
v1 = [ 1 2 3 ]
v2 = [ 4 5 6 ]
v1 + v2 = [ 5 7 9 ]
v1 . v2 = 32
\end{verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}


\end{enumerate}
\newpage

%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
%% S7 — Inheritance & Polymorphism
%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\secdivider{Section S7}{Inheritance \& Polymorphism (C++)}{cG}{cG}
\markboth{S7 --- Inheritance \& Polymorphism (C++)}{}
\label{sec:S7}
\titleformat{\section}{\large\bfseries\color{cG}}{}{0em}{}[\titlerule]
\section{S7 --- Inheritance \& Polymorphism (C++)}

\begin{enumerate}
\item What is the diamond problem? State with example how the diamond problem can be solved using virtual inheritance. Differentiate between virtual function and virtual inheritance. \mk{4+3+3}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2023--24}
%% ── S7-Q1 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Diamond problem, virtual inheritance, virtual function vs virtual inheritance}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{The Diamond Problem:}

When a class \texttt{D} inherits from \texttt{B} and \texttt{C},
both of which inherit from \texttt{A}, \texttt{D} contains
\textbf{two copies} of \texttt{A}'s members --- one from \texttt{B}
and one from \texttt{C}.  Accessing \texttt{A}'s members through
\texttt{D} is \textbf{ambiguous}:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class A { public: int x; };
class B : public A {};
class C : public A {};
class D : public B, public C {};
// D has B::A::x and C::A::x -- ambiguous!
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Solution --- Virtual Inheritance:}

Declare the inheritance from \texttt{A} as \texttt{virtual} in both
\texttt{B} and \texttt{C}.  The compiler ensures only
\textbf{one shared copy} of \texttt{A} exists in \texttt{D}:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class A { public: int x; void show(){ cout<<x<<"\n"; } };
class B : virtual public A {};   // virtual inheritance
class C : virtual public A {};   // virtual inheritance
class D : public B, public C {}; // single copy of A

int main(){
    D d;
    d.x = 42;    // no ambiguity -- only one x
    d.show();    // 42
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Virtual Function vs Virtual Inheritance:}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Virtual Function} & \textbf{Virtual Inheritance} \\
\midrule
Enables \textbf{runtime polymorphism}: the correct overridden
function is selected based on the actual object type. &
Ensures only \textbf{one copy} of a base class exists in a
diamond hierarchy --- solves the diamond problem. \\[3pt]
Declared with \texttt{virtual} in the base class function. &
Declared with \texttt{virtual} in the inheritance specifier. \\[3pt]
\texttt{virtual void f() \{ \}} &
\texttt{class B : virtual public A \{ \}} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Demonstrate the access of private, public, and protected members of a base class in a derived class and in main, when the derived class inherits using \textbf{private} inheritance. \mk{12}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23}
%% ── S7-Q17 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Private inheritance --- access demo in derived and main}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Base {
    int priv = 1;
protected:
    int prot = 2;
public:
    int pub = 3;
    void showBase(){ cout<<"priv="<<priv<<" prot="<<prot<<" pub="<<pub<<"\n"; }
};

class Derived : private Base {
    // pub  is now PRIVATE in Derived
    // prot is now PRIVATE in Derived
    // priv is INACCESSIBLE
public:
    void showDerived(){
        // priv = 10;  // ERROR: inaccessible (private in Base)
        prot = 20;     // OK: accessible inside Derived (was protected)
        pub  = 30;     // OK: accessible inside Derived (was public)
        showBase();    // OK: showBase() is accessible inside Derived
    }
};

int main(){
    Derived d;
    d.showDerived();    // OK: public method of Derived

    // d.pub = 5;       // ERROR: pub is private via private inheritance
    // d.showBase();    // ERROR: showBase() is private via private inheritance
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Summary:} With private inheritance, all inherited members
become private in the derived class --- inaccessible from outside the
derived class (\texttt{main}), but still accessible within the
derived class's own member functions.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Design a program to show the use of virtual functions in achieving runtime polymorphism. \mk{12}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23}

%% ── S7-Q20 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Runtime polymorphism with virtual functions}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Shape {
public:
    virtual double area()  const = 0;
    virtual void   show()  const { cout<<"Shape\n"; }
    virtual ~Shape(){}
};

class Circle : public Shape {
    double r;
public:
    Circle(double r): r(r){}
    double area() const override { return 3.14159*r*r; }
    void   show() const override { cout<<"Circle, area="<<area()<<"\n"; }
};

class Rectangle : public Shape {
    double w, h;
public:
    Rectangle(double w,double h):w(w),h(h){}
    double area() const override { return w*h; }
    void   show() const override { cout<<"Rectangle, area="<<area()<<"\n"; }
};

class Triangle : public Shape {
    double b, h;
public:
    Triangle(double b,double h):b(b),h(h){}
    double area() const override { return 0.5*b*h; }
    void   show() const override { cout<<"Triangle, area="<<area()<<"\n"; }
};

int main(){
    Shape* shapes[] = {
        new Circle(3),
        new Rectangle(4,5),
        new Triangle(6,8)
    };
    for(Shape* s: shapes){
        s->show();     // correct function called at run time
        delete s;
    }
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Output:}
\begin{lstlisting}
Circle, area=28.2743
Rectangle, area=20
Triangle, area=24
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Describe the use of a virtual base class. Explain why it is not possible to create an object of an abstract class. \mk{12}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22}

%% ── S7-Q7 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Virtual base class use; why abstract class cannot be instantiated}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Use of virtual base class:}
Eliminates the duplicate base class subobject in diamond inheritance,
providing a single shared instance of the base.  (See S7-Q3 for full
example.)

\textbf{Why an abstract class cannot be instantiated:}

An \textbf{abstract class} is a class containing at least one
\textbf{pure virtual function} (\texttt{= 0}).  A pure virtual
function has \textbf{no implementation} in the abstract class.

If the compiler allowed creating an object of the abstract class,
that object might have a call to the pure virtual function, which
has no body --- this would lead to undefined behaviour.  The compiler
therefore \textbf{prevents instantiation} of any class with
unimplemented pure virtual functions.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Shape {
public:
    virtual double area() const = 0;  // pure virtual: no body
};

// Shape s;   // ERROR: cannot instantiate abstract class
Shape* p = new Circle(5);  // OK: pointer to abstract class is fine
\end{lstlisting}

A derived class that implements \emph{all} pure virtual functions
becomes \textbf{concrete} and can be instantiated.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Describe the differences between function overloading and function overriding. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22}
\variant{CSE 107 | 2019--20}
%% ── S7-Q18 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Function overloading vs function overriding}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Function Overloading} & \textbf{Function Overriding} \\
\midrule
Multiple functions with the \textbf{same name} but
\textbf{different parameter lists} in the \textbf{same scope}. &
A derived class provides a \textbf{new implementation} for a
\textbf{virtual function} that already exists in the base class. \\[3pt]
Resolved at \textbf{compile time} (static polymorphism). &
Resolved at \textbf{run time} (dynamic polymorphism). \\[3pt]
Functions differ in number/type of parameters. &
Function signatures must \textbf{match exactly}. \\[3pt]
No inheritance required. &
Requires inheritance and \texttt{virtual} in base. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
// Overloading (same class):
class Calc {
public:
    int   add(int a, int b)       { return a+b; }
    double add(double a, double b){ return a+b; }  // overloaded
};

// Overriding (inheritance):
class Animal { public: virtual void speak(){ cout<<"...sound\n"; } };
class Dog : public Animal {
public:
    void speak() override { cout<<"Woof!\n"; }  // overridden
};
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Demonstrate the order of execution of constructor functions for multilevel inheritance with an example program. \mk{11}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22, 2019--20}
%% ── S7-Q22 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Constructor order in multilevel and multiple inheritance}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Multilevel inheritance --- constructors called from base to
most-derived; destructors in reverse:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Base { public:
    Base() { cout<<"Base constructor\n"; }
    ~Base(){ cout<<"Base destructor\n";  }
};
class Mid : public Base { public:
    Mid()  { cout<<"Mid constructor\n"; }
    ~Mid() { cout<<"Mid destructor\n";  }
};
class Der : public Mid { public:
    Der()  { cout<<"Der constructor\n"; }
    ~Der() { cout<<"Der destructor\n";  }
};

int main(){ Der d; }
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Output:}
\begin{lstlisting}
Base constructor
Mid constructor
Der constructor
Der destructor
Mid destructor
Base destructor
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Multiple inheritance --- constructor order follows declaration:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class X { public: X(){cout<<"X\n";} ~X(){cout<<"~X\n";} };
class Y { public: Y(){cout<<"Y\n";} ~Y(){cout<<"~Y\n";} };
class Z : public X, public Y { public: Z(){cout<<"Z\n";} ~Z(){cout<<"~Z\n";} };
int main(){ Z z; }
// Output: X  Y  Z  ~Z  ~Y  ~X
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Is it possible that a derived class inherits more than one copy of the base class? If yes, show the scenario; how can you avoid it? \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}

%% ── S7-Q5 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Can a derived class inherit more than one copy of base? How to avoid?}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Yes} --- without virtual inheritance, a derived class in a
diamond hierarchy inherits \textbf{multiple copies} of the base:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class A { public: int x=0; };
class B : public A {};   // B has A::x
class C : public A {};   // C has A::x
class D : public B, public C {};  // D has B::A::x AND C::A::x

D d;
// d.x = 5;      // ERROR: ambiguous -- which x?
d.B::x = 5;     // must qualify explicitly
d.C::x = 10;
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{How to avoid --- virtual inheritance:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class A { public: int x=0; };
class B : virtual public A {};
class C : virtual public A {};
class D : public B, public C {};

D d;
d.x = 42;   // unambiguous -- single shared A
cout << d.x; // 42
\end{lstlisting}

With \texttt{virtual}, only one copy of \texttt{A} is maintained
in \texttt{D}, shared by both \texttt{B} and \texttt{C} paths.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Differentiate between compile time polymorphism and run time polymorphism. \mk{8}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}
%% ── S7-Q19 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Compile-time vs run-time polymorphism}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Compile-Time Polymorphism} & \textbf{Run-Time Polymorphism} \\
\midrule
Resolved by compiler before execution. &
Resolved during program execution. \\[3pt]
Achieved via function overloading, operator overloading, templates. &
Achieved via virtual functions and base class pointers/references. \\[3pt]
Faster --- no vtable lookup overhead. &
Slight overhead --- vtable lookup per call. \\[3pt]
Also called \emph{static polymorphism}. &
Also called \emph{dynamic polymorphism}. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
// Compile-time:
void print(int x)    { cout<<x<<"\n"; }
void print(double x) { cout<<x<<"\n"; }
print(5);    // compiler picks print(int)
print(3.14); // compiler picks print(double)

// Run-time:
class Shape { public: virtual double area()const=0; };
class Circle: public Shape { double r; public: Circle(double r):r(r){}
    double area()const override{return 3.14159*r*r;} };
Shape* s=new Circle(3);
cout<<s->area();  // decided at run time: Circle::area()
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Describe a scenario when we cannot create an object of a class. \mk{7}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20}
\variant{CSE 201 | 2007--08}
%% ── S7-Q25 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Scenario when an object of a class cannot be created}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Scenario 1 --- Abstract class (pure virtual function):}

A class with at least one unimplemented pure virtual function is
\emph{abstract}; no object can be created from it.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Shape { public: virtual double area()=0; };
// Shape s;  // ERROR
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Scenario 2 --- Private constructor (Singleton pattern):}

The constructor is declared \texttt{private}; objects can only be
created through a controlled static factory method.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Singleton {
    Singleton(){}
public:
    static Singleton& get(){ static Singleton s; return s; }
};
// Singleton s;  // ERROR: constructor is private
Singleton& s = Singleton::get(); // OK
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Scenario 3 --- Deleted constructor:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class NoInstance {
public:
    NoInstance() = delete;
};
// NoInstance n;  // ERROR: use of deleted function
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Why virtual base classes are used? Give an example of a class hierarchy where virtual base class is necessary to avoid ambiguity. \mk{9}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}

%% ── S7-Q3 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Virtual base class --- why and when needed}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Virtual Base Class:}
A base class declared with \texttt{virtual} in the inheritance
specifier.  It ensures that when a class appears multiple times in a
hierarchy (diamond), only \textbf{one shared instance} of that base
is included in the most-derived class.

\textbf{When needed:} Whenever the same base class is inherited by
two or more intermediate classes that are themselves combined in a
derived class (diamond problem).

\textbf{Example:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Vehicle {
public:
    int speed;
    Vehicle(int s=0): speed(s){}
    void show(){ cout<<"Speed: "<<speed<<"\n"; }
};

class Car    : virtual public Vehicle {};
class Boat   : virtual public Vehicle {};
class Amphibian : public Car, public Boat {
public:
    Amphibian(int s): Vehicle(s){}  // initialises the ONE shared Vehicle
};

int main(){
    Amphibian a(120);
    a.speed = 80;    // no ambiguity
    a.show();        // Speed: 80
}
\end{lstlisting}

Without \texttt{virtual}, \texttt{Amphibian} would have
\texttt{Car::Vehicle} and \texttt{Boat::Vehicle} as two separate
subobjects; \texttt{a.speed} would be ambiguous.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Can constructor or destructor functions be declared as virtual? Explain briefly. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}

\item Explain what is wrong with the following code. Rewrite to solve the problem. What will be the output after fixing?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class A {
    int x;
public:
    void setX(int i) { x = i; }
    void print() { cout << x; }
};
class B : public A { public: B() { setX(10); } };
class C : public A { public: C() { setX(20); } };
class D : public B, public C { };
int main() { D d; d.print(); return 0; }
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17}

%% ── S7-Q6 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Fix diamond ambiguity in class D; output after fix}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Problem:} \texttt{D} inherits from both \texttt{B} and
\texttt{C}, each of which has its own copy of \texttt{A}.
\texttt{d.print()} is \textbf{ambiguous} --- the compiler does not
know whether to call \texttt{B::A::print()} or \texttt{C::A::print()}.

\textbf{Fix --- make A a virtual base class:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class A {
    int x;
public:
    void setX(int i) { x = i; }
    void print()     { cout << x << "\n"; }
};

class B : virtual public A {  // virtual inheritance
public:
    B() { setX(10); }
};

class C : virtual public A {  // virtual inheritance
public:
    C() { setX(20); }
};

class D : public B, public C {};

int main() {
    D d;        // B sets x=10, then C sets x=20 (one shared copy)
    d.print();  // unambiguous: prints 20
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Output after fixing:}
\begin{lstlisting}
20
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Reason:} The single shared \texttt{A::x} is set to 10 by
\texttt{B}'s constructor, then overwritten to 20 by \texttt{C}'s
constructor (base constructors are called in declaration order).

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Briefly explain the visibility of private, protected, and public members of a class in a derived class when the base class is inherited as private, protected, and public. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17}
%% ── S7-Q15 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
%% ── S7-Q15 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
 boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
 title={\textbf{Answer --- Visibility of Class Members in Inheritance}},
 fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

When a derived class inherits from a base class, the visibility of the inherited members depends on the access specifier used during inheritance. Regardless of the inheritance mode, \texttt{private} members of the base class are \textbf{never} directly accessible from the derived class.

\textbf{1. Public Inheritance:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \texttt{public} members of the base class remain \texttt{public} in the derived class.
    \item \texttt{protected} members of the base class remain \texttt{protected} in the derived class.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{2. Protected Inheritance:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Both \texttt{public} and \texttt{protected} members of the base class become \texttt{protected} in the derived class. They are accessible inside the derived class but inaccessible from outside.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{3. Private Inheritance:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Both \texttt{public} and \texttt{protected} members of the base class become \texttt{private} in the derived class. They can only be accessed by the member functions of the derived class itself.
\end{itemize}

\vspace{0.5em}
\textbf{Visibility Summary Table:}
\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.22\linewidth}p{0.22\linewidth}p{0.22\linewidth}p{0.22\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
& \multicolumn{3}{c}{\textbf{Inheritance type}} \\
\cmidrule{2-4}
\textbf{Base member} & \textbf{public} & \textbf{protected} & \textbf{private} \\
\midrule
public  & public  & protected & private \\
protected & protected & protected & private \\
private  & inaccessible & inaccessible & inaccessible \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Example --- Demonstrating Private Inheritance:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class Base
{
    int priv = 1;

protected:
    int prot = 2;

public:
    int pub = 3;
};

class Der : private Base
{
    //    pub -> becomes private in Der (accessible inside Der only)
    //    prot -> becomes private in Der (accessible inside Der only)
    //    priv -> totally inaccessible
public:
    void show()
    {
        cout << "pub: " << pub << "\n";   // OK
        cout << "prot: " << prot << "\n"; // OK
        // cout << priv;                // ERROR: inaccessible
    }
};

int main()
{
    Der d;
    // d.pub = 5;  // ERROR: pub is private via private inheritance
    d.show(); // OK: show() is a public function in Der
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Suppose class A has virtual functions and class B publicly inherits from A. What happens if class B does not implement some virtual functions? What if they are pure virtual? \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17}

%% ── S7-Q16 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- B inherits A (virtual); what if B doesn't implement some virtual functions?}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Case 1 --- Virtual functions (not pure):}

If \texttt{B} does not override a virtual function from \texttt{A},
\texttt{B} \textbf{inherits the base class implementation}.  Calling
the function through a \texttt{B} pointer uses \texttt{A}'s version.
\texttt{B} can still be instantiated.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class A { public: virtual void f(){ cout<<"A::f\n"; } };
class B : public A { };  // does not override f

B b;
b.f();          // calls A::f -- inherited
A* p = &b;
p->f();         // calls A::f (runtime dispatch still works)
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Case 2 --- Pure virtual functions:}

If \texttt{A} has a pure virtual function and \texttt{B} does
\textbf{not} implement it, \texttt{B} is also \textbf{abstract}
--- it cannot be instantiated.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class A { public: virtual void f()=0; };  // pure virtual
class B : public A { };                   // does NOT override f
// B is still abstract
// B b;   // ERROR: cannot instantiate abstract class B

class C : public A { public: void f(){ cout<<"C::f\n"; } };
C c;       // OK: C implements f
c.f();     // C::f
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Implement a \texttt{Queue} class by inheriting from the \texttt{List} class. Your queue class should override the three functions as follows:
\begin{itemize}
    \item[(i)] \texttt{store} --- override this to add a new item at the end of the queue
    \item[(ii)] \texttt{retrieve} --- override this to remove and return the value from the beginning of the queue
    \item[(iii)] \texttt{print} --- displays the queue items as a comma separated list at the stdout
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class ListItem
{
    int item;
    ListItem * next;
public:
    ListItem(int arg){ item = arg; next = NULL; }
    int getItem(){ return item; }
    void setNext(ListItem * n){ next = n; }
    ListItem * getNext() { return next; }
};

class List
{
private:
    ListItem * head;
    ListItem * tail;
public:
    List () { head = NULL; tail = NULL; }
    virtual void store(int item)=0;
    virtual int retrieve()=0;
    virtual void print()=0;
};
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{20}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17, 2013--14}
%% ── S7-Q30 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Queue inheriting List --- store, retrieve, print}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class ListItem
{
    int item;
    ListItem * next;
public:
    ListItem(int arg){ item = arg; next = NULL; }
    int getItem(){ return item; }
    void setNext(ListItem * n){ next = n; }
    ListItem * getNext() { return next; }
};

class List
{
protected: // Note: Changed from private to protected so Queue can access head & tail
    ListItem * head;
    ListItem * tail;
public:
    List () { head = NULL; tail = NULL; }
    virtual void store(int item)=0;
    virtual int retrieve()=0;
    virtual void print()=0;
    virtual ~List() { 
        while(head) { 
            ListItem* t = head; 
            head = head->getNext(); 
            delete t; 
        } 
    }
};

class Queue : public List {
public:
    // (i) store - add at end of the queue
    void store(int item) override {
        ListItem* n = new ListItem(item);
        if (tail == NULL) { 
            head = tail = n; 
        } else { 
            tail->setNext(n); 
            tail = n; 
        }
    }

    // (ii) retrieve - remove and return from beginning of the queue
    int retrieve() override {
        if (head == NULL) { 
            return -1; // Assuming -1 represents empty 
        }
        int v = head->getItem();
        ListItem* t = head;
        head = head->getNext();
        if (head == NULL) {
            tail = NULL;
        }
        delete t;
        return v;
    }

    // (iii) print - comma-separated list at stdout
    void print() override {
        ListItem* cur = head;
        while (cur != NULL) { 
            cout << cur->getItem(); 
            if (cur->getNext() != NULL) cout << ","; 
            cur = cur->getNext(); 
        }
        cout << "\n";
    }
};

int main(){
    Queue q;
    q.store(10); q.store(20); q.store(30);
    q.print();                   // 10,20,30
    cout << q.retrieve() << "\n"; // 10
    q.print();                   // 20,30
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Assume the class \texttt{hand} is inherited by both \texttt{man} and \texttt{monkey}. The class \texttt{animal} inherits both. What type of inheritance is this? What is the problem? How to solve it? \mk{8}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2015--16}
%% ── S7-Q2 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- hand/man/monkey/animal --- inheritance type, problem, solution}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Type of inheritance:} \textbf{Multiple inheritance} (diamond
pattern) --- \texttt{man} and \texttt{monkey} both inherit
\texttt{hand}; \texttt{animal} inherits both, making it a
\emph{diamond hierarchy}.

\textbf{Problem:} \texttt{animal} inherits \textbf{two copies} of
\texttt{hand} --- one via \texttt{man} and one via \texttt{monkey}.
Any reference to \texttt{hand}'s members inside \texttt{animal} is
\textbf{ambiguous}.

\textbf{Solution --- virtual base class:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class hand {
public:
    void wave() { cout << "Hand waves\n"; }
};
class man    : virtual public hand {};
class monkey : virtual public hand {};
class animal : public man, public monkey {};

int main() {
    animal a;
    a.wave();   // no ambiguity -- one shared copy of hand
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What are the differences between virtual function and pure virtual function? If a class contains a pure virtual function, what is it called and what restrictions apply? \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}

%% ── S7-Q11 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Virtual vs pure virtual function; abstract class restrictions}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Virtual Function} & \textbf{Pure Virtual Function} \\
\midrule
Has a body in the base class. &
Has \textbf{no} body (\texttt{= 0}). \\[3pt]
Derived class \emph{may} override. &
Derived class \textbf{must} override. \\[3pt]
Base class can be instantiated. &
Base class \textbf{cannot} be instantiated (abstract). \\[3pt]
\texttt{virtual void f()\{...\}} &
\texttt{virtual void f()=0;} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Abstract class --- restrictions:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Cannot create objects of an abstract class directly.
  \item Can declare pointers and references to it.
  \item A derived class that does not implement all pure virtual
    functions is also abstract.
  \item Constructors of abstract classes can be defined (called by
    derived constructors).
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Consider the bookshop example: \texttt{media} base class with \texttt{title} and \texttt{publication}; \texttt{book} subclass with number of pages; \texttt{tape} subclass with playing time. Each class has virtual \texttt{read()} and \texttt{show()}. Write a program modeling this hierarchy and processing objects using base class pointers. \mk{20}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2015--16}
%% ── S7-Q28 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Bookshop hierarchy --- media, book, tape with virtual functions}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
#include <iostream>
#include <string>
using namespace std;

class media {
protected:
    string title, publication;
public:
    media(string t, string p): title(t), publication(p){}
    virtual void read() const = 0;
    virtual void show() const = 0;
    virtual ~media(){}
};

class book : public media {
    int pages;
public:
    book(string t,string p,int pg): media(t,p), pages(pg){}
    void read() const override { cout<<"Reading book: "<<title<<"\n"; }
    void show() const override {
        cout<<"Book: "<<title<<" | "<<publication<<" | "<<pages<<" pages\n";
    }
};

class tape : public media {
    double playtime;
public:
    tape(string t,string p,double pt): media(t,p), playtime(pt){}
    void read()  const override { cout<<"Playing tape: "<<title<<"\n"; }
    void show()  const override {
        cout<<"Tape: "<<title<<" | "<<publication<<" | "<<playtime<<" min\n";
    }
};

int main(){
    media* arr[] = {
        new book("C++ Primer","Pearson",900),
        new tape("Data Structures","MIT",60.0)
    };
    for(auto m: arr){ m->show(); m->read(); delete m; }
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Suppose there are three classes A, B and C. Class C wants to inherit both A and B simultaneously. Describe two ways with code examples. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}
%% ── S7-Q31 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Two ways for class C to inherit both A and B}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Way 1 --- Direct multiple inheritance:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class A { public: void showA(){ cout<<"A\n"; } };
class B { public: void showB(){ cout<<"B\n"; } };
class C : public A, public B {};   // inherits both directly

int main(){
    C c;
    c.showA(); c.showB();  // A  B
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Way 2 --- Multilevel (chain) inheritance:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class A { public: void showA(){ cout<<"A\n"; } };
class B : public A { public: void showB(){ cout<<"B\n"; } };
class C : public B {};  // C gets both A and B via chain

int main(){
    C c;
    c.showA(); c.showB();  // A  B
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item What is wrong with the following code segment?
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class base {
    int x;
public:
    void setx(int n) { x = n; }
    void showx() { cout << x << "\n"; }
};
class derived : private base {
    int y;
public:
    void sety(int n) { y = n; }
    void showy() { cout << y << "\n"; }
};
int main() {
    derived ob;
    ob.setx(10); ob.sety(20);
    ob.showx(); ob.showy();
    return 0;
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}
%% ── S7-Q32 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Problem in private inheritance code}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class derived : private base { ... };
int main(){
    derived ob;
    ob.setx(10);  // ERROR: setx is private in derived (private inheritance)
    ob.showx();   // ERROR: showx is private in derived
    ...
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Problem:} \texttt{derived} inherits \texttt{base} as
\texttt{private}.  All \texttt{public} members of \texttt{base}
(\texttt{setx}, \texttt{showx}) become \textbf{private} in
\texttt{derived} and therefore \textbf{cannot be called from
\texttt{main}}.

\textbf{Fix options:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
  \item Change to \texttt{public} inheritance:
    \texttt{class derived : public base}
  \item Add public wrapper methods in \texttt{derived}.
  \item Use \texttt{using base::setx;} inside the public section of
    \texttt{derived} to re-expose specific members.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
// Fix (i):
class derived : public base {  // public inheritance
    int y;
public:
    void sety(int n){ y=n; }
    void showy(){ cout<<y<<"\n"; }
};
int main(){
    derived ob;
    ob.setx(10); ob.sety(20);   // now OK
    ob.showx();  ob.showy();
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Define virtual function and pure virtual function with appropriate examples. Differentiate between early binding and late binding. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% ── S7-Q10 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Virtual function, pure virtual function, early vs late binding}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Virtual Function:} Has a default implementation in the base
class; derived classes may override it.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Shape { public: virtual double area(){ return 0; } };
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Pure Virtual Function:} Has \textbf{no} implementation in
the base class (\texttt{= 0}); derived classes \emph{must} override
it.  Makes the class abstract.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Shape { public: virtual double area() const = 0; };
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Early binding (compile time):} Function call resolved by the
compiler based on the declared type.  Non-virtual functions.

\textbf{Late binding (run time):} Function call resolved at run time
based on the actual object type.  Virtual functions via vtable.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class A { public: void f(){cout<<"A::f\n";} virtual void g(){cout<<"A::g\n";} };
class B : public A { public: void f(){cout<<"B::f\n";} void g() override{cout<<"B::g\n";} };

A* p=new B();
p->f();  // early binding: A::f (declared type is A*)
p->g();  // late binding:  B::g (actual object is B)
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Define virtual base class with examples. Explain why a virtual base class might be necessary. \mk{4}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

\item What happens when a public member is inherited as public, private, or protected? \mk{8+9}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12, 2010--11}

%% ── S7-Q14 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Member visibility table for public/private/protected inheritance}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

When a base class member is inherited, its accessibility in the
derived class depends on \textbf{both} the original access specifier
\textbf{and} the inheritance type:

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}llll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Base member} & \textbf{public inh.} & \textbf{protected inh.} & \textbf{private inh.} \\
\midrule
\texttt{public}    & \texttt{public}    & \texttt{protected} & \texttt{private} \\
\texttt{protected} & \texttt{protected} & \texttt{protected} & \texttt{private} \\
\texttt{private}   & not accessible     & not accessible     & not accessible \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Key rules:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \textbf{private members} of the base are \emph{never}
    accessible in derived classes regardless of inheritance type.
  \item \textbf{public inheritance} preserves the original access
    levels (IS-A relationship).
  \item \textbf{protected inheritance} limits public members to
    protected in the derived class.
  \item \textbf{private inheritance} makes everything private in the
    derived class.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}



\item In what order are constructors called for \texttt{class myclass : public A, public B, public C \{ \}}? In what order are destructors called? \mk{3}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}
%% ── S7-Q24 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Constructor/destructor order for class D : public A, public B, public C}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

For \texttt{class myclass : public A, public B, public C}:

\textbf{Constructor order:} \texttt{A()} $\to$ \texttt{B()} $\to$
\texttt{C()} $\to$ \texttt{myclass()} (left-to-right declaration
order, base before derived).

\textbf{Destructor order} (always reverse of constructors):
\texttt{$\sim$myclass()} $\to$ \texttt{$\sim$C()} $\to$
\texttt{$\sim$B()} $\to$ \texttt{$\sim$A()}.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Define virtual function. Why is virtual function used in C++ programming? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}
\variant{CSE 201 | 2009--10; CSE 107 | 2017--18}

%% ── S7-Q9 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Virtual function --- definition and purpose}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Virtual Function:}
A member function declared with the \texttt{virtual} keyword in a
base class.  When called through a base class pointer or reference,
the \textbf{most-derived} version of the function is executed at
\emph{run time} (late binding / dynamic dispatch).

\textbf{Why used:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Runtime polymorphism:} Allows a single base-class
    pointer to correctly invoke the overridden function of whichever
    derived object it points to.
  \item \textbf{Extensibility:} New derived classes can be added
    without changing code that uses base class pointers.
  \item \textbf{Clean OOP design:} Separates interface (base class)
    from implementation (derived classes).
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Animal {
public:
    virtual void speak() { cout<<"...sound\n"; }
};
class Dog : public Animal {
public:
    void speak() override { cout<<"Woof!\n"; }
};
class Cat : public Animal {
public:
    void speak() override { cout<<"Meow!\n"; }
};

int main(){
    Animal* a;
    Dog d; Cat c;
    a=&d; a->speak();  // Woof!  -- runtime dispatch
    a=&c; a->speak();  // Meow!
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item What is virtual base class? Why and when is it needed? Explain with an example. \mk{12}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}
\variant{CSE 201 | 2008--09; CSE 107 | 2017--18}

\item Constructors cannot be virtual, but destructors can be virtual. Why? \mk{3}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}
%% ── S7-Q26 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Can constructors/destructors be virtual?}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{Constructors cannot be virtual:}

\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item A virtual function call is dispatched through the vtable,
    which requires the object to already exist and be fully
    constructed.
  \item During construction, the object is not yet complete; the
    vtable pointer is not yet set up for the derived class.
  \item Therefore, making a constructor virtual is meaningless and
    the C++ standard forbids it.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Destructors \emph{can} (and often \emph{should}) be virtual:}

\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item When a derived object is deleted through a base class pointer,
    the correct (most-derived) destructor must be called.
  \item Without a virtual destructor, only the base destructor is
    called --- the derived part is not cleaned up (memory/resource
    leak).
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Base {
public:
    virtual ~Base(){ cout<<"Base dtor\n"; }  // MUST be virtual
};
class Der : public Base {
public:
    ~Der(){ cout<<"Der dtor\n"; }
};

int main(){
    Base* p = new Der();
    delete p;
    // With virtual dtor: "Der dtor", then "Base dtor"  -- correct
    // Without virtual:   "Base dtor" only              -- LEAK
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item What are the implications of the following two definitions?
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \texttt{class A : public B, public C \{ \};}
  \item \texttt{class A : public C, public B \{ \};}
\end{enumerate}
\mk{4}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}
%% ── S7-Q8 ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Implications of class A : public B, public C vs class A : public C, public B}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

Both declarations make \texttt{A} inherit publicly from \textbf{both}
\texttt{B} and \texttt{C} (multiple inheritance).  The only
difference is the \textbf{order} in which base class subobjects are
laid out in memory and the order in which constructors and destructors
are called:

\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
  \item \texttt{class A : public B, public C \{ \};}
    \begin{itemize}[itemsep=1pt]
      \item Memory layout: \texttt{B} subobject first, then
        \texttt{C}.
      \item Constructors called: \texttt{B()} first, then
        \texttt{C()}, then \texttt{A()}.
      \item Destructors in reverse: \texttt{A}, \texttt{C}, \texttt{B}.
    \end{itemize}
  \item \texttt{class A : public C, public B \{ \};}
    \begin{itemize}[itemsep=1pt]
      \item Memory layout: \texttt{C} subobject first, then
        \texttt{B}.
      \item Constructors called: \texttt{C()} first, then
        \texttt{B()}, then \texttt{A()}.
    \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

Both are otherwise equivalent in terms of member accessibility.
The order matters when \texttt{B} and \texttt{C} have members with
the same name (ambiguity) or when constructor side effects depend on
initialisation order.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item When do we make a virtual function ``pure''? What are the implications? Explain with example. \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}
%% ── S7-Q12 ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,colback=cG!4,colframe=cG,
  boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- When to make a virtual function pure; implications}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cG]

\textbf{When to make a virtual function pure:}

Make a virtual function \emph{pure} when:
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item There is \textbf{no meaningful default implementation} in the
    base class.
  \item Every derived class \emph{must} provide its own specific
    implementation.
  \item The base class is intended as an \textbf{interface} or
    \textbf{abstract type} --- not to be instantiated directly.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Implications:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item The class becomes \textbf{abstract}; no objects can be
    created from it.
  \item Derived classes that do not implement the pure virtual
    function are also abstract.
  \item Only \textbf{concrete} derived classes (those that override
    all pure virtuals) can be instantiated.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=C++]
class Shape {         // abstract -- no meaningful default area
public:
    virtual double area() const = 0;  // pure virtual
    virtual ~Shape(){}
};

class Circle : public Shape {
    double r;
public:
    Circle(double r): r(r){}
    double area() const override { return 3.14159*r*r; }  // must implement
};

// Shape s;        // ERROR: cannot instantiate abstract class
Shape* p = new Circle(5); cout << p->area(); delete p; // 78.54
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}
