    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Output:}
\begin{verbatim}
Before: a=10, b=20
After:  a=20, b=10
\end{verbatim}
\textbf{Why it works:} The array reference is passed by value, but the
array object on the heap is shared, so modifications to \texttt{a[0]}
and \texttt{a[1]} are visible in \texttt{main()}.


\textbf{Or}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=java]
class NumberWrapper {
    int value;
    NumberWrapper(int value) { this.value = value; }
}

public class SwapObjects {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        NumberWrapper objA = new NumberWrapper(5);
        NumberWrapper objB = new NumberWrapper(10);

        swap(objA, objB);

        System.out.println("objA: " + objA.value); // Outputs 10
        System.out.println("objB: " + objB.value); // Outputs 5
    }

    public static void swap(NumberWrapper a, NumberWrapper b) {
        int temp = a.value;
        a.value = b.value;
        b.value = temp;
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write Java code for a \texttt{TwoDimMatrix} class: input, print, determinant, and \texttt{div(A)} methods. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2015--16}

%% -- Q23 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- TwoDimMatrix Class}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.util.Scanner;

class TwoDimMatrix {
    private double[][] m = new double[2][2];

    // Input 4 elements row-by-row
    void input() {
        Scanner sc = new Scanner(System.in);
        System.out.println("Enter 4 elements (row by row):");
        for (int i = 0; i < 2; i++)
            for (int j = 0; j < 2; j++)
                m[i][j] = sc.nextDouble();
    }

    // Print the 2x2 matrix
    void print() {
        for (int i = 0; i < 2; i++)
            System.out.printf("| %6.2f  %6.2f |%n",
                              m[i][0], m[i][1]);
    }

    // Determinant of a 2x2 matrix: ad - bc
    double determinant() {
        return m[0][0] * m[1][1] - m[0][1] * m[1][0];
    }

    // div(A): divide this matrix by A (multiply by A's inverse)
    // A^-1 = (1/det(A)) * [[d,-b],[-c,a]]
    TwoDimMatrix div(TwoDimMatrix A) {
        double det = A.determinant();
        if (det == 0)
            throw new ArithmeticException("Matrix A is singular.");

        TwoDimMatrix result = new TwoDimMatrix();
        // result = this * A^-1
        double[][] inv = {
            { A.m[1][1] / det, -A.m[0][1] / det},
            {-A.m[1][0] / det,  A.m[0][0] / det}
        };
        for (int i = 0; i < 2; i++)
            for (int j = 0; j < 2; j++)
                for (int k = 0; k < 2; k++)
                    result.m[i][j] += this.m[i][k] * inv[k][j];
        return result;
    }

    public static void main(String[] args) {
        TwoDimMatrix M = new TwoDimMatrix();
        M.input();
        System.out.println("Matrix M:"); M.print();
        System.out.println("det(M) = " + M.determinant());

        TwoDimMatrix A = new TwoDimMatrix();
        A.input();
        System.out.println("Matrix A:"); A.print();

        TwoDimMatrix Q = M.div(A);
        System.out.println("M / A:"); Q.print();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a Java program to store student records as a collection. \texttt{Student} contains Name, Age, CGPA. Sort by: descending CGPA first; if equal, ascending age; if equal, lexicographic order. \mk{20}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2015--16}

%% -- Q24 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Student Collection with Custom Sort}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.util.*;

class Student implements Comparable<Student> {
    String name;
    int    age;
    double cgpa;

    Student(String name, int age, double cgpa) {
        this.name = name; this.age = age; this.cgpa = cgpa;
    }

    // Sort: desc CGPA -> asc age -> lexicographic name
    @Override
    public int compareTo(Student o) {
        int cmp = Double.compare(o.cgpa, this.cgpa); // desc CGPA
        if (cmp != 0) return cmp;
        cmp = Integer.compare(this.age, o.age);       // asc age
        if (cmp != 0) return cmp;
        return this.name.compareTo(o.name);           // lex name
    }

    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return name + " (age=" + age + ", cgpa=" + cgpa + ")";
    }
}

class StudentSort {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        List<Student> list = new ArrayList<>();
        list.add(new Student("Alice", 21, 3.8));
        list.add(new Student("Bob",   20, 3.9));
        list.add(new Student("Carol", 22, 3.8));
        list.add(new Student("Dave",  21, 3.8));
        list.add(new Student("Eve",   19, 4.0));

        Collections.sort(list);

        System.out.println("Sorted students:");
        for (Student s : list)
            System.out.println("  " + s);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Output:}
\begin{verbatim}
Sorted students:
  Eve (age=19, cgpa=4.0)
  Bob (age=20, cgpa=3.9)
  Alice (age=21, cgpa=3.8)
  Dave (age=21, cgpa=3.8)
  Carol (age=22, cgpa=3.8)
\end{verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a program to take input of 120 students, sort them as described above, and write all student information to a file named \texttt{Students.Info}. Write student objects directly to file and read them back as objects. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2015--16}

%% -- Q25 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- 120 Students: Sort, Serialize to File and Read Back}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;
import java.util.*;

class Student implements Comparable<Student>, Serializable {
    String name; int age; double cgpa;

    Student(String name, int age, double cgpa) {
        this.name = name; this.age = age; this.cgpa = cgpa;
    }

    @Override
    public int compareTo(Student o) {
        int c = Double.compare(o.cgpa, this.cgpa);
        if (c != 0) return c;
        c = Integer.compare(this.age, o.age);
        if (c != 0) return c;
        return this.name.compareTo(o.name);
    }

    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return name + " age=" + age + " cgpa=" + cgpa;
    }
}

class StudentFile {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        Scanner sc = new Scanner(System.in);
        List<Student> list = new ArrayList<>();

        // Input 120 students
        for (int i = 0; i < 120; i++) {
            System.out.print("Name: ");   String name = sc.next();
            System.out.print("Age: ");    int    age  = sc.nextInt();
            System.out.print("CGPA: ");   double cgpa = sc.nextDouble();
            list.add(new Student(name, age, cgpa));
        }

        Collections.sort(list);

        // Write objects to file
        try (ObjectOutputStream oos = new ObjectOutputStream(
                 new FileOutputStream("Students.Info"))) {
            oos.writeObject(list);
            System.out.println("Written to Students.Info");
        }

        // Read objects back from file
        try (ObjectInputStream ois = new ObjectInputStream(
                 new FileInputStream("Students.Info"))) {
            @SuppressWarnings("unchecked")
            List<Student> loaded = (List<Student>) ois.readObject();
            System.out.println("Read back " + loaded.size() + " students:");
            for (Student s : loaded)
                System.out.println("  " + s);
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Key points:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \texttt{Student} implements \texttt{Serializable} so the entire
  object (and the \texttt{List}) can be written with
  \texttt{ObjectOutputStream}.
  \item The whole sorted list is written as a single object and read
  back with one \texttt{readObject()} call.
  \item \texttt{try-with-resources} ensures streams are closed
  automatically.
\end{itemize}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is the difference between declaring a variable with \texttt{protected} access modifier versus no modifier? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% -- Q6 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- \texttt{protected} vs Default (No Modifier)}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}lcc@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Access from}          & \textbf{No modifier (package-private)} & \textbf{\texttt{protected}} \\
\midrule
Same class                    & \checkmark & \checkmark \\
Same package                  & \checkmark & \checkmark \\
Subclass in different package & \texttimes & \checkmark \\
Non-subclass, different pkg   & \texttimes & \texttimes \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\medskip
The only difference is that \texttt{protected} additionally allows
access from \textbf{subclasses in other packages}, while the default
(no modifier) restricts access strictly to the same package.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
package p1;
class A {
    int x = 1;           // package-private
    protected int y = 2; // protected
}

package p2;
class B extends p1.A {
    void test() {
        // System.out.println(x); // ERROR -- different package
        System.out.println(y);    // OK -- protected, subclass
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item How is multiple inheritance implemented in Java? Can an abstract class be final? Explain briefly. \mk{4+4}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% -- Q7 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Multiple Inheritance in Java and abstract + final}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Multiple Inheritance in Java:}

Java does \emph{not} support multiple inheritance through classes (to
avoid the Diamond Problem). Instead, it achieves multiple inheritance
of \textbf{type} via \textbf{interfaces} --- a class can implement any
number of interfaces.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
interface Flyable  { void fly();  }
interface Swimmable { void swim(); }

class Duck implements Flyable, Swimmable {
    public void fly()  { System.out.println("Duck flies");  }
    public void swim() { System.out.println("Duck swims"); }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Can an abstract class be final?}

\textbf{No.} Declaring a class both \texttt{abstract} and \texttt{final}
is a \textbf{compile-time error} in Java. The two keywords are
contradictory:
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \texttt{abstract} requires the class to be \emph{subclassed}
  so that its abstract methods can be implemented.
  \item \texttt{final} \emph{prevents} any subclassing.
\end{itemize}
Together they make the class both ``must be extended'' and ``cannot be
extended'', which is logically impossible and the compiler rejects it.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a class \texttt{Animal} that has name and age as member variables. Write another class that adds 3 animals to an ArrayList and removes the animal named ``Lion''. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% -- Q1 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Animal Class with ArrayList}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.util.ArrayList;
import java.util.Iterator;

class Animal {
    private String name;
    private int age;

    Animal(String name, int age) { this.name = name; this.age = age; }

    public String getName() { return name; }
    public int    getAge()  { return age;  }

    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return "Animal{name=" + name + ", age=" + age + "}";
    }
}

class AnimalDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        ArrayList<Animal> list = new ArrayList<>();
        list.add(new Animal("Lion",   5));
        list.add(new Animal("Tiger",  3));
        list.add(new Animal("Parrot", 2));

        System.out.println("Before: " + list);

        Iterator<Animal> it = list.iterator();
        while (it.hasNext())
            if (it.next().getName().equals("Lion")) it.remove();

        System.out.println("After:  " + list);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Output:}
\begin{verbatim}
Before: [Animal{name=Lion, age=5}, Animal{name=Tiger, age=3}, ...]
After:  [Animal{name=Tiger, age=3}, Animal{name=Parrot, age=2}]
\end{verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item A movie club contains many movies. Each movie has a title and one or more directors. A movie may be borrowed and returned. Write \texttt{Movie} and \texttt{MovieClub} classes with a method to return current number of movies. \mk{16}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% -- Q2 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Movie and MovieClub Classes}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.util.ArrayList;

class Movie {
    private String title;
    private ArrayList<String> directors;
    private boolean borrowed;

    Movie(String title) {
        this.title     = title;
        this.directors = new ArrayList<>();
        this.borrowed  = false;
    }

    void addDirector(String d) { directors.add(d); }

    void borrow() {
        if (!borrowed) borrowed = true;
        else System.out.println(title + " is already borrowed.");
    }

    void returnMovie() {
        if (borrowed) borrowed = false;
        else System.out.println(title + " was not borrowed.");
    }

    boolean isBorrowed() { return borrowed; }

    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return title + " " + directors
               + (borrowed ? " [borrowed]" : " [available]");
    }
}

class MovieClub {
    private ArrayList<Movie> movies = new ArrayList<>();

    void addMovie(Movie m)    { movies.add(m); }
    void removeMovie(Movie m) { movies.remove(m); }
    int  currentCount()       { return movies.size(); }

    void listMovies() {
        for (Movie m : movies) System.out.println(m);
    }
}

class MovieDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Movie m1 = new Movie("Inception");
        m1.addDirector("Christopher Nolan");

        Movie m2 = new Movie("The Matrix");
        m2.addDirector("Lana Wachowski");
        m2.addDirector("Lilly Wachowski");

        MovieClub club = new MovieClub();
        club.addMovie(m1);
        club.addMovie(m2);

        System.out.println("Total movies: " + club.currentCount());
        m1.borrow();
        club.listMovies();
        m1.returnMovie();
        System.out.println("After return: " + club.currentCount());
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Output:}
\begin{verbatim}
Total movies: 2
Inception [Christopher Nolan] [borrowed]
The Matrix [Lana Wachowski, Lilly Wachowski] [available]
After return: 2
\end{verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a student class with: id, name, course, number of units completed. Write \texttt{inc()} to increment units by one. Write another class to create a student object, get units, call \texttt{inc()}, and display the object's state. \mk{14}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% -- Q3 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Student Class with inc() Method}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Student {
    private int    id;
    private String name;
    private String course;
    private int    unitsCompleted;

    Student(int id, String name, String course, int units) {
        this.id             = id;
        this.name           = name;
        this.course         = course;
        this.unitsCompleted = units;
    }

    int getUnits() { return unitsCompleted; }
    void inc()     { unitsCompleted++; }

    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return "Student{id=" + id + ", name=" + name
               + ", course=" + course
               + ", units=" + unitsCompleted + "}";
    }
}

class StudentDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Student s = new Student(1, "Alice", "CSE", 30);

        System.out.println("Units before inc(): " + s.getUnits());
        s.inc();
        System.out.println("Units after  inc(): " + s.getUnits());
        System.out.println("State: " + s);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Output:}
\begin{verbatim}
Units before inc(): 30
Units after  inc(): 31
State: Student{id=1, name=Alice, course=CSE, units=31}
\end{verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Show two examples of using the \texttt{super} keyword. \mk{4}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% -- Q4 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Two Uses of the \texttt{super} Keyword}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Use 1 --- Call the parent constructor:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Animal {
    String name;
    Animal(String name) { this.name = name; }
}

class Dog extends Animal {
    String breed;
    Dog(String name, String breed) {
        super(name);       // calls Animal(String)
        this.breed = breed;
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Use 2 --- Call an overridden parent method:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Shape {
    void draw() { System.out.println("Drawing Shape"); }
}

class Circle extends Shape {
    @Override
    void draw() {
        super.draw();      // calls Shape's draw()
        System.out.println("Drawing Circle");
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item What are the properties of static variables and methods? Why is \texttt{main()} declared as static? \mk{6+5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% -- Q5 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Static Variables, Methods and Why main() is Static}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Properties of static variables:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Belong to the \textbf{class}, not to any individual object.
  \item Only \textbf{one copy} exists in memory, shared by all instances.
  \item Initialized when the class is loaded (before any object is created).
  \item Accessed as \texttt{ClassName.variable} (no object needed).
  \item Retain their value across all instances throughout the program.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Properties of static methods:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Belong to the class; called without creating an object.
  \item Can access \textbf{only} static members directly.
  \item Cannot use \texttt{this} or \texttt{super} keywords.
  \item Cannot be overridden (only hidden in subclasses).
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Counter {
    static int count = 0;   // shared across all instances
    Counter() { count++; }
    static int getCount() { return count; }
}
// Counter.getCount() -- no object needed
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why is \texttt{main()} declared static?}

The JVM must call \texttt{main()} \emph{before} any object of the class
exists. Since no instance is available at startup, \texttt{main()} must
be \texttt{static} so the JVM can invoke it directly on the class
without instantiating it first.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a short note on the lifetime of an object in Java. \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% -- Q8 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Lifetime of an Object in Java}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

An object's lifetime spans from its \textbf{creation} to its
\textbf{garbage collection}. The stages are:

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}, itemsep=3pt]
  \item \textbf{Creation} --- \texttt{new} allocates memory on the
  heap and calls the constructor to initialise the object.

  \item \textbf{In use (reachable)} --- At least one live reference
  points to the object. It can be accessed and used freely.

  \item \textbf{Unreachable} --- All references go out of scope or are
  set to \texttt{null}. The object is no longer accessible but still
  occupies heap memory.

  \item \textbf{Garbage collection} --- The JVM's Garbage Collector
  (GC) detects the unreachable object and reclaims its memory
  automatically. The programmer cannot force or predict exactly when
  this happens.

  \item \textbf{Finalization (deprecated)} --- Before collection, the
  GC may call \texttt{finalize()} (deprecated since Java 9) for any
  last-resort cleanup, but relying on it is strongly discouraged.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
void demo() {
    Student s = new Student(1, "Alice", "CSE", 30); // created
    s.inc();                                          // in use
}   // s goes out of scope -- object becomes unreachable
    // GC will eventually reclaim the heap memory
\end{lstlisting}

Unlike C/C++, the programmer never explicitly frees memory; the GC
handles deallocation, preventing dangling pointers and most memory
leaks.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Discuss the access levels of a constructor method of a class in Java. \mk{8}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% -- Q10 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Access Levels of Constructors in Java}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

A constructor can have any of the four access modifiers:

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Modifier} & \textbf{Who can call it} & \textbf{Typical use} \\
\midrule
\texttt{public}    & Anyone, from any package    & Normal instantiation \\
\texttt{protected} & Same package + subclasses   & Abstract-like base classes \\
(no modifier)      & Same package only           & Package-level factories \\
\texttt{private}   & Only inside the same class  & Singleton, factory methods \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Singleton {
    private static Singleton instance;

    private Singleton() { }   // private constructor

    public static Singleton getInstance() {
        if (instance == null) instance = new Singleton();
        return instance;
    }
}

class Base {
    protected Base() { }  // only subclasses or same package
}

class Child extends Base {
    Child() { super(); }  // OK -- subclass can call protected ctor
}
\end{lstlisting}

If \emph{no} constructor is written, the compiler inserts a default
\texttt{public} no-arg constructor automatically.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is the purpose of the method \texttt{finalize} in \texttt{Object}? Why is its use discouraged? \mk{8}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% -- Q11 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- \texttt{finalize()} Purpose and Why It Is Discouraged}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Purpose:}

\texttt{protected void finalize() throws Throwable} is a method defined
in \texttt{java.lang.Object}. The Garbage Collector \emph{may} call it
on an object just before reclaiming its memory, giving the object a
chance to release non-memory resources (e.g., file handles, native
memory).

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Resource {
    @Override
    protected void finalize() throws Throwable {
        System.out.println("Cleaning up resource");
        super.finalize();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why its use is discouraged (deprecated since Java 9):}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \textbf{No guaranteed timing} --- the GC decides \emph{when}
  (or \emph{whether}) to call it; resources may be held for a very
  long time.
  \item \textbf{No guaranteed execution} --- the JVM may exit without
  ever calling \texttt{finalize()}.
  \item \textbf{Performance overhead} --- objects with finalizers take
  at least two GC cycles to collect.
  \item \textbf{Resurrection risk} --- a finalizer can accidentally
  make the object reachable again, causing subtle bugs.
  \item \textbf{Better alternatives exist} --- use
  \texttt{try-with-resources} and \texttt{AutoCloseable}/\texttt{Closeable}
  for deterministic cleanup.
\end{itemize}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Why does the \texttt{main} method not require an \texttt{argc}-type parameter for command line arguments? \mk{4}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% -- Q12 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Why Java's \texttt{main()} Needs No \texttt{argc}}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

In C/C++, \texttt{main(int argc, char* argv[])} requires a separate
\texttt{argc} because a raw array carries no length information.

In Java, \texttt{main(String[] args)} receives a \textbf{String array
object}, which already knows its own length via \texttt{args.length}.
Therefore a separate count parameter is unnecessary.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
public class Demo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        System.out.println("Argument count: " + args.length);
        for (int i = 0; i < args.length; i++)
            System.out.println("args[" + i + "] = " + args[i]);
    }
}
// Run: java Demo Hello World
// Output:
//   Argument count: 2
//   args[0] = Hello
//   args[1] = World
\end{lstlisting}

The JVM populates \texttt{args} automatically with the command-line
tokens; \texttt{args.length} replaces the role of \texttt{argc}.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is run-time polymorphism? How does Java achieve run-time polymorphism? \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% -- Q18 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Run-Time Polymorphism in Java}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Run-time polymorphism} (also called \emph{dynamic polymorphism})
is the ability to call an overridden method through a parent-class
reference, with the actual method executed determined at \textbf{run
time} based on the real type of the object, not the declared type of
the reference.

\textbf{How Java achieves it --- Dynamic Method Dispatch:}

Java uses a \emph{virtual method table} (vtable). Every overridable
method call through a reference is resolved at run time by looking up
the vtable of the actual object. This is automatic for all
non-\texttt{static}, non-\texttt{final}, non-\texttt{private} methods.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Animal {
    void sound() { System.out.println("Some sound"); }
}
class Dog extends Animal {
    @Override
    void sound() { System.out.println("Woof"); }
}
class Cat extends Animal {
    @Override
    void sound() { System.out.println("Meow"); }
}

class Demo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Animal a;           // declared type: Animal

        a = new Dog();
        a.sound();          // "Woof"  -- resolved at run time

        a = new Cat();
        a.sound();          // "Meow"  -- resolved at run time
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

The same reference \texttt{a} calls different implementations depending
on the actual object it holds --- this is run-time polymorphism.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Override the \texttt{equals(Object obj)} method in the following \texttt{Student} class so that two students are equal if they have the same \texttt{name} and \texttt{cgpa}:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Student {
    private int id; private String name; private double cgpa;
    public Student(int i, String n, double c) { id=i; name=n; cgpa=c; }
}
// s1.equals(s2) -> false (different name), s1.equals(s3) -> true (same name+cgpa)
// s1.equals(s4) -> false (same name, different cgpa)
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{11}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}

%% -- Q9 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Overriding \texttt{equals()} in Student}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Student {
    private int    id;
    private String name;
    private double cgpa;

    public Student(int i, String n, double c) {
        id = i; name = n; cgpa = c;
    }

    @Override
    public boolean equals(Object obj) {
        if (this == obj) return true;
        if (!(obj instanceof Student)) return false;
        Student other = (Student) obj;
        return this.name.equals(other.name)
            && Double.compare(this.cgpa, other.cgpa) == 0;
    }
}

class TestStudent {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Student s1 = new Student(1, "Alice", 3.8);
        Student s2 = new Student(2, "Bob",   3.8); // different name
        Student s3 = new Student(3, "Alice", 3.8); // same name+cgpa
        Student s4 = new Student(4, "Alice", 3.5); // same name, diff cgpa

        System.out.println(s1.equals(s2)); // false
        System.out.println(s1.equals(s3)); // true
        System.out.println(s1.equals(s4)); // false
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Note:} \texttt{Double.compare()} is used instead of \texttt{==}
for \texttt{double} comparison to avoid floating-point precision issues.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Suppose the following class is defined:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Point {
    int x, y;
    Point(int x1, int y1) { x = x1; y = y1; }
}
\end{lstlisting}
State the values of Point objects after each statement:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
Point point1; Point point2;
point1 = new Point(10, 20);
point2 = point1;
point1 = new Point(30, 60);
point1 = point2;
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% -- Q14 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Point Object Reference Tracing}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Statement} & \textbf{point1} & \textbf{point2} \\
\midrule
\texttt{Point point1; Point point2;} & \texttt{null} & \texttt{null} \\
\texttt{point1 = new Point(10, 20);} & \texttt{(10,20)} & \texttt{null} \\
\texttt{point2 = point1;}            & \texttt{(10,20)} & \texttt{(10,20)} (same obj) \\
\texttt{point1 = new Point(30, 60);} & \texttt{(30,60)} & \texttt{(10,20)} \\
\texttt{point1 = point2;}            & \texttt{(10,20)} & \texttt{(10,20)} (same obj) \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\medskip
\textbf{Key insight:} \texttt{point2 = point1} copies the
\emph{reference}, not the object. After \texttt{point1 = new
Point(30,60)}, \texttt{point2} still points to the original
\texttt{(10,20)} object. The final \texttt{point1 = point2} makes both
variables refer to the \texttt{(10,20)} object again; the
\texttt{(30,60)} object becomes unreachable and eligible for GC.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write Java code for the following regarding arrays of reference types vs primitive types. What is the difference between \texttt{int c[], x;} and \texttt{int[] c, x;}? \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% -- Q15 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Primitive vs Reference Arrays and Declaration Syntax}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Primitive array vs Reference array:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// Primitive array -- each element stores the value directly
int[] prims = new int[3];       // [0, 0, 0]
prims[0] = 10; prims[1] = 20;

// Reference array -- each element stores a reference (null by default)
String[] refs = new String[3];  // [null, null, null]
refs[0] = "Hello"; refs[1] = "World";

// Array of objects
Point[] points = new Point[2];  // [null, null]
points[0] = new Point(1, 2);    // must create each object separately
points[1] = new Point(3, 4);
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Difference between \texttt{int c[], x;} and \texttt{int[] c, x;}:}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Declaration} & \textbf{c} & \textbf{x} \\
\midrule
\texttt{int c[], x;} & array (\texttt{int[]}) & plain \texttt{int} \\
\texttt{int[] c, x;} & array (\texttt{int[]}) & array (\texttt{int[]}) \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

In \texttt{int c[], x;} the \texttt{[]} applies only to \texttt{c};
\texttt{x} is a simple \texttt{int}. In \texttt{int[] c, x;} the
\texttt{[]} is part of the type, so \emph{both} \texttt{c} and
\texttt{x} are \texttt{int} arrays. The second form is preferred as it
is less ambiguous.
\end{tcolorbox}


\end{enumerate}
\newpage

%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
%% S12 — Interfaces, Abstract Classes & Nested Classes
%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\secdivider{Section S12}{Interfaces, Abstract Classes \& Nested Classes}{cD}{cD}
\markboth{S12 --- Interfaces, Abstract Classes \& Nested Classes}{}
\label{sec:S12}
\titleformat{\section}{\large\bfseries\color{cD}}{}{0em}{}[\titlerule]
\section{S12 --- Interfaces, Abstract Classes \& Nested Classes}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Also fix the following:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
interface X1 { void m1(); static void m2(); default void m3(); }
interface X2 { private void m4(); void m5(); }
abstract class A1 implements X1 { final void m6(); abstract void m7(); }
class A2 extends A1 implements X2 { /* your code */ }
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2023--24}

%% ── Answer: Fix X1/X2/A1 errors + write A2 ─────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Fix \texttt{X1}/\texttt{X2}/\texttt{A1} \& Write \texttt{A2}}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Errors and fixes:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \texttt{interface X2: private void m4();} ---
    \texttt{private} interface method must have a body (Java 9+).
    Fix: \texttt{private void m4() \{ \}}

  \item \texttt{abstract class A1: final void m6();} ---
    A \texttt{final} method cannot be abstract (no body but cannot be overridden --- contradiction).
    Fix: \texttt{final void m6() \{ \}}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Corrected code:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
interface X1 { void m1(); static void m2() { } default void m3() { } }
interface X2 { private void m4() { }  void m5(); }  // Java 9+
abstract class A1 implements X1 {
    final void m6() { }     // fixed: body added
    abstract void m7();
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Minimum code for \texttt{A2}:}

Must implement: \texttt{m1()} (from \texttt{X1}),
\texttt{m5()} (from \texttt{X2}), \texttt{m7()} (abstract in \texttt{A1}).

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class A2 extends A1 implements X2 {
    @Override public void m1() { }
    @Override public void m5() { }
    @Override void m7() { }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What are two types of nested classes? What are the differences between them? List all problems in the following code. Write Java code to fix only the problems in the \texttt{main} method:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class X {
    int a = 1;
    void display() { Y obY = new Y(); obY.display(); Z obZ = new Z(); obZ.display(); }
    void showNested() { System.out.println(b); System.out.println(c); }
    class Y { int b = 2; void display() { System.out.println(a); System.out.println(b); } }
    static class Z { int c = 3; void display() { System.out.println(a); System.out.println(c); } }
}
class Test {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        X obX = new X(); obX.display(); obX.showNested();
        Y obY = new Y(); obY.display();
        Z obZ = new Z(); obZ.display();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{20}
\yr{CSE 107 | Jan 2021, 2023--24}

%% ── Answer: Nested classes types + differences + fix main ───────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Nested Class Types, Differences \& Fixed \texttt{main}}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Two types:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Inner class} (\texttt{class Y}) --- non-static; needs
    outer object; can access all outer members.
  \item \textbf{Static nested class} (\texttt{class Z}) --- static;
    no outer object needed; can only access outer \texttt{static} members.
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textbf{Problems in the given code:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \texttt{showNested()} prints \texttt{b} and \texttt{c} ---
    these belong to \texttt{Y} and \texttt{Z} respectively and are
    not directly accessible in \texttt{X}.
  \item \texttt{static class Z} tries to access \texttt{a} (non-static
    member of \texttt{X}) --- illegal.
  \item In \texttt{main}: \texttt{Y obY = new Y()} --- wrong; must be
    \texttt{X.Y} via outer object.
  \item In \texttt{main}: \texttt{Z obZ = new Z()} --- wrong; must be
    \texttt{X.Z}.
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textbf{Fixed \texttt{main} method:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Test {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        X obX = new X();
        obX.display();
        obX.showNested();

        X.Y obY = obX.new Y();  // inner class: needs outer object
        obY.display();

        X.Z obZ = new X.Z();    // static nested: no outer object needed
        obZ.display();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}



\item Outline the errors in the provided code and fix them without removing access modifiers. Then write the minimum code for class \texttt{c2} so it compiles:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
interface i1 { default void f1(); void f2(); static void f3(); }
interface i2 { void f4(); private void f5(); }
abstract class c1 implements i1 {
    abstract void f6(); final void f7() { }
}
class c2 extends c1 implements i2 { /* your code */ }
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23}

%% ── Answer: Fix interface/abstract class errors + c2 ────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Fix Errors in Interfaces \& Write \texttt{c2}}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Errors and fixes:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \texttt{interface i1: default void f1();} ---
    A \texttt{default} method must have a body.
    Fix: \texttt{default void f1() \{ \}}

  \item \texttt{interface i1: static void f3();} ---
    A \texttt{static} interface method must have a body (Java 8+).
    Fix: \texttt{static void f3() \{ \}}

  \item \texttt{interface i2: private void f5();} ---
    A \texttt{private} interface method must have a body (Java 9+).
    Fix: \texttt{private void f5() \{ \}}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Corrected declarations:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
interface i1 {
    default void f1() { }   // fixed: body added
    void f2();
    static void f3() { }    // fixed: body added
}
interface i2 {
    void f4();
    private void f5() { }   // fixed: body added (Java 9+)
}
abstract class c1 implements i1 {
    abstract void f6();
    final void f7() { }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Minimum code for \texttt{c2}:}

\texttt{c2} must implement: \texttt{f2()} (from \texttt{i1}),
\texttt{f4()} (from \texttt{i2}), and \texttt{f6()} (abstract in \texttt{c1}).

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class c2 extends c1 implements i2 {
    @Override public void f2() { }
    @Override public void f4() { }
    @Override void f6() { }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item 7 methods: \texttt{f1()}--\texttt{f7()}, 3 interfaces \texttt{i1}--\texttt{i3}, 1 abstract class \texttt{A}. Constraints: (i) each interface $\leq$ 2 methods; (ii) abstract class \texttt{A} can only implement 1 interface and define $\leq$ 1 abstract method; (iii) \texttt{MyClass} cannot implement any interface. Write Java code. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23, 2020--21}

%% ── Answer: 7 methods, 3 interfaces, abstract class, MyClass ────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- 7 Methods, 3 Interfaces, Abstract Class, No Interface for \texttt{MyClass}}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Strategy:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Chain \texttt{i1}, \texttt{i2}, \texttt{i3} so abstract class
    \texttt{A} implements only \texttt{i1} (1 interface rule).
  \item \texttt{A} declares at most 1 abstract method.
  \item \texttt{MyClass extends A} --- inherits everything, implements
    no interface directly.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
interface i1 { void f1(); void f2(); }
interface i2 extends i1 { void f3(); void f4(); }
interface i3 extends i2 { void f5(); void f6(); void f7(); }

abstract class A implements i3 {
    // implements f1..f6 concretely, declares f7 abstract
    public void f1() { System.out.println("f1"); }
    public void f2() { System.out.println("f2"); }
    public void f3() { System.out.println("f3"); }
    public void f4() { System.out.println("f4"); }
    public void f5() { System.out.println("f5"); }
    public void f6() { System.out.println("f6"); }
    public abstract void f7();   // <= 1 abstract method in A
}

class MyClass extends A {        // implements no interface directly
    public void f7() { System.out.println("f7"); }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write minimum code in class \texttt{c2}/\texttt{B} for successful compilation. You cannot define it as abstract:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
interface i1 { default void f1() { } void f2(); }
interface i2 { void f3(); void f4(); }
abstract class c1 implements i1 {
    abstract void f5();
    final void f6() { }
}
class c2 extends c1 implements i2 { /* your code */ }
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{6+10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17, 2019--20, 2021--22, Jan 2021}
\variant{CSE 201 | 2014--15, 2008--09}

%% ── Answer: Minimum code for c2 ─────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Minimum Code for \texttt{c2}}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Analysis of what \texttt{c2} must provide:}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Method} & \textbf{Source} & \textbf{Reason} \\
\midrule
\texttt{f2()} & \texttt{i1} (abstract) & not implemented by \texttt{c1} \\
\texttt{f5()} & \texttt{c1} (abstract) & declared abstract in \texttt{c1} \\
\texttt{f3()} & \texttt{i2} (abstract) & not yet implemented \\
\texttt{f4()} & \texttt{i2} (abstract) & not yet implemented \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textit{Note: \texttt{f1()} has a \texttt{default} body in \texttt{i1},
so \texttt{c2} need not override it. \texttt{f6()} is \texttt{final}
in \texttt{c1} --- already implemented, cannot be overridden.}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class c2 extends c1 implements i2 {
    @Override public void f2() { }   // from i1 (abstract)
    @Override void f5() { }          // from c1 (abstract)
    @Override public void f3() { }   // from i2
    @Override public void f4() { }   // from i2
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Explain three uses of the \texttt{final} keyword with short examples. Describe the fundamental differences between an interface and an abstract class. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22}

%% ── Answer: final keyword uses + interface vs abstract class ─────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- \texttt{final} Keyword (3 Uses) \& Interface vs.\ Abstract Class}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Three uses of \texttt{final}:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Final variable} --- value cannot be changed after initialisation.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
final int MAX = 100;
// MAX = 200; // compile error
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Final method} --- cannot be overridden in a subclass.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Base {
    final void show() { System.out.println("Base"); }
}
class Child extends Base {
    // void show() { } // compile error
}
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Final class} --- cannot be extended (subclassed).
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
final class Immutable { }
// class Sub extends Immutable { } // compile error
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textcolor{cD}{\rule{\textwidth}{0.4pt}}

\textbf{Fundamental differences between Interface and Abstract Class:}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Interface} & \textbf{Abstract Class} \\
\midrule
All fields are \texttt{public static final}. &
Fields can have any access modifier and be non-static. \\[4pt]
A class can implement multiple interfaces. &
A class can extend only one abstract class. \\[4pt]
No constructors allowed. &
Can have constructors. \\[4pt]
Represents a \emph{contract} (what to do). &
Represents a \emph{partial implementation} (what + how). \\[4pt]
Methods are \texttt{public} by default. &
Methods can have any access modifier. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}



\item What is the problem with the code below? Write two different ways to fix the problem:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
interface A { default void f() { System.out.println("A's f"); } }
interface B { default void f() { System.out.println("B's f"); } }
public class C implements A, B { }
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{7}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17, Jan 2021}

%% ── Answer: Default method conflict in interfaces ────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Default Method Conflict: Two Fixes}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Problem:} Both \texttt{A} and \texttt{B} provide a
\texttt{default void f()}, so \texttt{C} inherits two conflicting
default implementations. The compiler reports an error:
\textit{``class C inherits unrelated defaults for f()''}.

\medskip
\textbf{Fix 1 --- Override \texttt{f()} in \texttt{C} with its own implementation:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
public class C implements A, B {
    @Override
    public void f() {
        System.out.println("C's own f");
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Fix 2 --- Override \texttt{f()} in \texttt{C} and explicitly delegate to one interface:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
public class C implements A, B {
    @Override
    public void f() {
        A.super.f();   // explicitly call A's default
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textit{The syntax \texttt{InterfaceName.super.method()} is used to
disambiguate which interface's default method to call.}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Suppose there are 7 methods: \texttt{f1()}--\texttt{f7()}, 4 interfaces \texttt{i1}--\texttt{i4}, and a class \texttt{MyClass}. Constraints: (i) each interface defines at most 2 methods; (ii) \texttt{MyClass} can only implement 1 interface. Write Java code for \texttt{MyClass}. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2019--20, Jan 2021; CSE 201 | 2014--15, 2008--09}
\variant{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% ── Answer: 7 methods, 4 interfaces, MyClass implements 1 ───────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- 7 Methods, 4 Interfaces, \texttt{MyClass} Implements 1}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Strategy:} Chain interfaces using \texttt{extends} so that
\texttt{MyClass} only needs to implement \textbf{one} interface
(\texttt{i4}) yet still gets access to all 7 methods.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
interface i1 { void f1(); void f2(); }             // 2 methods
interface i2 extends i1 { void f3(); void f4(); }  // 2 new + 2 inherited
interface i3 extends i2 { void f5(); void f6(); }  // 2 new + 4 inherited
interface i4 extends i3 { void f7(); }             // 1 new + 6 inherited

class MyClass implements i4 {
    public void f1() { System.out.println("f1"); }
    public void f2() { System.out.println("f2"); }
    public void f3() { System.out.println("f3"); }
    public void f4() { System.out.println("f4"); }
    public void f5() { System.out.println("f5"); }
    public void f6() { System.out.println("f6"); }
    public void f7() { System.out.println("f7"); }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textit{\texttt{MyClass} implements only \texttt{i4} but must provide
bodies for all 7 methods because interfaces chain via \texttt{extends}.}

\end{tcolorbox}



\item Write three uses of \texttt{final} keyword with examples. \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08, 2008--09; CSE 107 | 2017--18}

%% ── Answer: Three uses of final (6 marks) ───────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Three Uses of \texttt{final} with Examples}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Final variable} --- creates a constant.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
final int MAX_SIZE = 50;  // cannot be reassigned
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Final method} --- prevents method overriding.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class A { final void show() { System.out.println("A"); } }
class B extends A { /* void show(){} */ } // not allowed
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Final class} --- prevents inheritance.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
final class Utility { }
// class Sub extends Utility { } // compile error
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is an inner class? Point out the problem in the following code segment (if any):
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Outer {
    int outer_x = 5;
    void test() { Inner inner = new Inner(); inner.display(); }
    class Inner { int z = 60; void display() { System.out.println(outer_x); } }
    void showz() { System.out.println(z); }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{3+6}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}

%% ── Answer: Inner class + problem in code ───────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Inner Class \& Problem in \texttt{Outer}/\texttt{Inner} Code}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Inner class:} A non-static class defined inside another class.
It has access to all members (including \texttt{private}) of the outer
class and holds an implicit reference to the outer class instance.

\medskip
\textbf{Problem in the given code:}

\texttt{showz()} in \texttt{Outer} tries to print \texttt{z}, but
\texttt{z} is a member of \texttt{Inner} --- not directly accessible
from \texttt{Outer}'s method.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Outer {
    int outer_x = 5;

    void test() {
        Inner inner = new Inner();
        inner.display();
    }

    class Inner {
        int z = 60;
        void display() {
            System.out.println(outer_x); // OK: inner accesses outer member
        }
    }

    void showz() {
        // System.out.println(z);  // ERROR: z not in scope here
        Inner inner = new Inner();
        System.out.println(inner.z);    // Fix: access via object
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is abstract class? Write the restrictions imposed on an abstract class. Point out and fix the problems in the following code:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
final class X { final void show() { System.out.println("Printed from a method"); } }
class Y extends X { void show() { System.out.println("In subclass method"); } }
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{3+2+6}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}

%% ── Answer: Abstract class restrictions + fix final class code ───────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Abstract Class Restrictions \& Fix}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Restrictions on an abstract class:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item Cannot be instantiated directly (\texttt{new AbstractClass()} is illegal).
  \item If a class has even one abstract method, the class must be declared \texttt{abstract}.
  \item A subclass must implement all abstract methods, or itself be declared \texttt{abstract}.
  \item Cannot use both \texttt{abstract} and \texttt{final} on the same class or method.
  \item Abstract methods cannot be \texttt{private}, \texttt{static}, or \texttt{final}.
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textbf{Problems in the given code:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \texttt{final class X} cannot be extended --- \texttt{class Y extends X} is a compile error.
  \item \texttt{final void show()} in \texttt{X} cannot be overridden --- \texttt{Y.show()} is a compile error.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Fix:} Remove \texttt{final} from the class and the method.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class X {                                       // removed final
    void show() {                               // removed final
        System.out.println("Printed from a method");
    }
}
class Y extends X {
    void show() { System.out.println("In subclass method"); }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Consider the following Java code and write minimum code for \texttt{myclass}:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
interface Intl { void f1(); void f2(); }
interface Int2 extends Intl { void f3(); }
class MyClass implements Int2 { /* Complete this class */ }
public class Test {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        MyClass my = new MyClass();
        my.f1(); my.f2(); my.f3();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\mk{9}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}

%% ── Answer: MyClass implementing Int2 ───────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Minimum Code for \texttt{MyClass}}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Analysis:} \texttt{Int2 extends Intl}, so \texttt{MyClass}
must implement \texttt{f1()}, \texttt{f2()} (from \texttt{Intl}) and
\texttt{f3()} (from \texttt{Int2}).

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
interface Intl { void f1(); void f2(); }
interface Int2 extends Intl { void f3(); }

class MyClass implements Int2 {
    public void f1() { System.out.println("f1"); }
    public void f2() { System.out.println("f2"); }
    public void f3() { System.out.println("f3"); }
}

public class Test {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        MyClass my = new MyClass();
        my.f1(); my.f2(); my.f3();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Output:}
\begin{lstlisting}
f1
f2
f3
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write Java code to do the following: (i) Declare a static nested class \texttt{Inner1} of \texttt{Outer1} and create an object of \texttt{Inner1} in \texttt{NestedDemo.main()}. (ii) Declare an inner class \texttt{Inner2} of \texttt{Outer2} and create an object of \texttt{Inner2} in \texttt{NestedDemo.main()}. \mk{8}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17}

%% ── Answer: Create static nested and inner class objects ─────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Static Nested \& Inner Class Object Creation}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// (i) Static nested class
class Outer1 {
    static class Inner1 {
        void display() { System.out.println("Inner1 (static nested)"); }
    }
}

// (ii) Inner class (non-static)
class Outer2 {
    class Inner2 {
        void display() { System.out.println("Inner2 (inner class)"); }
    }
}

public class NestedDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {

        // (i) Static nested: no outer object needed
        Outer1.Inner1 obj1 = new Outer1.Inner1();
        obj1.display();

        // (ii) Inner class: outer object required first
        Outer2 outer2 = new Outer2();
        Outer2.Inner2 obj2 = outer2.new Inner2();
        obj2.display();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Output:}
\begin{lstlisting}
Inner1 (static nested)
Inner2 (inner class)
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What are the two uses of the \texttt{super} keyword? What is an abstract class? What are the restrictions? \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17}

%% ── Answer: super keyword + abstract class ───────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Two Uses of \texttt{super} \& Abstract Class}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Two uses of the \texttt{super} keyword:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Call superclass constructor} --- must be the first
    statement in the subclass constructor.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Animal { Animal(String name) { System.out.println(name); } }
class Dog extends Animal {
    Dog() { super("Dog"); }  // calls Animal's constructor
}
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Access superclass method/variable} --- when overridden
    in the subclass.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class A { void show() { System.out.println("A"); } }
class B extends A {
    void show() {
        super.show();                   // calls A's show()
        System.out.println("B");
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textcolor{cD}{\rule{\textwidth}{0.4pt}}

\textbf{Abstract class:} A class declared with the \texttt{abstract}
keyword that may contain abstract (bodyless) methods.

\textbf{Restrictions:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Cannot be instantiated.
  \item Subclass must implement all abstract methods or be abstract itself.
  \item Abstract methods cannot be \texttt{private}, \texttt{static}, or \texttt{final}.
  \item A class with an abstract method must be declared abstract.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}







\item When we say ``a class is final'' or ``a method is final'' --- what does it mean? Show example codes. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}

%% ── Answer: final class and final method meaning ─────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Meaning of \texttt{final} Class and \texttt{final} Method}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Final class:} A class declared \texttt{final} cannot be
subclassed.  Any attempt to extend it causes a compile-time error.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
final class Lock { }
// class SpecialLock extends Lock { }  // compile error
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Final method:} A method declared \texttt{final} cannot be
overridden in a subclass, but it \emph{can} be inherited and called.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Parent {
    final void display() { System.out.println("Parent display"); }
}
class Child extends Parent {
    // void display() { }  // compile error: cannot override final method
}
public class Main {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Child c = new Child();
        c.display();   // inherited fine; Output: Parent display
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textit{Common use of \texttt{final} class: \texttt{java.lang.String}
is a final class --- it cannot be subclassed to prevent modification
of its behaviour.}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What is a static code block? When is it executed? Can there be multiple static code blocks? In what sequence? Show with example. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}

%% ── Answer: Static code block ────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Static Code Block}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Static code block:} A block of code preceded by the
\texttt{static} keyword, declared inside a class but outside any
method.  It is used to initialise static variables or perform
one-time class-level setup.

\textbf{When executed:} Once, when the class is \emph{loaded} by the
JVM (before any object is created or any static method is called).

\textbf{Multiple static blocks:} Allowed.  They execute in the
\textbf{order they appear} in the source file.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Demo {
    static int x;
    static int y;

    static {
        x = 10;
        System.out.println("First static block: x = " + x);
    }

    static {
        y = 20;
        System.out.println("Second static block: y = " + y);
    }

    Demo() { System.out.println("Constructor called"); }
}

public class Main {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        System.out.println("main() started");
        Demo d1 = new Demo();
        Demo d2 = new Demo();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Output:}
\begin{lstlisting}
First static block: x = 10
Second static block: y = 20
main() started
Constructor called
Constructor called
\end{lstlisting}

\textit{The static blocks ran once before \texttt{main()}, in
top-to-bottom order.  The constructor ran twice (once per object).}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What are the three uses of Java \texttt{final} keyword? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2014--15}

%% ── Answer: Three uses of final ─────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Three Uses of the \texttt{final} Keyword}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Final variable} --- acts as a constant; cannot be
    reassigned after initialisation.
    \begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
final int SPEED_LIMIT = 80;
    \end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Final method} --- cannot be overridden by any subclass.
    \begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Vehicle {
    final void fuelType() { System.out.println("Petrol"); }
}
    \end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Final class} --- cannot be extended; prevents inheritance.
    \begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
final class SecureToken { }
    \end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}



\item Write an interface and a class that implements it. Show how objects can be created using an interface. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% ── Answer: Interface and implementing class ─────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Interface, Implementing Class \& Object Creation}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// Interface declaration
interface Shape {
    double area();
    double perimeter();
}

// Class implementing the interface
class Circle implements Shape {
    double radius;

    Circle(double r) { radius = r; }

    @Override
    public double area() { return Math.PI * radius * radius; }

    @Override
    public double perimeter() { return 2 * Math.PI * radius; }
}

// Main class
public class Main {
    public static void main(String[] args) {

        // Method 1: object via concrete class reference
        Circle c = new Circle(5.0);
        System.out.println("Area: " + c.area());

        // Method 2: object via interface reference (polymorphism)
        Shape s = new Circle(7.0);
        System.out.println("Perimeter: " + s.perimeter());
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Key point:} An interface reference (\texttt{Shape s}) can hold any
object whose class implements \texttt{Shape}.  Only the methods declared
in the interface are accessible through \texttt{s}.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write the differences between abstract class and interface. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% ── Answer: Abstract class vs interface ─────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Abstract Class vs.\ Interface}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Abstract Class} & \textbf{Interface} \\
\midrule
Declared with \texttt{abstract class}. &
Declared with \texttt{interface}. \\[4pt]
Can have both abstract and concrete methods. &
Methods are implicitly abstract (Java 7 and earlier);
\texttt{default}/\texttt{static} methods allowed from Java 8. \\[4pt]
Can have instance variables (any access modifier). &
Variables are implicitly \texttt{public static final} (constants). \\[4pt]
A class can extend only \textbf{one} abstract class. &
A class can implement \textbf{multiple} interfaces. \\[4pt]
Can have constructors. &
Cannot have constructors. \\[4pt]
Used when classes share common state/behaviour. &
Used to define a contract with no shared state. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write the use of \texttt{abstract} and \texttt{final} keywords in Java with examples. \mk{7}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% ── Answer: abstract and final keywords ─────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- \texttt{abstract} and \texttt{final} Keywords in Java}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{\texttt{abstract} keyword:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Abstract class} --- cannot be instantiated; may contain
    abstract and concrete methods.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
abstract class Animal {
    abstract void sound();          // must be overridden
    void breathe() { System.out.println("Breathing"); }
}
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Abstract method} --- has no body; subclass must implement it.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Dog extends Animal {
    void sound() { System.out.println("Woof"); }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textbf{\texttt{final} keyword:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Final variable} --- constant; value fixed after assignment.\\
    \texttt{final double PI = 3.14159;}

  \item \textbf{Final method} --- cannot be overridden in subclass.\\
    \texttt{final void show() \{ \}}

  \item \textbf{Final class} --- cannot be subclassed.\\
    \texttt{final class MyClass \{ \}}
\end{enumerate}

\textit{Note: A method cannot be both \texttt{abstract} and \texttt{final}
--- \texttt{abstract} requires overriding while \texttt{final} prevents it.}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What are the differences between static nested class and inner class? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}

%% ── Answer: Static nested vs inner class differences ────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Static Nested Class vs.\ Inner Class}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Static Nested Class} & \textbf{Inner Class} \\
\midrule
Declared with \texttt{static} keyword. &
Declared without \texttt{static}. \\[4pt]
Does not hold a reference to outer class instance. &
Holds an implicit reference to outer class instance. \\[4pt]
Can only access \texttt{static} members of outer class. &
Can access all members (including \texttt{private}) of outer class. \\[4pt]
Instantiated without outer object:\\
\texttt{new Outer.Nested()} &
Requires outer object:\\
\texttt{outer.new Inner()} \\[4pt]
Can have \texttt{static} members of its own. &
Cannot have \texttt{static} members (except constants). \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Do you agree or disagree: ``All interfaces are abstract classes but not all abstract classes are interfaces''? Give valid reasoning. \mk{4}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}

%% ── Answer: Interface vs abstract class relationship ─────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- ``All interfaces are abstract classes but not all abstract classes are interfaces''}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Verdict: Disagree.}

Conceptually, an interface is \emph{similar} to a fully abstract class,
but in Java they are \textbf{distinct constructs}:

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item An interface is \emph{not} an abstract class in Java's type
    system.  \texttt{interface} and \texttt{abstract class} are
    separate keywords.

  \item An abstract class can have instance variables, constructors,
    and concrete methods --- an interface (before Java 8) had none of
    these.

  \item A class can implement multiple interfaces but extend only one
    abstract class.

  \item Technically, the JVM does treat interfaces as a special kind
    of abstract type, but the statement ``all interfaces \emph{are}
    abstract classes'' is misleading because the two serve different
    design purposes.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct statement:} Every interface is \emph{implicitly
abstract}, but it is \emph{not} an abstract class --- it is its own
independent type.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What are the two types of nested classes in Java? Briefly describe them. What are the rules regarding access of nested class and outer class? \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% ── Answer: Two types of nested classes + access rules ──────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cD!4,colframe=cD,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Types of Nested Classes \& Access Rules}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cD]

\textbf{Two types of nested classes:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}]
  \item \textbf{Static nested class} --- declared with the
    \texttt{static} keyword inside an outer class.
    It does \emph{not} hold a reference to an outer class instance.

  \item \textbf{Inner class (non-static nested class)} --- declared
    without \texttt{static}.  It holds an implicit reference to the
    enclosing outer class instance.
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textbf{Access rules:}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.46\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Inner class} & \textbf{Static nested class} \\
\midrule
Can access all members (including \texttt{private}) of the outer class. &
Can only access \texttt{static} members of the outer class. \\[4pt]
Outer class can access all members (including \texttt{private}) of the inner class. &
Outer class can access all members of the static nested class. \\[4pt]
Requires outer class object to instantiate:
\texttt{Outer.Inner obj = outer.new Inner();} &
Can be instantiated without outer object:
\texttt{Outer.Nested obj = new Outer.Nested();} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}


\end{enumerate}
\newpage
