\section{S17 --- File I/O \& Serialization}

\begin{enumerate}




\item Consider a Java program that copies \texttt{src.txt} to \texttt{dst.txt} line by line. Implement using try-with-resources. You are not allowed to throw Exception anywhere. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2023--24}

%% -- Q18 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Copy src.txt to dst.txt Line by Line (try-with-resources)}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;

public class FileCopy {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        try (BufferedReader reader = new BufferedReader(
                 new FileReader("src.txt"));
             BufferedWriter writer = new BufferedWriter(
                 new FileWriter("dst.txt"))) {

            String line;
            while ((line = reader.readLine()) != null) {
                writer.write(line);
                writer.newLine();
            }
            System.out.println("File copied successfully.");

        } catch (FileNotFoundException e) {
            System.out.println("File not found: " + e.getMessage());
        } catch (IOException e) {
            System.out.println("IO error: " + e.getMessage());
        }
        // reader and writer closed automatically -- no throw anywhere
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Key points:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \texttt{try-with-resources} closes both \texttt{reader} and
  \texttt{writer} automatically in reverse order.
  \item All exceptions are caught locally --- no \texttt{throws} clause
  is used anywhere.
\end{itemize}
\end{tcolorbox}


\item Consider a Java program that copies binary file \texttt{src.mkv} to \texttt{dst.mkv} using chunks of 8 KB. Implement using try-with-resources. Not allowed to throw Exception anywhere. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22}

%% -- Q19 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Copy Binary File in 8 KB Chunks (try-with-resources)}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;

public class BinaryFileCopy {
    private static final int CHUNK = 8 * 1024; // 8 KB

    public static void main(String[] args) {
        try (InputStream  in  = new BufferedInputStream(
                 new FileInputStream("src.mkv"), CHUNK);
             OutputStream out = new BufferedOutputStream(
                 new FileOutputStream("dst.mkv"), CHUNK)) {

            byte[] buffer = new byte[CHUNK];
            int bytesRead;
            long total = 0;

            while ((bytesRead = in.read(buffer)) != -1) {
                out.write(buffer, 0, bytesRead);
                total += bytesRead;
            }

            System.out.printf("Copied %.2f MB%n",
                              total / (1024.0 * 1024));

        } catch (FileNotFoundException e) {
            System.out.println("File not found: " + e.getMessage());
        } catch (IOException e) {
            System.out.println("IO error: " + e.getMessage());
        }
        // no throws anywhere -- all caught locally
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Key points:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \texttt{byte[] buffer = new byte[8192]} reads/writes exactly
  8 KB at a time for efficiency.
  \item \texttt{in.read(buffer)} returns \texttt{-1} at end of file.
  \item \texttt{out.write(buffer, 0, bytesRead)} writes only the bytes
  actually read --- important for the last chunk.
\end{itemize}
\end{tcolorbox}


\item Write necessary Java code to write \texttt{object1} to a file named \texttt{serial}/\texttt{test} and read the object from the same file and assign it to \texttt{object2}. \mk{8+9}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|p{0.45\textwidth}|p{0.5\textwidth}|}
\hline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.45\textwidth}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, basicstyle=\ttfamily\small, tabsize=4]
class MyClass {
    String s;
    int i;
    public MyClass(String s, int i) {
        this.s = s;
        this.i = i;
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{minipage} & 
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.5\textwidth}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, basicstyle=\ttfamily\small, tabsize=4]
public class FileDemo {
    public static void main(String args[]) {
        MyClass object1 = new MyClass("James Bond", 7);
        // your code
        MyClass object2;
        // your code
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{minipage} \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\yr{CSE 107 | 2016--17, 2017--18}

%% -- Q4 --------------------------11------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Serialize object1 to File and Deserialize to object2}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;

class MyClass implements Serializable {
    int id; String name;
    MyClass(int id, String name) { this.id=id; this.name=name; }
    @Override
    public String toString() { return "MyClass{id="+id+",name="+name+"}"; }
}

class SerialDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        MyClass object1 = new MyClass(42, "Alice");

        // Write object1 to file "serial"
        try (ObjectOutputStream oos = new ObjectOutputStream(
                 new FileOutputStream("serial"))) {
            oos.writeObject(object1);
            System.out.println("Written: " + object1);
        } catch (IOException e) { e.printStackTrace(); }

        // Read object from "serial" and assign to object2
        MyClass object2 = null;
        try (ObjectInputStream ois = new ObjectInputStream(
                 new FileInputStream("serial"))) {
            object2 = (MyClass) ois.readObject();
            System.out.println("Read:    " + object2);
        } catch (IOException | ClassNotFoundException e) {
            e.printStackTrace();
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Output:}
\begin{verbatim}
Written: MyClass{id=42,name=Alice}
Read:    MyClass{id=42,name=Alice}
\end{verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item You are given a text file called \texttt{MyAnalysisOfStudentsPerformance.txt}. Write necessary code to read each line from the file and print out to the console. However, in your output each line should be prefixed with the line number. Your I/O operations should be efficient. Once you have run your code, you observe (to your surprise) that the first line in the console output is:
\begin{center}
    {\bengalifont 1. আমার ভয় হচ্ছে তোমরা হয়তো পাস করতে পারবে না!}
\end{center}
Here, the ``1.'' is the line number you added from your code, and the remaining part was the line read from the file. How were you able to read the Bangla text? (Hint: By opening the text file in Notepad program, you have noticed that the encoding of the file is \texttt{UTF-8}). \mk{10+5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}

%% -- Q8 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Efficient File I/O with UTF-8 Encoding}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Part 1: Efficient Java Program (10 Marks)}

To perform file reading efficiently while ensuring character encoding compatibility, we wrap a \texttt{FileInputStream} inside an \texttt{InputStreamReader} configured with explicit \texttt{UTF-8} decoding, and then buffer the stream using \texttt{BufferedReader}.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.BufferedReader;
import java.io.FileInputStream;
import java.io.InputStreamReader;
import java.io.IOException;
import java.nio.charset.StandardCharsets;

public class PerformanceReader {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        String filePath = "MyAnalysisOfStudentsPerformance.txt";

        // Try-with-resources statement ensures proper, automatic resource closure
        try (BufferedReader reader = new BufferedReader(
                new InputStreamReader(
                        new FileInputStream(filePath), 
                        StandardCharsets.UTF_8))) {
            
            String line;
            int lineNumber = 1;

            // Efficiently read line-by-line via the internal character buffer
            while ((line = reader.readLine()) != null) {
                System.out.println(lineNumber + ". " + line);
                lineNumber++;
            }

        } catch (IOException e) {
            System.err.println("An error occurred while reading the file: " + e.getMessage());
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Part 2: Explanation of Bangla Text Reading (5 Marks)}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Limitations of Default FileReaders:} Standard file utilities like legacy \texttt{FileReader} inherently rely on the host platform's default native character encoding (e.g., \texttt{Windows-1252} or extended \texttt{ASCII}). These legacy single-byte charsets are fundamentally incapable of parsing complex scripts, rendering them as garbled symbols or question marks (\texttt{???}).
    \item \textbf{The UTF-8 Multi-byte Standard:} Bangla characters are non-ASCII and fall within the extended Unicode spectrum, requiring $3$ bytes per character under \texttt{UTF-8} encoding scheme specifications. 
    \item \textbf{Explicit Character Set Configuration:} By explicitly instantiating an \texttt{InputStreamReader} combined with \texttt{StandardCharsets.UTF\_8}, we override system defaults. This directly instructs the low-level stream engine to combine sequential raw byte groups correctly and map them directly into Java's internal 16-bit \texttt{UTF-16} code units (\texttt{char}), allowing Bangla text to display natively in a compatible terminal console.
    \item \textbf{I/O Efficiency Handling:} Wrapping this decoder loop inside a \texttt{BufferedReader} limits expensive underlying hardware disk reads by retrieving substantial data blocks directly into memory buffers upfront, meeting the target system requirement.
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Now, instead of writing each line to the console, you need to write to another file. There is no need to prefix the lines with line number. Modify your code written in 8(a) so that it reads the destination file name from command line and copies the content of \texttt{MyAnalysisOfStudentsPerformance.txt} line by line. Keep in mind that the input file uses \texttt{UTF-8} encoding. Identify what additional code you had to write to account for \texttt{UTF-8} encoding and why? If you did not write any additional code, then explain why. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}

%% -- Q8b -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Stream-Based File Copying with Explicit Target Encoding}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Part 1: Modified Java Program}

The program reads the destination file name dynamically via command-line arguments (\texttt{args[0]}), extracts data line-by-line using a buffered reader loop, and safely pushes it into an explicitly encoded buffered output writer stream.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.BufferedReader;
import java.io.BufferedWriter;
import java.io.FileInputStream;
import java.io.FileOutputStream;
import java.io.InputStreamReader;
import java.io.OutputStreamWriter;
import java.io.IOException;
import java.nio.charset.StandardCharsets;

public class FileCopier {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        // Validation check to prevent ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException
        if (args.length < 1) {
            System.err.println("Error: Please provide the destination file name as a command-line argument.");
            return;
        }

        String srcFile = "MyAnalysisOfStudentsPerformance.txt";
        String destFile = args[0];

        // Try-with-resources manages clean multi-stream lifecycles 
        try (BufferedReader reader = new BufferedReader(
                new InputStreamReader(
                        new FileInputStream(srcFile), 
                        StandardCharsets.UTF_8));
             BufferedWriter writer = new BufferedWriter(
                new OutputStreamWriter(
                        new FileOutputStream(destFile), 
                        StandardCharsets.UTF_8))) {

            String line;
            while ((line = reader.readLine()) != null) {
                writer.write(line);
                writer.newLine(); // Writes platform-appropriate line separator
            }
            System.out.println("File content successfully copied to: " + destFile);

        } catch (IOException e) {
            System.err.println("An I/O error occurred during the copying process: " + e.getMessage());
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Part 2: Analysis of UTF-8 Target Encoding Requirement}

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{What Additional Code Was Added:} 
    An explicit encoding parameter, \texttt{StandardCharsets.UTF\_8}, was introduced into the constructor chain of the output stream writer tier:
    \begin{center}
        \texttt{new OutputStreamWriter(new FileOutputStream(destFile), StandardCharsets.UTF\_8)}
    \end{center}
    
    \item \textbf{Why This Code Is Essential:}
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item \textbf{Platform Default Behavior:} In older standard Java platform specifications (prior to JDK 18), text stream implementations like legacy \texttt{FileWriter} or unconfigured character streams default to the host environment's native default charset (e.g., \texttt{Windows-1252} on standard Western systems).
        \item \textbf{Preventing Character Corruption (Mojibake):} The input source contains complex multi-byte scripts (such as Bangla). While the input stream successfully parses these into Java's internal 16-bit Unicode format via its own \texttt{UTF-8} reader configuration, writing them back to disk without an explicit output encoding will force the system to translate those broad characters through a narrow local system single-byte encoder. 
        \item \textbf{Result of Skipping:} Leaving out this additional instruction will structurally compress the distinct 3-byte character groups into localized ANSI/ASCII maps, resulting in corrupted files dominated by unreadable text elements or block questions marks (\texttt{???}). Explicitly setting \texttt{StandardCharsets.UTF\_8} preserves the structural integrity of the output.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item You are writing a networked 2-person shooter game. Here, a bullet is modeled by a class containing positional coordinates:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Bullet implements Serializable, Cloneable {
    int _posX, _posY;
    public Bullet(int x, int y) { _posX = x; _posY = y; }
    public Object Clone() { return new Bullet(_posX, _posY); }
}
\end{lstlisting}
When one person shoots, the bullet leaves his weapon and follows a certain calculation model to traverse through the scene. You do not need to worry about the calculation model. During animation, the model continually updates the position of the bullet and sends the updated coordinates to the other person's machine over the network. This is done by calling the \texttt{updateBullet()} method of the \texttt{TwoPersonShooterGame} class:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
public class TwoPersonShooterGame {
    ObjectOutputStream _oos; 
    ObjectInputStream _ois;

    public void updateBullet(Bullet bullet, int x, int y) throws IOException {
        bullet._posX = x; 
        bullet._posY = y;
        _oos.writeObject(bullet);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
On the other player's machine, the bullet is rendered accordingly.
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Why does \texttt{Bullet} implement \texttt{Serializable}?
    \item Write a constructor for \texttt{TwoPersonShooterGame}. It should have a \texttt{Socket} as a parameter and hook up the \texttt{\_ois} and \texttt{\_oos} variables to the socket's input and output streams respectively. The signature of the constructor should not have a \texttt{throws} clause.
    \item Add a method in \texttt{TwoPersonShooterGame} class to read a bullet object from the network. The method should have the following signature:
    \begin{center}
        \texttt{public Bullet receiveBulletFromNetwork()}
    \end{center}
    \item Once you have completed the tasks of (i) and (ii), the project is complete. However, you observe that in the first person's machine the bullet is moving through the screen, yet it remains stationary on the second person's machine. Why is this happening? Show three different solutions for the problem (with code and explanation).
    \item Finally, notice that \texttt{\_posX} and \texttt{\_posY} are not encapsulated. If you make them \texttt{private}, \texttt{Bullet} objects can no longer be cloned as the \texttt{Clone()} method is currently written. Why? Correct the \texttt{Clone()} method so that \texttt{Bullet} objects can still be cloned.
\end{enumerate} \mk{3+3+3+11+4}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}

%% -- Q9 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Object Serialization and Stream Caching Dynamics}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{(i) Requirement for Serializable (3 Marks)}
\\The \texttt{ObjectOutputStream.writeObject()} method requires that any object passed into it must implement the \texttt{java.io.Serializable} marker interface. If a class lacks this interface, the JVM will halt execution and throw a \texttt{NotSerializableException}. Implementing it enables the serialization engine to convert the object's state into a sequential sequence of bytes suitable for transmission across a network socket.

\medskip
\textbf{(ii) Constructor without Throws Clause (3 Marks)}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
public TwoPersonShooterGame(Socket socket) {
    try {
        // Crucial: ObjectOutputStream must always be initialized BEFORE ObjectInputStream
        // to flush the stream header and prevent both endpoints from deadlocking on startup.
        this._oos = new ObjectOutputStream(socket.getOutputStream());
        this._ois = new ObjectInputStream(socket.getInputStream());
    } catch (IOException e) {
        System.err.println("Failed to initialize game network streams: " + e.getMessage());
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{(iii) Implementation of receiveBulletFromNetwork() (3 Marks)}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
public Bullet receiveBulletFromNetwork() {
    try {
        return (Bullet) _ois.readObject();
    } catch (IOException | ClassNotFoundException e) {
        System.err.println("Error reading bullet from stream: " + e.getMessage());
        return null;
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{(iv) Root Cause Analysis \& Three Solutions (11 Marks)}

\textbf{The Problem:} 
By default, an \texttt{ObjectOutputStream} maintains an internal **wire handle lookup table (cache)** of references for every object it serializes during its lifetime. When the exact same object reference (\texttt{bullet}) is sequentially passed to \texttt{writeObject()}, the serialization algorithm optimizes bandwidth consumption by writing an ultra-compact **back-reference handle** instead of re-parsing the object's fields. Consequently, even though the internal values of \texttt{\_posX} and \texttt{\_posY} change on the sender side, the receiver side ignores them and simply links back to the original cached instance data from the very first frame.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// --- Solution 1: Explicit Stream Cache Clearing via reset() ---
// Explanation: Calling reset() discards the reference history table inside the stream layout, 
// forcing a fresh, full deep serialization of the fields of any objects passed next.
public void updateBullet(Bullet bullet, int x, int y) throws IOException {
    bullet._posX = x; 
    bullet._posY = y;
    _oos.reset(); // Purges the object tracking table to ensure deep transmission
    _oos.writeObject(bullet);
}

// --- Solution 2: Bypassing Cache via writeUnshared() ---
// Explanation: writeUnshared() instructs the stream to treat this write operation as an 
// entirely isolated entity, ignoring prior reference history mapping and bypassing the cache.
public void updateBullet(Bullet bullet, int x, int y) throws IOException {
    bullet._posX = x; 
    bullet._posY = y;
    _oos.writeUnshared(bullet); // Bypasses standard back-reference caching
}

// --- Solution 3: Emitting Deep Heap Copies (Clones) ---
// Explanation: Instantiating a new heap allocation address breaks the reference lookup entirely. 
// The stream treats the new reference address as a fresh object graph entity and re-sends its data.
public void updateBullet(Bullet bullet, int x, int y) throws IOException {
    bullet._posX = x; 
    bullet._posY = y;
    
    // Invokes the clone method to pass a unique instance pointer over the stream
    _oos.writeObject(bullet.Clone()); 
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{(v) Encapsulation and Cloning Correction (4 Marks)}

\textbf{Why cloning fails with the current method:} 
While the Java compiler allows a class to access its own private fields, implementing a manual \texttt{Clone()} method via the constructor \texttt{new Bullet(\_posX, \_posY)} creates issues with \textbf{inheritance}. If a subclass extends \texttt{Bullet} and attempts to utilize or inherit this manual cloning logic, it will not have access to the \texttt{private} fields of its parent. Furthermore, standard Java cloning should hook into the intrinsic JVM clone mechanism rather than manually passing fields to constructors.

\textbf{Correction:}
We correct this by adhering to the standard \texttt{java.lang.Cloneable} contract. By overriding the intrinsic \texttt{clone()} method from \texttt{Object} and utilizing \texttt{super.clone()}, the JVM automatically handles field-by-field memory duplication (a shallow copy) completely bypassing access modifier restrictions.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// Replaces the manual Clone() method inside the Bullet class
@Override
public Object clone() {
    try {
        // Native JVM bitwise copy; safely accesses all fields regardless of private modifiers
        return super.clone(); 
    } catch (CloneNotSupportedException e) {
        // This will never be reached as Bullet implements Cloneable
        throw new AssertionError("Cloning failed"); 
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write Java code to copy a file \texttt{src.txt} to \texttt{dst.txt} line by line using \texttt{BufferedReader} and \texttt{BufferedWriter}. What are the Java classes and methods that can read/write objects of a class to I/O stream? \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2014--15}

%% -- Q2 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Copy Text File and Object Stream Classes}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;

public class TextFileCopy {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        try (BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(
                 new FileReader("src.txt"));
             BufferedWriter bw = new BufferedWriter(
                 new FileWriter("dst.txt"))) {
            String line;
            while ((line = br.readLine()) != null) {
                bw.write(line);
                bw.newLine();
            }
            System.out.println("Copied successfully.");
        } catch (IOException e) {
            System.out.println("Error: " + e.getMessage());
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Classes and methods for reading/writing objects:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \texttt{ObjectOutputStream} --- wraps an \texttt{OutputStream};
  method \texttt{writeObject(Object)} serializes an object.
  \item \texttt{ObjectInputStream} --- wraps an \texttt{InputStream};
  method \texttt{readObject()} deserializes an object.
  \item The class being serialized must implement
  \texttt{java.io.Serializable}.
\end{itemize}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item What do you understand by serialization in Java I/O? What are the uses of serialization and how is it implemented? \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% -- Q6 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Serialization in Java}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Serialization} is the process of converting a Java object into
a \textbf{byte stream} so it can be saved to a file, sent over a
network, or stored in a database. The reverse process is called
\textbf{deserialization}.

\textbf{Uses:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Persisting object state to disk (save/load).
  \item Transmitting objects over a network (e.g., RMI, sockets).
  \item Caching objects in memory or a database.
  \item Deep cloning of objects.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Implementation:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;

// Step 1: implement Serializable
class Person implements Serializable {
    String name; int age;
    Person(String n, int a) { name=n; age=a; }
}

class SerialExample {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        Person p = new Person("Alice", 25);

        // Serialize
        ObjectOutputStream oos = new ObjectOutputStream(
            new FileOutputStream("person.dat"));
        oos.writeObject(p); oos.close();

        // Deserialize
        ObjectInputStream ois = new ObjectInputStream(
            new FileInputStream("person.dat"));
        Person p2 = (Person) ois.readObject(); ois.close();

        System.out.println(p2.name + ", " + p2.age); // Alice, 25
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item There is a file named \texttt{text.txt}. Write a Java program that reads each line of the file one at a time and prints the line number of the first occurrence of the word ``Bangladesh''. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% -- Q10 -------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Find First Occurrence of ``Bangladesh''}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;

public class FindWord {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        String target = "Bangladesh";
        int foundAt = -1;

        try (BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(
                 new FileReader("text.txt"))) {
            String line;
            int lineNum = 1;
            while ((line = br.readLine()) != null) {
                if (line.contains(target)) {
                    foundAt = lineNum;
                    break;       // stop at first occurrence
                }
                lineNum++;
            }
        } catch (IOException e) {
            System.out.println("Error: " + e.getMessage());
        }

        if (foundAt != -1)
            System.out.println("\"" + target + "\" first found at line "
                               + foundAt);
        else
            System.out.println("\"" + target + "\" not found.");
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Sample output:}
\begin{verbatim}
"Bangladesh" first found at line 3
\end{verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}


\item Why is \texttt{BufferedReader} a preferred reader? Write a program that appends a string in an existing file named \texttt{a.txt}. \mk{3+8}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}


%% -- Q1 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- BufferedReader and Appending to a File}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Why BufferedReader is preferred:}
\texttt{BufferedReader} wraps another \texttt{Reader} and maintains an
internal buffer (default 8192 chars). Instead of performing a system
call for each character, it reads a large block at once, drastically
reducing I/O overhead. It also provides the convenient
\texttt{readLine()} method.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;

public class AppendDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        // true = append mode
        try (BufferedWriter bw = new BufferedWriter(
                 new FileWriter("a.txt", true))) {
            bw.write("This line is appended.");
            bw.newLine();
            System.out.println("String appended to a.txt");
        } catch (IOException e) {
            System.out.println("Error: " + e.getMessage());
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
Passing \texttt{true} to \texttt{FileWriter} opens the file in append
mode so existing content is preserved.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item The members of class \texttt{Record} are a String \texttt{id}, String \texttt{recordDate}, and double \texttt{amount}. Give the class definition. Assume \texttt{recordList.dat} contains 10 instances. Write code to read them into a \texttt{Record} array. \mk{11}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% -- Q5 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Record Class and Reading 10 Objects from File}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;

class Record implements Serializable {
    private String id;
    private String recordDate;
    private double amount;

    Record(String id, String recordDate, double amount) {
        this.id = id; this.recordDate = recordDate; this.amount = amount;
    }

    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return "Record{id=" + id + ", date=" + recordDate
               + ", amount=" + amount + "}";
    }
}

class RecordReader {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Record[] records = new Record[10];

        try (ObjectInputStream ois = new ObjectInputStream(
                 new FileInputStream("recordList.dat"))) {
            for (int i = 0; i < 10; i++)
                records[i] = (Record) ois.readObject();
        } catch (IOException | ClassNotFoundException e) {
            e.printStackTrace();
        }

        for (Record r : records)
            System.out.println(r);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Explain Object Serialization-Deserialization with an example. \mk{8}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}

%% -- Q7 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Object Serialization-Deserialization Example}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\textbf{Serialization} converts an object to bytes;
\textbf{Deserialization} converts bytes back to an object.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;

class Student implements Serializable {
    private static final long serialVersionUID = 1L;
    String name; int roll; double cgpa;

    Student(String name, int roll, double cgpa) {
        this.name=name; this.roll=roll; this.cgpa=cgpa;
    }
    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return name + " Roll:" + roll + " CGPA:" + cgpa;
    }
}

class SerialDesDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Student s1 = new Student("Bob", 101, 3.75);

        // --- Serialization ---
        try (ObjectOutputStream oos = new ObjectOutputStream(
                 new FileOutputStream("student.ser"))) {
            oos.writeObject(s1);
            System.out.println("Serialized: " + s1);
        } catch (IOException e) { e.printStackTrace(); }

        // --- Deserialization ---
        try (ObjectInputStream ois = new ObjectInputStream(
                 new FileInputStream("student.ser"))) {
            Student s2 = (Student) ois.readObject();
            System.out.println("Deserialized: " + s2);
        } catch (IOException | ClassNotFoundException e) {
            e.printStackTrace();
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\textbf{Output:}
\begin{verbatim}
Serialized:   Bob Roll:101 CGPA:3.75
Deserialized: Bob Roll:101 CGPA:3.75
\end{verbatim}
\texttt{serialVersionUID} is recommended to ensure version
compatibility during deserialization.
\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write Java code to copy a binary file \texttt{src.dat} to \texttt{dest.dat} using byte stream technique. \mk{7}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2008--09}

%% -- Q3 --------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Copy Binary File Using Byte Streams}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;

public class BinaryCopy {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        try (FileInputStream  fis = new FileInputStream("src.dat");
             FileOutputStream fos = new FileOutputStream("dest.dat")) {
            byte[] buf = new byte[4096];
            int n;
            while ((n = fis.read(buf)) != -1)
                fos.write(buf, 0, n);
            System.out.println("Binary file copied.");
        } catch (IOException e) {
            System.out.println("Error: " + e.getMessage());
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}
\end{tcolorbox}



\item Write a Java program that counts the total number of characters of the alternating lines of a file named \texttt{input.txt} (starting from the 1st line). Use Character Streams.
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--2008}

%% ── Q12: Alternating lines character count ───────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cA!4,colframe=cA,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Count Characters of Alternating
  Lines Using Character Streams}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cA]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;

public class AltLineCount {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        int totalChars = 0;
        int lineNumber = 0;

        // FileReader is a character stream (not byte stream)
        try (BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(
                new FileReader("input.txt"))) {

            String line;
            while ((line = br.readLine()) != null) {
                lineNumber++;
                // Count characters only from odd lines
                // (1st, 3rd, 5th, ...)
                if (lineNumber % 2 != 0)
                    totalChars += line.length();
            }

        } catch (FileNotFoundException e) {
            System.err.println("File not found: input.txt");
        } catch (IOException e) {
            System.err.println("I/O error: " + e.getMessage());
        }

        System.out.println("Total characters in alternating"
            + " lines (starting from line 1): " + totalChars);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{How it works:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \texttt{FileReader} is the character-stream
  equivalent of \texttt{FileInputStream}; it reads
  characters (Unicode) rather than raw bytes.
  \item \texttt{BufferedReader} wraps it to provide
  efficient \texttt{readLine()}.
  \item \texttt{line.length()} gives the number of
  characters in the line (excluding the newline).
  \item Lines are counted from 1; odd-numbered lines
  (1, 3, 5, \ldots) are the alternating lines
  starting from the first.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}


\end{enumerate}
\newpage

%% S18 — Networking
