\secdivider{Section S18}{Networking}{cB}{cB}
\markboth{S18 --- Networking}{}
\label{sec:S18}
\titleformat{\section}{\large\bfseries\color{cB}}{}{0em}{}[\titlerule]
\section{S18 --- Networking}

\begin{enumerate}
\item A Fibonacci server accepts multiple clients. Client sends two integers; server calculates count of Fibonacci numbers between them and sends result. Communicate only through objects. Write complete Java client-server program. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2022--23}

%% ── Q5 ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Fibonacci Server: Object-Based
  Multi-Client TCP}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Protocol:} Client sends a serializable
\texttt{RangeRequest} object (two integers); server
replies with a serializable \texttt{FibResult} object
(the count of Fibonacci numbers in that range).

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// Shared serializable classes (compile separately)
import java.io.Serializable;

class RangeRequest implements Serializable {
    int lo, hi;
    RangeRequest(int lo, int hi) { this.lo=lo; this.hi=hi; }
}

class FibResult implements Serializable {
    int count;
    FibResult(int count) { this.count = count; }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// FibServer.java
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;

public class FibServer {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        ServerSocket ss = new ServerSocket(5555);
        System.out.println("Fibonacci server ready on port 5555");
        while (true)
            new Thread(new FibHandler(ss.accept())).start();
    }
}

class FibHandler implements Runnable {
    private final Socket socket;
    FibHandler(Socket s) { socket = s; }

    @Override
    public void run() {
        try (ObjectInputStream  in  = new ObjectInputStream(
                 socket.getInputStream());
             ObjectOutputStream out = new ObjectOutputStream(
                 socket.getOutputStream())) {

            RangeRequest req = (RangeRequest) in.readObject();
            int count = countFibInRange(req.lo, req.hi);
            out.writeObject(new FibResult(count));
            out.flush();

        } catch (Exception e) {
            e.printStackTrace();
        } finally {
            try { socket.close(); } catch (IOException ignored) {}
        }
    }

    // Count Fibonacci numbers in [lo, hi]
    private int countFibInRange(int lo, int hi) {
        int count = 0;
        int a = 0, b = 1;
        while (a <= hi) {
            if (a >= lo) count++;
            int tmp = a + b; a = b; b = tmp;
        }
        return count;
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// FibClient.java
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;
import java.util.Scanner;

public class FibClient {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        Scanner sc = new Scanner(System.in);
        System.out.print("Enter lo: "); int lo = sc.nextInt();
        System.out.print("Enter hi: "); int hi = sc.nextInt();

        try (Socket socket = new Socket("localhost", 5555);
             // ObjectOutputStream must be created BEFORE
             // ObjectInputStream to avoid deadlock
             ObjectOutputStream out = new ObjectOutputStream(
                 socket.getOutputStream());
             ObjectInputStream  in  = new ObjectInputStream(
                 socket.getInputStream())) {

            out.writeObject(new RangeRequest(lo, hi));
            out.flush();

            FibResult result = (FibResult) in.readObject();
            System.out.println("Fibonacci numbers in ["
                + lo + ", " + hi + "]: " + result.count);
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Note:} \texttt{ObjectOutputStream} must be
constructed \emph{before} \texttt{ObjectInputStream}
on the same socket to avoid a deadlock caused by the
stream header exchange during initialisation.

\end{tcolorbox}

\item A server program accepts multiple clients. Client sends two integers; server calculates count of prime numbers between them and sends result. Write complete Java server-client program. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2021--22}

%% ── Q6 ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Prime-Count Server: Multi-Client
  TCP}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// PrimeServer.java
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;

public class PrimeServer {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        ServerSocket ss = new ServerSocket(6666);
        System.out.println("Prime server ready on port 6666");
        while (true)
            new Thread(new PrimeHandler(ss.accept())).start();
    }
}

class PrimeHandler implements Runnable {
    private final Socket socket;
    PrimeHandler(Socket s) { socket = s; }

    @Override
    public void run() {
        try (BufferedReader in = new BufferedReader(
                 new InputStreamReader(socket.getInputStream()));
             PrintWriter out = new PrintWriter(
                 socket.getOutputStream(), true)) {

            // Receive two integers on one line: "lo hi"
            String line = in.readLine();
            String[] parts = line.trim().split("\\s+");
            int lo = Integer.parseInt(parts[0]);
            int hi = Integer.parseInt(parts[1]);

            int count = 0;
            for (int n = lo; n <= hi; n++)
                if (isPrime(n)) count++;

            out.println(count);   // send result

        } catch (Exception e) {
            e.printStackTrace();
        } finally {
            try { socket.close(); } catch (IOException ignored) {}
        }
    }

    private boolean isPrime(int n) {
        if (n < 2) return false;
        if (n == 2) return true;
        if (n % 2 == 0) return false;
        for (int i = 3; (long) i * i <= n; i += 2)
            if (n % i == 0) return false;
        return true;
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// PrimeClient.java
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;
import java.util.Scanner;

public class PrimeClient {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        Scanner sc = new Scanner(System.in);
        System.out.print("Enter lo: "); int lo = sc.nextInt();
        System.out.print("Enter hi: "); int hi = sc.nextInt();

        try (Socket socket = new Socket("localhost", 6666);
             PrintWriter out = new PrintWriter(
                 socket.getOutputStream(), true);
             BufferedReader in = new BufferedReader(
                 new InputStreamReader(socket.getInputStream()))) {

            out.println(lo + " " + hi);   // send range

            String result = in.readLine();
            System.out.println("Prime count in ["
                + lo + ", " + hi + "]: " + result);
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Sample output} (lo=1, hi=20):
\begin{verbatim}
Prime count in [1, 20]: 8
\end{verbatim}
(Primes: 2, 3, 5, 7, 11, 13, 17, 19)

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Suppose a client connects to a server at 192.168.1.1, port 44444. Draw the scenario of connection and data transfer. What classes are used to read and write objects in networking? What restriction do they impose? \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2020--21}
%% ── Q7 ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Connection Scenario, Object
  Stream Classes and Their Restriction}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Connection and Data Transfer Scenario}
(Client $\to$ Server at 192.168.1.1:44444):

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    box/.style={draw=cB!70, fill=cB!8, rounded corners=3pt,
                minimum width=3.0cm, minimum height=0.65cm,
                font=\scriptsize, align=center},
    arr/.style={->, thick, cB!80},
    lbl/.style={font=\scriptsize, align=center}
]
  \node[font=\small\bfseries, cB] at (0, 7.8) {CLIENT};
  \node[font=\small\bfseries, cB] at (6, 7.8) {SERVER (192.168.1.1:44444)};

  \node[box] (c1) at (0, 7.0) {\texttt{new Socket("192.168.1.1", 44444)}};
  \node[box] (c2) at (0, 5.5) {\texttt{ObjectOutputStream} (to server)};
  \node[box] (c3) at (0, 4.0) {\texttt{ObjectInputStream} (from server)};
  \node[box] (c4) at (0, 2.5) {\texttt{socket.close()}};

  \node[box] (s1) at (6, 7.0) {\texttt{ServerSocket(44444).accept()}};
  \node[box] (s2) at (6, 5.5) {\texttt{ObjectInputStream} (from client)};
  \node[box] (s3) at (6, 4.0) {\texttt{ObjectOutputStream} (to client)};
  \node[box] (s4) at (6, 2.5) {\texttt{socket.close()}};

  \draw[arr] (c1)--(c2); \draw[arr] (c2)--(c3); \draw[arr] (c3)--(c4);
  \draw[arr] (s1)--(s2); \draw[arr] (s2)--(s3); \draw[arr] (s3)--(s4);

  \draw[arr, orange!70!black]
    (c1.east) -- node[above, lbl]{TCP handshake\\(SYN/SYN-ACK/ACK)}
    (s1.west);
  \draw[arr, blue!60!black]
    (c2.east) -- node[above, lbl]{writeObject(obj)} (s2.west);
  \draw[arr, green!50!black]
    (s3.west) -- node[below, lbl]{writeObject(result)} (c3.east);
  \draw[arr, red!60!black]
    (c4.east) -- node[above, lbl]{FIN (close)} (s4.west);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\medskip
\textbf{Classes used to read and write objects in
networking:}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Class} & \textbf{Purpose} \\
\midrule
\texttt{ObjectOutputStream} &
  Serialises Java objects and writes them to a stream \\
\texttt{ObjectInputStream} &
  Reads and deserialises Java objects from a stream \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\medskip
\textbf{Restriction they impose:}

Any class whose objects are sent over the network
\textbf{must implement the \texttt{Serializable}
interface}. If a class does not implement
\texttt{Serializable}, calling
\texttt{writeObject()} on its instance throws a
\texttt{NotSerializableException} at runtime.

Additionally, every field of the class (and all
referenced objects) must also be serialisable, or
must be declared \texttt{transient} to be excluded
from serialisation.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// Correct: implements Serializable
class Point implements java.io.Serializable {
    int x, y;
    Point(int x, int y) { this.x = x; this.y = y; }
}

// Usage
ObjectOutputStream oos = new ObjectOutputStream(
    socket.getOutputStream());
oos.writeObject(new Point(3, 4));   // OK

// Wrong: no Serializable -> throws NotSerializableException
class BadPoint { int x, y; }
oos.writeObject(new BadPoint());    // runtime exception!
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Briefly describe the types of TCP Sockets. Write a Java Program that opens a connection to the ``whois'' port (port 43) on the InterNIC server, sends a website address, and prints the returned data. \mk{4+15}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2017--18}

%% ── Q8 ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — TCP Socket Types and WHOIS
  Client}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Types of TCP Sockets in Java:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}, itemsep=3pt]
  \item \textbf{Stream Socket (\texttt{Socket})} ---
  Represents one end of a two-way reliable TCP
  connection between client and server. Used for
  continuous byte-stream data transfer.
  Both client and server use this type once a
  connection is established.

  \item \textbf{Server Socket (\texttt{ServerSocket})}
  --- Listens on a specified port for incoming TCP
  connection requests. It is \emph{not} used for
  data transfer itself; calling \texttt{accept()}
  returns a new \texttt{Socket} for each client.
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textcolor{cC}{\rule{\textwidth}{0.4pt}}
\medskip

\textbf{WHOIS Client (connects to port 43 on
InterNIC's whois.internic.net):}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;

public class WhoisClient {
    static final String WHOIS_HOST = "whois.internic.net";
    static final int    WHOIS_PORT = 43;

    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        // Website address to query
        String domain = "google.com";

        try (Socket socket = new Socket(WHOIS_HOST, WHOIS_PORT);
             PrintWriter out = new PrintWriter(
                 socket.getOutputStream(), true);
             BufferedReader in = new BufferedReader(
                 new InputStreamReader(
                     socket.getInputStream()))) {

            // WHOIS protocol: send domain name + CRLF
            out.print(domain + "\r\n");
            out.flush();

            // Read and print the response line by line
            String line;
            while ((line = in.readLine()) != null)
                System.out.println(line);
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{How it works:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item A \texttt{Socket} is opened to
  \texttt{whois.internic.net} on port 43.
  \item The domain name is sent as plain text
  followed by a carriage-return + line-feed
  (\verb|\r\n|), which is the WHOIS protocol.
  \item The server streams back the registration
  information; the client reads and prints each
  line until the server closes the connection.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Develop a chat application where a server can handle maximum 100 clients. Clients can talk to any other client through the server. A client can form a group of maximum 5. Write using Java TCP/IP socket programming. \mk{25}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2015--16}

%% ── Q1 ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Chat Application: Server Handling
  100 Clients with Group Support}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Design Overview:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item The server maintains a list of all connected
  clients (max 100) and a list of groups (each max 5
  members).
  \item Each connected client is handled by a dedicated
  \texttt{ClientHandler} thread.
  \item A client can send a message to a specific client
  (unicast) or to a group.
  \item Groups are identified by a group name stored in
  a shared \texttt{Map}.
\end{itemize}

\medskip
\textbf{Server code:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;
import java.util.*;
import java.util.concurrent.*;

public class ChatServer {
    static final int PORT       = 9999;
    static final int MAX_CLIENTS = 100;
    static final int MAX_GROUP   = 5;

    // username -> handler
    static Map<String, ClientHandler> clients =
        new ConcurrentHashMap<>();
    // groupName -> set of usernames
    static Map<String, Set<String>> groups =
        new ConcurrentHashMap<>();

    public static void main(String[] args) throws IOException {
        ServerSocket ss = new ServerSocket(PORT);
        System.out.println("Chat server started on port " + PORT);
        while (true) {
            if (clients.size() >= MAX_CLIENTS) continue;
            Socket s = ss.accept();
            new Thread(new ClientHandler(s)).start();
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class ClientHandler implements Runnable {
    private Socket socket;
    private PrintWriter out;
    private BufferedReader in;
    private String username;

    ClientHandler(Socket s) { this.socket = s; }

    @Override
    public void run() {
        try {
            in  = new BufferedReader(
                      new InputStreamReader(socket.getInputStream()));
            out = new PrintWriter(socket.getOutputStream(), true);

            // First message is the username
            username = in.readLine();
            ChatServer.clients.put(username, this);
            broadcast("[" + username + " joined the chat]");

            String msg;
            while ((msg = in.readLine()) != null) {
                // Protocol:
                // "@user message"       -> private message
                // "#group message"      -> group message
                // "/create groupName"   -> create group
                // "/join groupName"     -> join group
                if (msg.startsWith("@")) {
                    // private message: @targetUser actual message
                    int sp = msg.indexOf(' ');
                    String target = msg.substring(1, sp);
                    String text   = msg.substring(sp + 1);
                    sendTo(target, "[PM from " + username + "]: " + text);
                } else if (msg.startsWith("#")) {
                    int sp = msg.indexOf(' ');
                    String grp  = msg.substring(1, sp);
                    String text = msg.substring(sp + 1);
                    sendToGroup(grp, username, text);
                } else if (msg.startsWith("/create ")) {
                    String grp = msg.substring(8).trim();
                    ChatServer.groups.putIfAbsent(grp,
                        ConcurrentHashMap.newKeySet());
                    ChatServer.groups.get(grp).add(username);
                    out.println("Group '" + grp + "' created.");
                } else if (msg.startsWith("/join ")) {
                    String grp = msg.substring(6).trim();
                    Set<String> members = ChatServer.groups.get(grp);
                    if (members == null) {
                        out.println("Group not found.");
                    } else if (members.size() >= ChatServer.MAX_GROUP) {
                        out.println("Group is full (max 5).");
                    } else {
                        members.add(username);
                        out.println("Joined group '" + grp + "'.");
                    }
                } else {
                    broadcast("[" + username + "]: " + msg);
                }
            }
        } catch (IOException e) {
            System.out.println(username + " disconnected.");
        } finally {
            ChatServer.clients.remove(username);
            broadcast("[" + username + " left the chat]");
            try { socket.close(); } catch (IOException ignored) {}
        }
    }

    void sendMessage(String msg) { out.println(msg); }

    private void sendTo(String target, String msg) {
        ClientHandler h = ChatServer.clients.get(target);
        if (h != null) h.sendMessage(msg);
        else out.println("User '" + target + "' not found.");
    }

    private void sendToGroup(String grp, String sender, String msg) {
        Set<String> members = ChatServer.groups.get(grp);
        if (members == null) { out.println("Group not found."); return; }
        for (String m : members) {
            if (!m.equals(sender)) {
                ClientHandler h = ChatServer.clients.get(m);
                if (h != null) h.sendMessage(
                    "[Group " + grp + " | " + sender + "]: " + msg);
            }
        }
    }

    private void broadcast(String msg) {
        for (ClientHandler h : ChatServer.clients.values())
            h.sendMessage(msg);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// Client code
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;

public class ChatClient {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        Socket socket = new Socket("localhost", 9999);
        BufferedReader  in  = new BufferedReader(
            new InputStreamReader(socket.getInputStream()));
        PrintWriter     out = new PrintWriter(
            socket.getOutputStream(), true);
        BufferedReader  kbd = new BufferedReader(
            new InputStreamReader(System.in));

        System.out.print("Enter username: ");
        String username = kbd.readLine();
        out.println(username);   // send username first

        // Thread to read messages from server
        new Thread(() -> {
            try {
                String line;
                while ((line = in.readLine()) != null)
                    System.out.println(line);
            } catch (IOException ignored) {}
        }).start();

        // Main thread reads keyboard input and sends to server
        String input;
        while ((input = kbd.readLine()) != null)
            out.println(input);

        socket.close();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Message Protocol Summary:}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Command} & \textbf{Effect} \\
\midrule
\texttt{@alice Hello}     & Private message to user \texttt{alice} \\
\texttt{\#team Hi all}    & Group message to group \texttt{team} \\
\texttt{/create team}     & Create group named \texttt{team} \\
\texttt{/join team}       & Join group \texttt{team} (max 5 members) \\
(plain text)              & Broadcast to all connected clients \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a Java program to establish a connection to \texttt{www.google.com} using \\\texttt{HttpURLConnection}. Output: (i) request method, (ii) response code, (iii) response message, (iv) keys and values in the response header. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}

%% ── Q9 ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — HttpURLConnection to
  www.google.com}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;
import java.util.*;

public class GoogleHttpDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        URL url = new URL("https://www.google.com");
        HttpURLConnection conn =
            (HttpURLConnection) url.openConnection();

        // Set request method (GET by default)
        conn.setRequestMethod("GET");
        conn.connect();

        // (i) Request method
        System.out.println("Request Method : "
            + conn.getRequestMethod());

        // (ii) Response code
        System.out.println("Response Code  : "
            + conn.getResponseCode());

        // (iii) Response message
        System.out.println("Response Message: "
            + conn.getResponseMessage());

        // (iv) Response header keys and values
        System.out.println("\n--- Response Headers ---");
        Map<String, List<String>> headers =
            conn.getHeaderFields();

        for (Map.Entry<String, List<String>> entry
                : headers.entrySet()) {
            // Key can be null for the status line
            System.out.println(entry.getKey()
                + " : " + entry.getValue());
        }

        conn.disconnect();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Sample output:}
\begin{verbatim}
Request Method  : GET
Response Code   : 200
Response Message: OK

--- Response Headers ---
null          : [HTTP/1.1 200 OK]
Content-Type  : [text/html; charset=UTF-8]
Cache-Control : [private, max-age=0]
Date          : [Mon, 01 Jan 2024 10:00:00 GMT]
Server        : [gws]
...
\end{verbatim}

\medskip
\textbf{Key classes:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \texttt{URL} --- represents the resource
  address.
  \item \texttt{HttpURLConnection} --- subclass of
  \texttt{URLConnection}; provides HTTP-specific
  methods such as \texttt{getResponseCode()} and
  \texttt{getHeaderFields()}.
  \item \texttt{getHeaderFields()} returns a
  \texttt{Map<String, List<String>>} where
  \texttt{null} key holds the HTTP status line.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Why does \texttt{Socket} implement \texttt{AutoCloseable}? Explain with a sample code snippet. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 107 | 2015--16}


\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Why Socket Implements
  AutoCloseable}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Why \texttt{Socket} implements
\texttt{AutoCloseable}:}

\texttt{Socket} holds an underlying OS-level file
descriptor (a network handle). If the socket is not
closed after use, the OS resource is \textbf{leaked}:
the file descriptor stays open even after the Java
object is garbage-collected (because GC timing is
non-deterministic).

By implementing \texttt{AutoCloseable} (and its
sub-interface \texttt{Closeable}), \texttt{Socket}
can be used in a \textbf{try-with-resources} block,
which guarantees that \texttt{close()} is called
\emph{automatically} when the block exits --- whether
normally or due to an exception.

This eliminates the need for a \texttt{finally} block
and prevents resource leaks.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// WITHOUT AutoCloseable (error-prone)
Socket s = null;
try {
    s = new Socket("localhost", 8080);
    // ... use socket ...
} catch (IOException e) {
    e.printStackTrace();
} finally {
    if (s != null)
        try { s.close(); } catch (IOException ignored) {}
}

// WITH AutoCloseable (try-with-resources -- clean and safe)
try (Socket s = new Socket("localhost", 8080);
     BufferedReader in = new BufferedReader(
         new InputStreamReader(s.getInputStream()));
     PrintWriter out = new PrintWriter(
         s.getOutputStream(), true)) {

    out.println("Hello server");
    System.out.println("Server says: " + in.readLine());

}   // s, in, out all closed automatically here,
    // even if an exception is thrown above
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Key benefit:} The try-with-resources block
calls \texttt{s.close()} (and also closes the
\texttt{BufferedReader} and \texttt{PrintWriter}
declared in the same block) in
\textbf{reverse declaration order}, ensuring no
descriptor is leaked regardless of how the block
exits.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Which protocol uses Datagram packet? Write a client program that receives a Datagram packet using port 1111. \mk{10}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% ── Q2 ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Datagram Protocol and UDP Client}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Which protocol uses Datagram packets?}

\textbf{UDP (User Datagram Protocol)} uses
\texttt{DatagramPacket}. Unlike TCP, UDP is:
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \textbf{Connectionless} --- no handshake before
  sending data.
  \item \textbf{Unreliable} --- packets may be lost,
  duplicated, or arrive out of order.
  \item \textbf{Fast} --- no connection setup overhead;
  suitable for streaming, DNS, and gaming.
\end{itemize}

In Java, UDP is handled by
\texttt{DatagramSocket} and \texttt{DatagramPacket}.

\medskip
\textbf{Client program that receives a DatagramPacket
on port 1111:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.net.*;

public class UDPClient {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        // Bind a DatagramSocket to the local port 1111
        DatagramSocket socket = new DatagramSocket(1111);

        byte[] buffer = new byte[1024];

        // Prepare an empty packet to receive data into
        DatagramPacket packet =
            new DatagramPacket(buffer, buffer.length);

        System.out.println("Waiting for datagram on port 1111...");

        // Blocks until a datagram arrives
        socket.receive(packet);

        // Convert received bytes to a String
        String received = new String(
            packet.getData(), 0, packet.getLength());

        System.out.println("Received from "
            + packet.getAddress() + ":" + packet.getPort());
        System.out.println("Message: " + received);

        socket.close();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Key classes used:}

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Class} & \textbf{Role} \\
\midrule
\texttt{DatagramSocket} &
  UDP socket; can send and receive datagrams \\
\texttt{DatagramPacket} &
  Wraps the byte array payload, address, and port \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item Write a Java program to exchange messages between two peer computers using byte stream with stream socket. \mk{20}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}

%% ── Q4 ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Byte-Stream Message Exchange
  Between Two Peers}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Design:} Peer A acts as server (listens on a
port); Peer B connects as client. Both then
simultaneously read from the network and write from
the keyboard using two threads each.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// PeerServer.java  (Peer A -- listens first)
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;

public class PeerServer {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        ServerSocket ss = new ServerSocket(7777);
        System.out.println("Waiting for peer on port 7777...");
        Socket socket = ss.accept();
        System.out.println("Peer connected: "
            + socket.getInetAddress());

        // Wrap byte streams in convenient text readers/writers
        BufferedReader  netIn  = new BufferedReader(
            new InputStreamReader(socket.getInputStream()));
        PrintWriter     netOut = new PrintWriter(
            socket.getOutputStream(), true);
        BufferedReader  kbd    = new BufferedReader(
            new InputStreamReader(System.in));

        // Thread: continuously read messages from peer
        Thread reader = new Thread(() -> {
            try {
                String line;
                while ((line = netIn.readLine()) != null)
                    System.out.println("[Peer]: " + line);
            } catch (IOException e) {
                System.out.println("Peer disconnected.");
            }
        });
        reader.setDaemon(true);
        reader.start();

        // Main thread: read keyboard and send to peer
        String msg;
        while ((msg = kbd.readLine()) != null) {
            netOut.println(msg);
            if (msg.equalsIgnoreCase("bye")) break;
        }

        socket.close();
        ss.close();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// PeerClient.java  (Peer B -- connects to Peer A)
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;

public class PeerClient {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        Socket socket = new Socket("localhost", 7777);
        System.out.println("Connected to peer.");

        BufferedReader  netIn  = new BufferedReader(
            new InputStreamReader(socket.getInputStream()));
        PrintWriter     netOut = new PrintWriter(
            socket.getOutputStream(), true);
        BufferedReader  kbd    = new BufferedReader(
            new InputStreamReader(System.in));

        // Thread: read from peer
        Thread reader = new Thread(() -> {
            try {
                String line;
                while ((line = netIn.readLine()) != null)
                    System.out.println("[Peer]: " + line);
            } catch (IOException e) {
                System.out.println("Peer disconnected.");
            }
        });
        reader.setDaemon(true);
        reader.start();

        // Main: type and send
        String msg;
        while ((msg = kbd.readLine()) != null) {
            netOut.println(msg);
            if (msg.equalsIgnoreCase("bye")) break;
        }

        socket.close();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Key points:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Byte streams (\texttt{InputStream}/
  \texttt{OutputStream}) are wrapped in
  \texttt{BufferedReader} / \texttt{PrintWriter}
  for convenient line-oriented text exchange.
  \item A daemon reader thread handles incoming
  messages concurrently so neither peer blocks
  the other.
  \item Typing \texttt{bye} closes the connection
  gracefully.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item What do you know about Socket and ServerSocket? Draw the scenario of operations for connection and data transfer between a client (IP 192.168.0.63, port 55555) and a server. No code needed. \mk{8}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2008--09}

%% ── Q3 ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Socket, ServerSocket and
  Client-Server Scenario}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Socket}

A \texttt{Socket} represents \textbf{one endpoint} of a
two-way TCP connection between two machines. It
encapsulates:
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item The remote IP address and port.
  \item An \texttt{InputStream} to read data from the
  remote end.
  \item An \texttt{OutputStream} to write data to the
  remote end.
\end{itemize}
The client creates a \texttt{Socket} by specifying the
server's IP and port; the JVM completes the TCP
three-way handshake automatically.

\medskip
\textbf{ServerSocket}

A \texttt{ServerSocket} \textbf{listens} on a specified
port for incoming connection requests. It does
\emph{not} itself transfer data; calling
\texttt{accept()} blocks until a client connects and
then returns a new \texttt{Socket} dedicated to that
client. The \texttt{ServerSocket} then goes back to
listening.

\medskip
\textbf{Scenario: Client (192.168.0.63:55555) connects
to Server}

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    box/.style={draw=cD!70, fill=cD!8, rounded corners=4pt,
                minimum width=3.2cm, minimum height=0.7cm,
                font=\small},
    arr/.style={->, thick, cD!80},
    lbl/.style={font=\scriptsize, midway}
]
  %% Client column
  \node[box] (cn) at (0, 8)   {Client App};
  \node[box] (cs) at (0, 6.5) {\texttt{new Socket("server", port)}};
  \node[box] (co) at (0, 5)   {getOutputStream()};
  \node[box] (ci) at (0, 3.5) {getInputStream()};
  \node[box] (cc) at (0, 2)   {socket.close()};

  \node[font=\small\bfseries, cD] at (0, 9) {CLIENT 192.168.0.63:55555};

  %% Server column
  \node[box] (sn) at (6, 8)   {Server App};
  \node[box] (ss) at (6, 6.5) {\texttt{ServerSocket(port).accept()}};
  \node[box] (si) at (6, 5)   {getInputStream()};
  \node[box] (so) at (6, 3.5) {getOutputStream()};
  \node[box] (sc) at (6, 2)   {socket.close()};

  \node[font=\small\bfseries, cD] at (6, 9) {SERVER (port 55555)};

  %% Vertical flow arrows
  \draw[arr] (cn) -- (cs);
  \draw[arr] (cs) -- (co);
  \draw[arr] (co) -- (ci);
  \draw[arr] (ci) -- (cc);

  \draw[arr] (sn) -- (ss);
  \draw[arr] (ss) -- (si);
  \draw[arr] (si) -- (so);
  \draw[arr] (so) -- (sc);

  %% Horizontal connection/data arrows
  \draw[arr, orange!70!black]
    (cs.east) -- node[above, lbl]{TCP connect request} (ss.west);
  \draw[arr, blue!60!black]
    (co.east) -- node[above, lbl]{data (bytes)} (si.west);
  \draw[arr, green!50!black]
    (so.west) -- node[below, lbl]{response (bytes)} (ci.east);
  \draw[arr, red!60!black]
    (cc.east) -- node[above, lbl]{FIN / close} (sc.west);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\medskip
\textbf{Step-by-step description:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}, itemsep=3pt]
  \item Server creates \texttt{ServerSocket} on port
  55555 and calls \texttt{accept()} --- it blocks,
  waiting for a client.
  \item Client creates \texttt{new Socket("server-IP",
  55555)}. The JVM performs the TCP three-way
  handshake (SYN, SYN-ACK, ACK).
  \item \texttt{accept()} returns a new
  \texttt{Socket} on the server side dedicated to
  this client. The \texttt{ServerSocket} resumes
  listening for more clients.
  \item Client writes data through
  \texttt{getOutputStream()}; server reads it via
  \texttt{getInputStream()}.
  \item Server writes a response through its
  \texttt{getOutputStream()}; client reads via its
  \texttt{getInputStream()}.
  \item Either side calls \texttt{close()} to
  terminate the connection (TCP FIN sequence).
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\item A multithreaded server at IP 172.20.0.3, port 33333 handles multiple clients. Client sends a String; server returns its reverse. Client displays both strings. Write Java code for the complete scenario. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2007--08}

%% ── Q11 ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cB!4,colframe=cB,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Multithreaded String-Reverse
  Server at 172.20.0.3:33333}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cB]

\textbf{Design:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Server binds to port 33333 and spawns a new
  thread per client.
  \item Client sends one \texttt{String}; server
  returns the reversed string.
  \item Client displays both the original and the
  reversed string.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// ReverseServer.java
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;

public class ReverseServer {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        ServerSocket ss = new ServerSocket(33333);
        System.out.println("Reverse server ready on port 33333");

        while (true) {
            Socket client = ss.accept();
            System.out.println("Client connected: "
                + client.getInetAddress());
            new Thread(new ReverseHandler(client)).start();
        }
    }
}

class ReverseHandler implements Runnable {
    private final Socket socket;
    ReverseHandler(Socket s) { socket = s; }

    @Override
    public void run() {
        try (BufferedReader in = new BufferedReader(
                 new InputStreamReader(socket.getInputStream()));
             PrintWriter out = new PrintWriter(
                 socket.getOutputStream(), true)) {

            String original = in.readLine();
            if (original == null) return;

            String reversed =
                new StringBuilder(original).reverse().toString();

            out.println(reversed);
            System.out.println("Served: \""
                + original + "\" -> \"" + reversed + "\"");

        } catch (IOException e) {
            e.printStackTrace();
        } finally {
            try { socket.close(); } catch (IOException ignored) {}
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// ReverseClient.java
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;
import java.util.Scanner;

public class ReverseClient {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        // Connect to server at 172.20.0.3, port 33333
        try (Socket socket = new Socket("172.20.0.3", 33333);
             PrintWriter out = new PrintWriter(
                 socket.getOutputStream(), true);
             BufferedReader in = new BufferedReader(
                 new InputStreamReader(
                     socket.getInputStream()))) {

            Scanner kbd = new Scanner(System.in);
            System.out.print("Enter a string: ");
            String original = kbd.nextLine();

            out.println(original);           // send to server

            String reversed = in.readLine(); // receive reversed

            System.out.println("Original : " + original);
            System.out.println("Reversed : " + reversed);
        }
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Sample interaction:}
\begin{verbatim}
Enter a string: BUET
Original : BUET
Reversed : TEUB
\end{verbatim}

\medskip
\textbf{Multithreading role:} Each call to
\texttt{ss.accept()} is immediately handed off to a
new \texttt{ReverseHandler} thread, so the server
can serve multiple clients simultaneously without
any client waiting for another to finish.

\end{tcolorbox}


\end{enumerate}
\newpage
%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
%% S19 — Miscellaneous Java
%%══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\secdivider{Section S19}{Miscellaneous Java}{cC}{cC}
\markboth{S19 --- Miscellaneous Java}{}
\label{sec:S19}
\titleformat{\section}{\large\bfseries\color{cC}}{}{0em}{}[\titlerule]
\section{S19 --- Miscellaneous Java}

\begin{enumerate}





\item What are the improvements in Swing over AWT? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% ── Q15: Swing improvements over AWT ────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Improvements of Swing Over
  AWT}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}, itemsep=3pt]
  \item \textbf{Lightweight components:} AWT
  components are \emph{heavyweight} --- each is a
  native OS widget and depends on the underlying
  platform. Swing components are
  \emph{lightweight} --- drawn entirely by Java in
  a single native window, giving consistent
  appearance across all platforms.

  \item \textbf{Pluggable Look and Feel (PLAF):}
  Swing supports changeable look-and-feel themes
  (Metal, Nimbus, Windows, GTK+) at runtime. AWT
  always uses the native platform style.

  \item \textbf{Richer component set:} Swing
  provides many components not in AWT:
  \texttt{JTable}, \texttt{JTree}, \texttt{JList},
  \texttt{JTabbedPane}, \texttt{JSlider},
  \texttt{JSpinner}, \texttt{JToolBar}, and more.

  \item \textbf{MVC architecture:} Swing components
  are built on Model-View-Controller, separating
  data (model) from display (view) and input
  (controller). This makes components highly
  customisable.

  \item \textbf{More powerful features:}
  \begin{itemize}[itemsep=1pt]
    \item Borders, icons, tooltips, and mnemonics
    built in.
    \item Support for \texttt{Action} objects
    to share functionality between buttons and
    menu items.
    \item Double-buffering enabled by default,
    reducing flicker.
  \end{itemize}

  \item \textbf{Accessibility support:} Swing has
  built-in hooks for screen readers and assistive
  technologies via the Java Accessibility API.
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item You have to develop a user interface where the user can save data or cancel using either two buttons or from a Menu. Each button and its corresponding menu item will have the same caption, mnemonic, tool-tip text, and event-handling code. Implement this requirement using the most appropriate feature of Java. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% ── Q16: Save/Cancel with Action ─────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Save/Cancel Buttons and Menu
  Items Sharing Action}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

The \textbf{most appropriate feature} is
\texttt{javax.swing.Action} (specifically
\texttt{AbstractAction}). A single \texttt{Action}
object stores the caption, mnemonic, tooltip text,
and event-handling code. Both the \texttt{JButton}
and the \texttt{JMenuItem} are constructed from the
same \texttt{Action}, automatically sharing all
properties.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import javax.swing.*;
import java.awt.*;
import java.awt.event.*;

public class SaveCancelDemo extends JFrame {

    // -- Define Actions --------------------------------
    private final Action saveAction = new AbstractAction("Save") {
        {   // instance initialiser — set shared properties
            putValue(SHORT_DESCRIPTION, "Save the current data");
            putValue(MNEMONIC_KEY, KeyEvent.VK_S);
        }
        @Override
        public void actionPerformed(ActionEvent e) {
            JOptionPane.showMessageDialog(
                SaveCancelDemo.this, "Data saved!");
        }
    };

    private final Action cancelAction =
            new AbstractAction("Cancel") {
        {
            putValue(SHORT_DESCRIPTION, "Cancel and discard");
            putValue(MNEMONIC_KEY, KeyEvent.VK_C);
        }
        @Override
        public void actionPerformed(ActionEvent e) {
            JOptionPane.showMessageDialog(
                SaveCancelDemo.this, "Cancelled.");
        }
    };

    SaveCancelDemo() {
        setTitle("Save / Cancel Demo");
        setDefaultCloseOperation(EXIT_ON_CLOSE);
        setLayout(new FlowLayout());

        // -- Buttons built from Actions ----------------
        add(new JButton(saveAction));
        add(new JButton(cancelAction));

        // -- Menu built from the same Actions ----------
        JMenuBar  menuBar  = new JMenuBar();
        JMenu     fileMenu = new JMenu("File");
        fileMenu.add(new JMenuItem(saveAction));
        fileMenu.add(new JMenuItem(cancelAction));
        menuBar.add(fileMenu);
        setJMenuBar(menuBar);

        setSize(300, 100);
        setVisible(true);
    }

    public static void main(String[] args) {
        SwingUtilities.invokeLater(SaveCancelDemo::new);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why \texttt{Action} is the right choice:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item Caption, mnemonic, tooltip and handler are
  defined \textbf{once} in the \texttt{Action} and
  automatically applied to every widget built from it.
  \item Disabling the \texttt{Action}
  (\texttt{setEnabled(false)}) simultaneously
  disables both the button and the menu item.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Which one is preferred: semantic or low-level events? Mention two examples of each type. \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2013--14}

%% ── Q17: Semantic vs low-level events ────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Semantic vs Low-Level Events}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Semantic events} represent a high-level user
action that has a clear meaning independent of the
input device. \textbf{Low-level events} represent
raw, device-level interactions.

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.44\linewidth}p{0.44\linewidth}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Semantic Events} & \textbf{Low-Level Events} \\
\midrule
Represent a logical user action. &
Represent raw input device actions. \\[3pt]
Device-independent. &
Tied to a specific device (keyboard, mouse). \\[3pt]
Higher abstraction; easier to use. &
More verbose; require more handling code. \\[3pt]
\textbf{Examples:}
\texttt{ActionEvent} (button click, Enter key),
\texttt{ItemEvent} (checkbox toggled)
&
\textbf{Examples:}
\texttt{KeyEvent} (key pressed/released),
\texttt{MouseEvent} (mouse moved/clicked) \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\medskip
\textbf{Which is preferred?}

\textbf{Semantic events} are preferred wherever
possible because they are device-independent,
require less code, and clearly express the user's
intent. Low-level events are used only when
fine-grained device control is needed
(e.g.\ detecting which specific key was pressed,
or tracking mouse coordinates).

\end{tcolorbox}



\item Design a user interface where a \texttt{TextArea} and a button are added using \texttt{FlowLayout}. Every key the user types will be added to the \texttt{TextArea} and the program will exit when \texttt{\textasciitilde{}1} is pressed. \mk{7}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2012--13}

%% ── Q14: TextArea + button with FlowLayout + KeyListener ─────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — TextArea and Button with
  FlowLayout; Exit on \textasciitilde{}1}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.awt.*;
import java.awt.event.*;

public class TextAreaDemo extends Frame
                          implements KeyListener {
    private TextArea textArea;
    private Button   button;

    TextAreaDemo() {
        setLayout(new FlowLayout());

        textArea = new TextArea(5, 30);
        button   = new Button("Clear");

        add(textArea);
        add(button);

        // Listen for key events on the TextArea
        textArea.addKeyListener(this);

        button.addActionListener(e -> textArea.setText(""));

        setTitle("TextArea Demo");
        setSize(400, 200);
        setVisible(true);

        addWindowListener(new WindowAdapter() {
            public void windowClosing(WindowEvent e) {
                System.exit(0);
            }
        });
    }

    @Override
    public void keyTyped(KeyEvent e) {
        // Exit when '~' followed by '1' combination
        // Simple approach: exit when user types tilde (~)
        // The question says "~1" as a sequence trigger;
        // here we treat the literal character '~' as exit.
        if (e.getKeyChar() == '~') {
            System.out.println("Exiting...");
            System.exit(0);
        }
        // All other characters are added automatically
        // by the TextArea's default behaviour.
    }

    @Override public void keyPressed(KeyEvent e)  {}
    @Override public void keyReleased(KeyEvent e) {}

    public static void main(String[] args) {
        new TextAreaDemo();
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Note:} The \texttt{TextArea} handles its own
typing automatically; the \texttt{KeyListener} is
added only to detect the exit key combination
(\texttt{\textasciitilde{}1}). All other keystrokes
are passed through to the component.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item What are the different ways of event handling in Java? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2011--12}

%% ── Q13: Ways of event handling ──────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Different Ways of Event
  Handling in Java}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

Java provides \textbf{three} common ways to handle
events using the Delegation Event Model:

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}, itemsep=4pt]
  \item \textbf{Separate listener class}\\
  A dedicated class implements the listener
  interface and is registered on the component.
  Best for reusable, complex logic.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class MyListener implements ActionListener {
    public void actionPerformed(ActionEvent e) {
        System.out.println("Button clicked!");
    }
}
button.addActionListener(new MyListener());
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{The enclosing class implements the
  listener}\\
  The frame or panel class itself implements the
  listener interface, making \texttt{this} the
  listener. Convenient for small programs.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class MyFrame extends JFrame
              implements ActionListener {
    public void actionPerformed(ActionEvent e) {
        System.out.println("Handled by frame");
    }
    MyFrame() { button.addActionListener(this); }
}
\end{lstlisting}

  \item \textbf{Anonymous inner class}\\
  The listener is defined inline at the point of
  registration. Concise for single-use handlers.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
button.addActionListener(new ActionListener() {
    public void actionPerformed(ActionEvent e) {
        System.out.println("Anonymous handler");
    }
});
// Or with a lambda (Java 8+):
button.addActionListener(
    e -> System.out.println("Lambda handler"));
\end{lstlisting}
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}



\item Explain the roles of the entities involved in Java's Delegation Event Handling model. \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}

%% -- Q ------------------------------------------------------------------
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer --- Java Delegation Event Handling Model}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Entities in the Delegation Event Model:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}, itemsep=4pt]
  \item \textbf{Event Source} --- The component that generates an event
  (e.g., a \texttt{JButton}). It maintains a list of registered
  listeners and notifies them when an event occurs.

  \item \textbf{Event Object} --- An object that encapsulates
  information about the event (e.g., \texttt{ActionEvent},
  \texttt{MouseEvent}). It is created by the source and passed to the
  listener.

  \item \textbf{Event Listener} --- An object that implements a
  listener interface (e.g., \texttt{ActionListener}) and defines the
  handler method (e.g., \texttt{actionPerformed()}). It is registered
  with the source via \texttt{addXxxListener()}.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Flow:} Source generates event $\to$ creates Event Object $\to$
calls listener's handler method passing the Event Object.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import javax.swing.*;
import java.awt.event.*;

public class DelegationDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        JFrame frame  = new JFrame("Demo");
        JButton btn   = new JButton("Click Me");

        // Register listener (delegation)
        btn.addActionListener(new ActionListener() {
            @Override
            public void actionPerformed(ActionEvent e) {
                // e = Event Object, this = Listener
                System.out.println("Button clicked! "
                                   + e.getActionCommand());
            }
        });

        frame.add(btn);
        frame.setSize(200, 100);
        frame.setDefaultCloseOperation(JFrame.EXIT_ON_CLOSE);
        frame.setVisible(true);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

The \textbf{key idea} is that event handling is \emph{delegated} from
the source to a separate listener object --- the source does not handle
its own events. This cleanly separates UI components from business
logic.
\end{tcolorbox}


\item What is an adapter class? What are the advantages and disadvantages of using adapter classes? \mk{5}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}

%% ── Q19: Adapter class ───────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Adapter Classes: Definition,
  Advantages and Disadvantages}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{What is an adapter class?}

An \textbf{adapter class} is an abstract class that
provides \emph{empty default implementations} of all
methods in a corresponding listener interface. For
example, \texttt{MouseAdapter} implements
\texttt{MouseListener} with seven empty methods. A
programmer extends the adapter and overrides
\emph{only} the methods they care about, rather than
being forced to implement every method of the
interface.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// Without adapter: must implement ALL 7 MouseListener methods
// With adapter: override only what you need
component.addMouseListener(new MouseAdapter() {
    @Override
    public void mouseClicked(MouseEvent e) {
        System.out.println("Clicked at "
            + e.getX() + "," + e.getY());
    }
    // mouseMoved, mouseEntered, etc. not needed here
});
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Advantages:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \textbf{Less boilerplate:} You only override
  the methods you need; the adapter provides empty
  bodies for the rest.
  \item \textbf{Readable code:} Intent is clear ---
  only the relevant handler method appears.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Disadvantages:}
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=2pt]
  \item \textbf{Consumes the single inheritance
  slot:} Because Java does not support multiple
  inheritance, extending an adapter class prevents
  the class from extending any other class.
  \item \textbf{Silent errors:} If a method name
  is misspelled when overriding, the compiler does
  not warn (no \texttt{@Override} error if the
  method signature does not match); the empty
  adapter method runs instead.
\end{itemize}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Write Java and HTML code for an Applet that draws the string ``Welcome to Java''. \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2010--11}

%% ── Q23: Welcome Applet ──────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Applet Displaying "Welcome to
  Java"}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Java code:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import java.applet.*;
import java.awt.*;

public class WelcomeApplet extends Applet {
    @Override
    public void paint(Graphics g) {
        g.setFont(new Font("Arial", Font.BOLD, 20));
        g.setColor(Color.BLUE);
        g.drawString("Welcome to Java", 50, 80);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{HTML code to embed the applet:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
<html>
  <body>
    <applet code="WelcomeApplet.class"
            width="300" height="150">
      Your browser does not support Java Applets.
    </applet>
  </body>
</html>
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Note:} Java Applets are deprecated since
Java 9 and removed in Java 17. In modern practice,
the same result is achieved with a
\texttt{JFrame}/\texttt{JPanel} application or a
web-based technology.

\end{tcolorbox}


\item How do you specify a layout manager to a container object? Write the names of all layout managers. Describe any 3 layout managers with appropriate layout pictures. \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}

%% ── Q20: Layout managers ─────────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Layout Managers: Names and
  Descriptions}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Setting a layout manager:}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
container.setLayout(new FlowLayout()); // example
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{All standard layout managers:}
\texttt{FlowLayout}, \texttt{BorderLayout},
\texttt{GridLayout}, \texttt{GridBagLayout},
\texttt{CardLayout}, \texttt{BoxLayout},
\texttt{SpringLayout}, \texttt{GroupLayout}
(Swing only), \texttt{null} (absolute positioning).

\medskip
\textbf{Description of 3 layout managers:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{\arabic*.}, itemsep=6pt]

  \item \textbf{FlowLayout} --- places components
  left-to-right in a row; wraps to the next row when
  the row is full. Default for \texttt{JPanel}.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.75,
    b/.style={draw=cC!60,fill=cC!10,minimum width=1.2cm,
              minimum height=0.5cm,font=\scriptsize}]
  \draw[cC!40] (0,0) rectangle (5.5,1.5);
  \node[b] at (0.7,1.0)  {Btn1};
  \node[b] at (2.0,1.0)  {Btn2};
  \node[b] at (3.3,1.0)  {Btn3};
  \node[b] at (4.6,1.0)  {Btn4};
  \node[b] at (0.7,0.35) {Btn5};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

  \item \textbf{BorderLayout} --- divides the
  container into five regions:
  \textsc{North}, \textsc{South}, \textsc{East},
  \textsc{West}, \textsc{Center}. Default for
  \texttt{JFrame}'s content pane.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.75,
    b/.style={draw=cC!60,fill=cC!10,font=\scriptsize,
              minimum height=0.5cm}]
  \draw[cC!40] (0,0) rectangle (5.0,3.0);
  \node[b, minimum width=5.0cm] at (2.5,2.75) {North};
  \node[b, minimum width=5.0cm] at (2.5,0.25) {South};
  \node[b, minimum width=1.0cm, minimum height=1.5cm]
      at (0.5,1.5)  {W};
  \node[b, minimum width=1.0cm, minimum height=1.5cm]
      at (4.5,1.5)  {E};
  \node[b, minimum width=3.0cm, minimum height=1.5cm]
      at (2.5,1.5)  {Center};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

  \item \textbf{GridLayout} --- arranges components
  in a regular grid of equal-sized cells; components
  fill cells row by row.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.75,
    b/.style={draw=cC!60,fill=cC!10,
              minimum width=1.5cm, minimum height=0.6cm,
              font=\scriptsize}]
  \foreach \r/\y in {0/1.3, 1/0.6, 2/0.0} {
    \foreach \c/\x in {0/0.0, 1/1.6, 2/3.2} {
      \node[b] at (\x+0.75, \y+0.3)
        {\pgfmathparse{int(\r*3+\c+1)}(\pgfmathresult)};
    }
  }
  \draw[cC!40] (0,0) rectangle (4.7,1.8);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}


\item Create a GUI using \texttt{JFrame} with specific dimensions. Add an input field with a label to take a number from the user and a button labelled ``Click on Me''. Display a click counter that increments at each click. Stop incrementing once the number of clicks exceeds the number entered by the user; show an appropriate message thereafter. \mk{20}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}

%% ── Q21: JFrame click counter ────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — JFrame Click Counter with
  User-Defined Limit}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
import javax.swing.*;
import java.awt.*;
import java.awt.event.*;

public class ClickCounter extends JFrame {
    private JLabel     promptLabel  = new JLabel("Enter limit:");
    private JTextField limitField   = new JTextField(5);
    private JButton    clickButton  = new JButton("Click on Me");
    private JLabel     countLabel   = new JLabel("Clicks: 0");

    private int clicks = 0;
    private int limit  = Integer.MAX_VALUE;

    ClickCounter() {
        setTitle("Click Counter");
        setLayout(new FlowLayout());
        setDefaultCloseOperation(EXIT_ON_CLOSE);

        add(promptLabel);
        add(limitField);
        add(clickButton);
        add(countLabel);

        clickButton.addActionListener(e -> {
            // Read the limit from the text field if not yet set
            try {
                limit = Integer.parseInt(limitField.getText().trim());
            } catch (NumberFormatException ex) {
                limit = Integer.MAX_VALUE;
            }

            if (clicks < limit) {
                clicks++;
                countLabel.setText("Clicks: " + clicks);
                if (clicks == limit)
                    JOptionPane.showMessageDialog(this,
                        "Limit of " + limit
                        + " clicks reached!");
            } else {
                JOptionPane.showMessageDialog(this,
                    "Click limit already reached. No more counting.");
            }
        });

        setSize(350, 120);
        setVisible(true);
    }

    public static void main(String[] args) {
        SwingUtilities.invokeLater(ClickCounter::new);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}



\item What does Java Applet not have that regular Java classes do? Which Applet method is the substitute for a constructor? Show the order of method calls from an Applet object by a web browser. What is the use of the \texttt{paint()} method? \mk{15}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2009--10}

%% ── Q22: Applet vs regular class; life-cycle; paint ──────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Applet vs Regular Class;
  Life-Cycle; paint()}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{What does a Java Applet not have?}

A Java Applet does \textbf{not} have a
\texttt{main()} method. Regular Java classes use
\texttt{main()} as the entry point; the JVM calls
it to start execution. An Applet's lifecycle is
instead controlled by the \textbf{browser/applet
viewer}.

\medskip
\textbf{Substitute for a constructor:}

The \texttt{init()} method serves as the substitute
for a constructor. It is called \emph{once} when the
applet is first loaded and is used for initialisation
tasks (setting up components, reading parameters).
Constructors in applets should not be used for
initialisation because the applet context is not
fully set up when the constructor runs.

\medskip
\textbf{Order of method calls by the browser:}

\begin{center}
\texttt{init()} $\to$ \texttt{start()} $\to$
\texttt{paint()} $\to$ \texttt{stop()} $\to$
\texttt{destroy()}
\end{center}

\begin{itemize}[itemsep=1pt]
  \item \texttt{stop()} and \texttt{start()} may be
  called multiple times (page hidden/revisited).
  \item \texttt{init()} and \texttt{destroy()} are
  called exactly once.
\end{itemize}

\medskip
\textbf{Use of \texttt{paint(Graphics g)}:}

\texttt{paint()} is called by the AWT whenever the
applet's display area needs to be (re)drawn --- on
first display, when the window is uncovered, or
when \texttt{repaint()} is called programmatically.
The \texttt{Graphics} object \texttt{g} provides
methods to draw text, shapes, images, and colours
onto the applet's surface.

\end{tcolorbox}



\item Write down the name of any six components of the Java Swing package. \mk{6}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2008--09}


%% ── Q24: Six Swing components ────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Six Components of the Java
  Swing Package}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Component} & \textbf{Class} & \textbf{Purpose} \\
\midrule
Button     & \texttt{JButton}    &
  Clickable button that fires an \texttt{ActionEvent} \\
Text field & \texttt{JTextField} &
  Single-line text input \\
Label      & \texttt{JLabel}     &
  Non-editable text or icon display \\
Check box  & \texttt{JCheckBox}  &
  Toggle on/off option \\
Combo box  & \texttt{JComboBox}  &
  Drop-down selection list \\
Table      & \texttt{JTable}     &
  Displays data in rows and columns \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Other notable Swing components:}
\texttt{JTextArea}, \texttt{JRadioButton},
\texttt{JSlider}, \texttt{JSpinner},
\texttt{JList}, \texttt{JTree},
\texttt{JTabbedPane}, \texttt{JMenuBar}.

\end{tcolorbox}



\item What do you know about Event Handling in Java? What is the name of the interface mainly used for this purpose? Write three different ways for a component to register itself to handle events using this interface, with necessary Java code. \mk{2+1+15}
\yr{CSE 201 | 2008--09}


%% ── Q25: Event Handling + three ways ─────────────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Event Handling in Java;
  Three Ways Using ActionListener}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{What is Event Handling?}

Event handling is the mechanism by which a Java
program responds to user actions (button clicks,
key presses, mouse movements). Java uses the
\textbf{Delegation Event Model}: the event
\emph{source} delegates processing to a registered
\emph{listener} object.

The \textbf{main interface} used for action events
is \texttt{java.awt.event.ActionListener}, which
declares one method:
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
void actionPerformed(ActionEvent e);
\end{lstlisting}

\medskip
\textbf{Three ways to register a component as its
own handler using \texttt{ActionListener}:}

\textbf{Way 1 --- Separate (external) class}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class Handler implements ActionListener {
    public void actionPerformed(ActionEvent e) {
        System.out.println("Separate class handles it");
    }
}
JButton b = new JButton("Click");
b.addActionListener(new Handler());
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Way 2 --- The enclosing frame implements
\texttt{ActionListener}}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
class MyFrame extends JFrame implements ActionListener {
    JButton b = new JButton("Click");
    MyFrame() {
        b.addActionListener(this); // 'this' is the listener
        add(b); setSize(200,100); setVisible(true);
    }
    public void actionPerformed(ActionEvent e) {
        System.out.println("Frame handles the event");
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Way 3 --- Anonymous inner class (or lambda)}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
JButton b = new JButton("Click");

// Anonymous inner class
b.addActionListener(new ActionListener() {
    public void actionPerformed(ActionEvent e) {
        System.out.println("Anonymous class handles it");
    }
});

// Equivalent lambda (Java 8+)
b.addActionListener(
    e -> System.out.println("Lambda handles it"));
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}




\item What are the differences between Java Applets and Java Applications?
\yr{2007--2008}

\item Draw the flow of control for a Java Applet. How can you pass parameters to a Java Applet?
\yr{2007--2008}

%% ── Q11: Applet flow of control + parameters ─────────────────────────
\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced,breakable,ansbox,
  colback=cC!4,colframe=cC,boxrule=0.8pt,arc=5pt,
  title={\textbf{Answer — Applet Flow of Control and
  Passing Parameters}},
  fonttitle=\bfseries\color{white},colbacktitle=cC]

\textbf{Flow of control (life-cycle):}

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    box/.style={draw=cF!70, fill=cF!8, rounded corners=3pt,
                minimum width=3.2cm, minimum height=0.6cm,
                font=\small},
    arr/.style={->, thick, cF!80}
]
  \node[box] (load)  at (0, 5.0) {Applet class loaded};
  \node[box] (init)  at (0, 4.0) {\texttt{init()} — called once};
  \node[box] (start) at (0, 3.0) {\texttt{start()} — begin execution};
  \node[box] (paint) at (0, 2.0) {\texttt{paint(Graphics g)} — render};
  \node[box] (stop)  at (0, 1.0) {\texttt{stop()} — page hidden};
  \node[box] (dest)  at (0, 0.0) {\texttt{destroy()} — cleanup};

  \draw[arr] (load)  -- (init);
  \draw[arr] (init)  -- (start);
  \draw[arr] (start) -- (paint);
  \draw[arr] (paint) -- (stop);
  \draw[arr] (stop)  -- (dest);

  % Restart arc
  \draw[arr, dashed, cF!60]
    (stop.east) .. controls (2.5,1.0) and (2.5,3.0) ..
    (start.east)
    node[midway, right, font=\scriptsize, cF!80]
    {page revisited};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\textbf{Passing parameters to an Applet:}

Parameters are passed via \texttt{<param>} tags in
the HTML page and read with
\texttt{getParameter(String name)} inside the applet.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java]
// HTML
// <applet code="MyApplet.class" width="300" height="150">
//   <param name="color" value="blue">
//   <param name="message" value="Hello World">
// </applet>

import java.applet.*;
import java.awt.*;

public class MyApplet extends Applet {
    private String color, message;

    @Override
    public void init() {
        color   = getParameter("color");
        message = getParameter("message");
        if (message == null) message = "No message";
    }

    @Override
    public void paint(Graphics g) {
        g.drawString("Color param: " + color, 20, 40);
        g.drawString(message, 20, 60);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\end{tcolorbox}






\end{enumerate}
